The Red Page
The Place Poem
There are 46 places in Australia, anc
none in Maonland, which at least one patri
otic bard has thought worth while celebrat
ing m verse. Each of the following place
boasts two poets apiece: Castlemaine
agga, Broken Hill and Mount Gambier
Either these are remarkably poetical places
or they are inhabited chiefly by noets.
Despite the repeated warnings, many of th
place-poems are purely topographical. Th
poet laureate of Gunbar is patriotic and pre
cise: Of all the places I have been, be they
near or far, no place on earth I’ve ever seen
he tells us, “is the equal of Gunbar.” Also
it s on the road from Carrathool, and 6(
miles from Hay,” and, finally, it is “on the
map of the Mother State, a part of New
South Wales.”
Where the poet has been cunning enough
to impart a human interest to his descrip
tive matter, he succeeds better. The crude
way is to work off the topography first, and
“ th ® last . stanza, lightly add the
George Scott’s “Hamil
ton-on-Forth” (Tas.) :
Dear old houses! dear old wood bridge!
Dear old gum-trees evergreen !
I can see thee from that hill ridge,
But—with blinding tears between !
Another method is to stress the nata
interest, as Blac-kboy” does for “Wyalong”
I ve tramped the towns Australian,
_ I’ve camped both east and west;
I’ve tramped on highways alien,
But love Australia best.
Through districts celebrated
In story and in song,
My wander-lust is sated.
You’ll do me, Wyalong.
• bi £ th ~P lace interest is better stressed
in Ben Sun’s’ “Castlemaine”:—
Ay, Castlemaine, I’ve wandered since around the
world a bit,
Had good hnd bad and doubtful luck, but never
grovelled low,
For in my veins there is the blood, and in my heart
the grit,
That won you from the wilderness, a-down the
years to go.
And always, whether up or down, in triumph or
despair,
In heights of sweetest pleasure, or in throes of
poignant pain,
My mind reverting to the past divided aye its
care:
One thought for selfish ends and aims, and two for
Castlemaine.
And at the end he asks Destiny for this
simple Australian boon :
Yet once again the winding streets and grand old
hills to view,
And in its own good ale to pledge success to
Castlemaine.
“H.R.8.” loves his “Broken Hill,” with a
love that can contemplate her death. His
verse too often drops into the commonplace,
but his concluding stanzas are on a higher
plane:—
But not for ever shall Pactolus flow;
Ourselvek may sleep here when its dust shall fail:
And yet our dust, well-laid, will lie too low
To swell the burden of the passing gale;
Ere long our works, our very place, shall be
To children’s children but a memory.
Sturt peas will blossom ’neath the quandong trees
When Broken Hill is buried out of mind ;
And camel fleets will tack across the seas
Of grey-blue salt-bush, when the unresting grind
Of tube-mills dies; and always will remain
‘The fascination of the rolling plain.
The home instinct, too, is nicely stressed,
though in commonplace words, in the un-
identified “Our Place” of “6 Tooth” :
What worlds of quiet comfort there
When scorching summer’s day has passed!
Or, winter’s night, from fireside chair
To hearken to the south wind’s blast!
When all the -bairns are fast asleep,
And she, who tends them through the day,
Lists to our drone of wool or sheep,
While busy fingers mend away!
A flat—a house in town—for some I
For me—with her—Our Place is Home!
Personification adds another interest. A
fair example is “R.’s” “Townsville,” of
which the first verse is the best:—
I bring the dower of fruit or flower
Of rain-fed forest and dell,
But, strong in my right to stem the night
Of ail the winds of Hell,
I cling with pride to my brown hill-side
Ana sleep in the sun right well.
But solely by the descriptive method the
pobtic imaginaf’ >n may make something even
of Broken H.U. Thus R. J. Cassidy, in a
poem too long here to quote, reserved for
possible future publication, in fine phrases
indicates the magic of the Hill. It is the
best of the purely descriptive verses sent in,
but it lacks that htiman touch that lifts
verse into poetry. It is objective, not sub-
jective: a place seen, 1 not felt.
There is room, too, for humor in a place-
poem. Here are two examples :
OUR COW YARD.
A swamp before—a cemet’ry behind,
Between the two our humpy rears its head.
We rise before the dawn by kerosene,
And just before the dawn we go to bed.
We milk 100 cows—and as the last
Gives down .her milk and lurches from the pen
We head ’em off the pasture—for it’s time
To start and milk the - cows again.
The scenery. 0 Hell! who’s got the time?
But if you want the details in a poem,
The cow shed’s built of iron and 6x3;
Outside the shed the soil’s a heavy loam ;
Inside the shed there’s bails and wooden stools ;
Around the shed the fence is six foot high
While underfoot most everything is mud.
And up above —why, there’s the sky.
D. D. D. MACKINNON.
THE TOWN OF NOTHING IN PARTICULAR.
‘Twas most ambiguous and crude—
Implying blankness, Nature nude,
A vacuum in desuetude—
And little short of beastly rude
To label thus a neighborhood—
Nothing !
The object, Heaven—Goodness knows,
A district fair to diagnose
By what it wears or wins or owes,
And then, in very vulgar prose
A title trite like this impose—
Nix !
But still they did, and so until
The Judgment Morning, grey and chill,
W r hen Gabriel, as waiters will,
Presents, with frill, his little bill.
The borough will be branded ill—
Nought!
It happened thus (and hence my bliss)
Some wanted “that” and others “this.”
(In nomenclature what’s amiss.
No need to practice artifice.)
And so they billed the village this—
Cipher!
Though not indicative of much
Except the footprint of a crutch,
A void, hiatus, hole or such
Which echoes neither “Bake—!” or “Butch
Of genius it was a touch—
Nil!
No other place would rear or snort,
Or say the Solons here had sought
To borrow brains, or thieve its thought
With wrath no rival town was wrought.
All they could say was—well, in short,
Nhill!
A. E. B. WOOD.
There are left for final adjudication four
poems. . “White Timber,” by L. Kenny, is
purely descriptive, though written charm-
ingly, and the poet does not sign the blank
cheque of the first stanza:—
And I will sing of Gippsland
With gullies green and cool,
Where winds a play-trod pathway
Beside an old bush school.
“Devonport,” by May Rankin, is also de-
cnptlve, but with a human longing in it
that almost makes it lyric:—
DEVONPORT.
Blew ye now by Devonport,
Seawind of the South?
I can feel the kiss of her
Salt upon my mouth!
I can see the face of her
Rose against the morn;
Dales and downs of Devonport
White with budding thorn.
Sunset flame on headlands dun,
Beaches’ driven snow,
Rockpool gates of Wonderland
Where the sea-blooms blow;
Wild-rose stars against the cliff
Scenting every breeze....
Wind that blew by Devonport,
Whisper me of these!
Blown brown sails against the blue,
Wheeling wings of grey,
Sense of her and soul of her,
Weary years away ;
Oh, they leap to life again,
Gleaming fold and fold,
Dales and downs of ‘Devonport,
Lit with rose and gold!
MAY RANKIN.
So there are two poems, each suffused with
emotion, each hinting of the soul, and not
the bones, of a place, each depending for
its appeal on its mere name and the
poignancy of the memories of the writers.
The subject of each has been lifted from a
mere place to an emotional influence. One
is written by a girl who apologises “for any
mistakes or foolishness in the verses or the
(covering) letter on account of my youth
and inexperience,” but the anatomical' divi-
sion of her heart in the last stanza, and her
obvious groping for rimes, leaves her second
in the competition, though, as always, all
poems quoted fully here are paid for at space
rates. The winner is Marie E. J. Pitt, to
whom one guinea has been sent.
KARRLA HILL.
There are little birds that sing from tree to tree on
Karela.
In my dreams I hear them sing from tree to tree
In the branches where they swing so merrily—
But, ah me!
I may never seek the shadow of a tree on Karela
(Oh, the coolness of the shadow of a tree I)
There are little furry rabbits in the grass on Karela,
And I know just how they’re running through the
grass,
Or'they’re hiding from the shadow-clouds that pass—
Biit, alas!
I may never set my feet among the grass on Karela !
(Oh, the silver shine and shadow of the grass!)
On thy silver elope is buried half my heart, 0
Karela,
And the other half is drifting on the breeze
Where it swings among the branches of thy trees.
Though the seas '
Of adversity have held us far apart, 0 Karela,
Still thy silver shine and shadow holds my heart.
VICTORIA.
PATRICIA MURCHISON.
MATHINNA.
Ah, woe the day! And woe the day,
Betwixt the golden and the gray,
And woe the star that set for aye —
My star of dream — Mathinna!
I saw the lone moon lift her lamp,
I saw the white star-sentries tramp;
But far, far was the stranger's camp
By glen and gorge, by fell and swamp —
Far. far thy hills, Mathinna.
1 saw the dawn leap from his bed.
All wild and red and thunder-fed,
Rehind Ben Lomond's purple head
And the mountains —and Mathinna!
I saw the ragged red sun rise
And climb the steel stairs of the shies,
Like blood between mine aching eyes
And the mountains —and Mathinna.
I saw the thin road wind away,
A single thread of dusty grey,
Into the heart of burning day;
And my heart went with it all the way
To the mountains —and Mathinna.
I turned me from the mountains tall;
Behind me distance built her wall;
But still I hear thee call and call —
The sweetest, saddest voice of all —
/ hear thee call, Mathinna.
Yea, one sweet song will come to me
Where’er on earth my home shall be,
A song of all wild things and free,
The song that was the soul of thee,
Wild soul of song, Mathinna!
Dust is the white rose by the door,
And dust the scarlet poppy's core,
But thou wilt breathe for evermore
On winds of some enchanted shore —
Red rose of dreams-—Mathinna!
TASMANIA.
MARIE E. J. PITT.
Swinburne
Swinburne passes. His death in 1909
leaves him rather a curiosity than a force in
English literature. His biographer, Edward
Thomas, in Algernon Swinburne, a Critical
Study, in the fine series of biographies pub-
lished by Martin Seeker ( per Dymock’s
Library, Sydney), is more critical than
enthusiastic. His meticulous analysis of
Swinburne’s use of words damningly reveals
the mechanism that produced such sonorous
sound—and some poetry :
“Fire” and “light,” “bright” and “shine,”
with “desire” and “high” and “sky,” and
other words which their vowel sound and
Swinburne’s usage make cognate are master
words in his poetry. It can almost be said
that >he never writes one of those words
without repeating it or matching it with one
of the others The repetition—repetition
of single vowels or consonant sounds, of
single words, of groups of words, of ideas:
whether always conscious or not, these are
essentials in Swinburne’s art.... Since the
adjective is most ready when words are
wanted he used a great number, yet without
equally great variety. He kept as it were a
harem of words, to which he was constant
and absolutely faithful. Some he favored
more than others, but he neglected none.
He used them more often out of compliment
than of necessity.. ..
Yet the poet’s rhythm was sufficient tri-
umphantly to sweep the verbiage of his words
along:—
But Swinburne had almost no magic
facility of words. He can astonish and melt
but seldom thrill, and when he does it is
not by any felicity of as it were God-given
inevitable words. He has to depend on
sound and an atmosphere of words which is
now and then concentrated and crystallised
into an intensity of effect which is almost
magical, perhaps never quite magical. The
atmosphere comes from a vocabulary very
rich in words connected with objects and
sensations and emotions of pleasure and
beauty, but used somewhat lightly and even
in appearance indiscriminately. No poet
could be poorer in brief, electric phrases,
pictorial or emotional Perhaps the great-
est of his triumphs is in keeping up a stately
solemn play of words not unrelated to the
object suggested by his title and commence-
ment, but more closely related to rimes, and
yet in the end giving a compact and power-
ful impression. The play of words often on
the very marge of nonsense has acted as an
incantation, partly by pure force of cadence
and kiss of rhymes, partly by the accumula-
tive force of words in the right key, though
otherwise lightly used. Hardly one verse
means anything in particular, hardly one
line means anything at all, but nothing is
done inconsistent with the opening, nothing
which the _ rashest critic would venture to
call unavailing in, the complete effect.
And Edward Thomas adds that in some of
his poems. “English words sang together as
before 1866 they had never done,” and in
some others “the riming words have a life of
their own, as of birds singing or fauns danc- '
ing.”
I’oqm after poem is worth much or
nothing, according as the reader can take the
first line or verse as a keynote and then
allow the, metre to sing, with occasional
guidance from the words “light,” “men,”
“sea,” “thundering,” “sleep,” “weep,”
“sword,” “grave,” “time,” “crown,” etc. Not
that they are to be regarded as majestic
nonsense rimes, for they treat grave matters
gravely and grammatically.... It is not
thought set to music, but mnsm
absorbed thought. .. .In “A
sage” there is* fflSfrlt ’ as '
man being poetical on a steamer which t
no less and no more absurd than w
poetical in an omnibus ; but being noeti^ I*B 1 * 8
Poetry.. ..H may be said of most
that they love men and Nature Sr e C
S 3& 0t Sw, " b “™ * loved' 83
Equally detailed is his biographer’s an
alysis of Swinburne’s prose—but then Swin
ourne could not write prose.
Of the man, Edward Thorns
supplies a gallery of portraits. One comnl
site portrait shows him as a noticeably stn a n
man with a “glorious abundance” of '“fiery”
or “reddish yellow” or “orange” hair and
blue-grey or ‘clear green” eyes softened
by thick brown lashes. When he was reneat-
>ug poetry and apparently lie was nearly
always repeating poetry—his head hung ™
one side, his body shook, his high-pitched
voice expressed the utmost fervor and excite-
ment. Also, he might “jump about the room
in a manner somewhat embarrassing to the
listener.” He was always restless; his walk
was a dance. Another picture shows'him as
a short man with a cranium out of all pre-
portion to the rest of the structure, an enor-
mous head with too small a chin. He was
never known to complain of fatigue, of a
toothache or of a headache, he required very
little sleep, and out of doors he was “like
something blown before a wind,” having the
movements of a somnambulist.
This is a penetrating biography, composed
by a cold, logical mind—the last sort of mind
that the. poet would have chosen for the task
Edward Thomas’s niggard praise has, h«w-
ever, more value than mere superlatives.
An Australian Literary Shrine
. “Fox” suggests, chiefly for scenic reasoas
the humpy of the late George Essex Evans:
Re the advertisement appearing in
the wanted column of the “Red Page,” call-
ing for literary shrines, I recommend one—the
home of the late poet, George Essex Evans.
Evans’s works require no testimonial. The
house under mention is situated on the Main
Range (Great Dividing), Toowoomba (Q.),
about two-thirds of the way down on the
road to Brisbane, called by Toowoombaites
the Toll Bar-road. This spot is just
far enough out of the town of Toowoomba
to make a visit to it an excursion. Half a
day could easily be put in journeying to and
from the cottage and viewing the sights, v
The shrine proposed would have the fol-
lowing advantage over its fellow or rival
shrines. The scene from the top of the
Range is one of striking loveliness. Stretch-
ing away below the Range as far as the
vision can penetrate are cultivated farms,
patches of scrub, timbered and open plains,
mountain ranges, and isolated houses; and in
the middle distance is the town of Helidon,
the whole commingling to the satisfaction
of the eye. All is wrapped in a blue, misty
atmosphere that gives the far-off landscape
a sea-like appearance.
On the slopes of the Range itself is still
the forest primeval. In the immediate
foreground are Table Top and one or two
other solitary crags. Table Top is an in-
sulated mountain with a roof like to a bowl-
ing green. No tree adorns its grassy head,
but its sides are clothed w(th timber and
decked with giant boulders. Altogether it is
a dignified and impressive mount. At our
backs lies Toowoomba, surrounded by the
diversified greens of small cultivations.
The Toowoomba people are erecting a
monument to Evans’s memory in Webb
Park, on the top of the Range. I think,
however, it is preferable to pay homage to
the departed bard at his erstwhile residence
than at the foot of a piece of monumental
masonry that Evans never saw.
Wanted: A Sonnet on the Federal Capital
Now that the Federal Capital is named, it
becomes a fit —though rather dry, if not
parched—subject for the Australian poet.
This page offers fl Is. for the best sonnet
received, addressed to this page, by April 30.
The sonnet may be complimetary or condem-
natory, topographical or prophetical, but it
must be in the strict sonnet form. To lines
1,4, 5, 8 there must be only one rime, and
lines 2,3, 6, 7 must also all rime to one
sound. The sestette may have two or three
rimes, but must not end in a riming couplet.
Also, it is suggested that the whole 14 lines
should be poetry, or thereabouts.
— 'f
In these days of solemn reviews, a wel-
come is readily extended to a freak magazine,
entitled Rhythm (Seeker, Is., monthly).
Here you have “precious” stories, mad cari-
catures, and the cult of the triumphant post-
impressionist. Of this new art we learn that
“Post-Impressionist works are hung at all
the chief exhibitions on the Continent:
names of P.-I. painters are becoming as well-
known as those of the Academicians. It is
becoming increasingly evident that youth is
with the new movement: that the vital work
of the day is being done by young men who
are under the P.-I. influence.” It is evi-
dently time for Australian artists to get a
hustle on.

ELECTRIC LIGHT PLANT
(Lister-Bruston System—Patented).
This plant generates electricity automatically, and does away with large and
expensive storage battery. Can be started from drawing-room or bedroom.
With the exception of keeping the plant clean, lubricating and filling the
tanks, no attention is required.
Upon switching on the lights the plant works, and when the lights are turned
off the plant stops automatically.
The set is self-contained, and is delivered ready to work. No erection or
foundations necessary; all one has to do is to connect to the wires to the house,
shop, hall, hotel, or town lights, and fill up the tanks with oil. It is exceptionally
cheap as regards lighting costs, the user being able to enjoy all the many advantages
of electric light, at a rate per candle power cheaper than most systems of petrol
gas, acetylene, and other artificial lights, and have current always ready for lights,
fans, cooking, pumps, saws, separators, milking and chaffcutting, sewing and other
machines.
Is absolutely safe and reliable; no smell or fumes, dirt or smoke.

The Inky Way.
THE TOILERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“It is solid graft.”— Volunteer’s opinion of gas-
making.
With sweat and toil the world goes round,
And labor, like a hungry bear,
In nearly every spot is found
Waiting to catch us unaware;
We mostly find it worth our while
To take our punishment and smile.
The wheels must turn, the ships must go,
The paper must be sent to press;
We must have certain coins to show
When Mabel wants another dress,
And so we take the shortest way
To grip the world and make it pay.
But every fellow looks to see
The way the other earns his bread,
And, as a rule, it seems that he
Should change and take our job instead ;
For from our special point of view
He gets the easy things to do.
The man who needs must use his brain
Till all his nerves are worn and thin,
Is envied by the men who gain
A living teaching wheels to spin,
Or lumping coals, or chopping wood,
Or chasing bulls with hardihood.
The man who, near to Nature’s heart,
Rides round the brown and dusty run,
Is envied by the man of art
His splendid bronzing in the sun;
He thinks that he would freely change
His easel for a mountain range.
And they who with especial skill
May dodge the common lot of man,
And sit aloof, remote and still,
Because in cash their days began,
Are apt to find their golden shirk
Uncommonly like solid work.
Therefore in toil I set my trust,
And with a mad, unresting pen
String rhymes together for a crust
And envy not my brother men;
For all they do and all they think
I mix with music, mirth and ink.
N.s.w.
GEORGE STREET.
“Box 2459” (from Johannesburg) : “Splodger”
(B. 19/12/’l2), talking about the cost of living in
early Coolgardie, mentions G. H. Kingswell’s emo-
tion when confronted with a certain elongated ac-
count. Kingswell is here in Johannesburg, where he
manages, part owns, and is everything that is worth
talking about in connection with the Sunday Times,
a large, weighty 40-page Sabbath paper, which is
now filling its spare columns with squeals anent the
Betting Bill, a measure which was given its kick
off by the Crimes. L. R. McLeod, who edited the
Crimes for a couple of years after the christening,
seceded to start the Observer —an alleged copy of
The Bulletin. The parody had its last forme
locked exactly 12 moons after birth; it was then
metamorphosed into a second Sunday organ, labelled
the Sunday Post, and it appears to be more than
holding its own.
* * * *
Frank Whitcombe, who not long ago joined the
staff of Melbourne Herald, is not one of vour nar-
row-circle, small-range men. An Englishman by
birth, he was young and married when the Chicago
big fire burnt him out. He set to work naintinrr «,
titbits of the ruins, and sold his picturettes on an
ascending scale from the price of chops to the nri™
of joints. Presently, coming to Australia he tmt
on the staff of Melbourne Evening Standard and
wrote a few one-act plays which were professionally
performed, and had some success. When the Stanti
ard followed many bright papers on the drearv
to the devil ‘‘Whit” retired to his fannlet between
Brighton and Caulfield. A year or two later he was
dragged on to Nathaniel Levi’s Evening News
When the News crumbled, he started farming exten’'
sively in East Gippsland, and kept it going for
several years, writing at odd times for the Argus and
others. Now, he is back once more to staff wort ■
but no “duty book” can ever deaden his pen. " ’
* * *
Pensioned off by Melbourne Herald, as a reward
for years of service whereof the memory of man ex
tendeth not to the contrary, “Jim” Middleton at last
lays down the pen that was nearly always a pencil
Formerly editor of the paper, Middleton has like
Mulvaney with the Oul Regiment, seen its personnel
change, “not wanst, nor twice, but scores av times ”
His strongest point was law-court reporting, all in
longhand. Give Middleton a soft pencil, a ’wad of
copy-paper and a big, joyful case, and he would turn
out three and four columns a day in a style that
would tax many a Pitmanite to equal. Failin»-
health drove him from the Inky Way.
It was at the old Melbourne Immigrants’ Home
adjoining the Alexandra Gardens—an ancient build'
ing that is now about to perish—that Julian Thomas
earned his pen-name of “The Vagabond.” lie vaga-
bonded there for a night or two, and made his earlv
literary splash out of the copy thus collected. In
like manner Greenwood became “The Amateur
Casual” through trying the casual ward of a British
workhouse in quest of copy. But Greenwood encoun-
tered bitter cold, skilly, vermin, a hard bed, stone-
breaking at daylight, and the vile abuse of a Bumble
dressed in a cocked hat and a shred of brief authority.
He earned his designation at much greater cost than
Julian Thomas.
“J.F.H.”: Passed away J. W. Arrowsmith, of Bris-
tol, the leading English provincial publisher. His
prosperity was built up on “Called Back,” a story
written by a Bristol auctioneer named Fred Fargus
(pen-name, Hugh Conway). It was Henry Labou-
ehere, M.P., who set the boom going. He alighted
upon “Called Back” accidentally, and was so fasci-
nated by the story that he sat up till four in the
morning to finish it. When he sounded its praises in
Truth, the orders began to roll in from all quarters.
Arrowsmith had six presses running night and day,
and even then was unable to keep pace with the de-
mand. Fargus sold the copyright to Arrowsmith for
£BO, but the latter could afford to be generous, and
he gave the author thousands of pounds which he
could legally have kept for himself. It was to
Arrowsmith that Rudvard Kipling offered his first
book, “Plain Tales from the Hills,” but the Bristol
publisher did not think much of them, and declined.
He afterwards publicly proclaimed himself an idiot
for doing it.
“Penman”: If my hair did not stand on end
already, it would over the preposterous notice of
editor Windsor in the London Times. Some brilliant
hack, whose knowledge was confined to the fact that
Windsor edited the Age for 28 years, wrote what mvst
be the facts, seeing that the Times prints them,
though he is wrong in every particular. No mention,
for instance, is made of David Svme! Windsor, it
seems, with terrific energy, plumped upon the Age
when it was fearfullv low down, inaugurated the
vigorous advocacy of Protection, for which he was a
zealot, and carried it into the triumphant Australian
policy. All the politicians were afraid of him. He
virtually appointed the whole of the Victorian dele-
gates to the Convention, which framed the National
Constitution. In short, the whole life-work of aggres-
sive and masterful! David Syme is transferred to
Windsor, whose fine literary points don’t get a
word. Thus is Australia written up for England : and
the most ridiculous part of the business is that_ the
opinions of such ignoramauses on Australian subjects
are cabled to us. and we are supposed to be tremen-
dously impressed by them.

All parts of the Pump End are sub-

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Joy Ride.
Ah Gawd! It makes me sick to think
Of what I ’eard an ’ seen;
Poor ’Arry like a wet rag flung
Across the wrecked machine;
An’ Rose, ’er face all chiner-white
Against the gory green.
Now ’Arry Cox ’e drives a car
For Doctor Percy Gray.
Ses ’e to me: “On Sund’y nex’
The Doc. will be away.
’Ow is it for a little trip
To Fernville for the day?
“I know two bonzer girls,” ’e ses;
“Fair ’otties, both, they are.
There’s Rose who serves behind thG joint
In Mudge’s privit bar,
An’ Lena Crump who jerks the pump
Down at the Southern Star.”
Now, who’d refuse a Sund’y trip
With girls an’ all give in?
The car was there an’ oil to spare.
To rat would be a sin!
An’ who’d refuse a drop o’ booze
When pals is flush o’ tin?
Wot all the courts an ’ papers say
Can’t add to my distress ....
Rose, with the hlood upon ’er face
An' on ’er crumpled dress!
An’ that poor chump who got the hump —
Ah, Gawd! ’E was a mess!
The girls ’ad stout at ten mile out,
An' we was drinkin’ beer.
I swear they lies like ’ell who ses
That we was on our ear!
For we was both, I take me oath,
As sober as me here.
Now, Lena was a dashin’ piece,
’lgh-spirited an’ flash.
’Twas plain enough to me that day
That ’Arry’d done ’is dash.
An’ Rose —(Ah! how ’er eyes did stare)
Rose was my speshul mash.
It’s easy now fer folks to talk
Who might have done the same.
We meant no ’arm to anyone,
An’ ’Arry knew ’is game.
’Twas like a flash, the skid —the crash.
An’ we was not to blame.
I wislit I could shut out that sight;
Fergit that awful row!
Poor Rose! ’Er face all chiner-white,
Like I can see it now;
An’ ’Arry like a heap o’ clothes
Jist chucked there any’ow.
They ses we painted Fernville red;
They ses that we was gay;
But wot come after dulls me mind
To wot them liars say.
We never dreamed of death an’ ’ell
When we set out that day.
’Twas ev’nin’ when we turned for ’ome ;
The moon shone full that night;
An’ for a mile or more ahead
The road lay gleamin’ white;
An’ Rose sat close aside o’ me,
’Er face turned to the light.
Wot if we sung a song or two?
Wot if they ’eard us shout?
Is song an’ laughter things to curse
An’ make a fuss about?
“Go faster! faster!” Lena screams.
An’ ’Arry let ’er out.
I’d give me soul jist to ferget.
Lord! how ’er eyes did stare!
’Er kisses warm upon me lips,
I seen ’er lyin’ there,
Blood on ’er face, all chiner-white,
An’ on ’er yeller ’air.
I never took no ’eed ’o pace
(I’ve been on twenty trips),
An’ Rose was restin’ in me arms,
’Er cheek against my lips.
A precious lot I dreamed of skids,
A lot I thought o’ slips.
I only know we never thinks —
I know we never dreams
Of folk out walkin’ on that road ;
Till, sudden, Lena screams....
An’, after that, the sights I saw
I’ve seen again in dreams.
We never seen the bloke ahead!
’Ow can they call us rash?
I jist seen ’Arry move to shove
’ls arm around ’is mash;
I seen ’er jump to grab the wheel,
Then, Lord ! there came the smash !
Aw they can hlame an ’ cry their shame!
It ain’t for that I care.
I held ’er in my arms an laugnea
Then seen ’er lyin’ there,
The moonlight streamin’ on er face,
An ’ on ’er yeller hair.
VICTORIA.
DEN.
The Butterfly.
At sunset came a butterfly
A-flitting through the dog-wood scrub.
I saw it as my horse went by
A flimsy grey-brown butterfly
I heard the kookaburras cry
Up where the creaking branches rub,
And then I saw the butterfly
Come flitting through the dogwood scrub.
My feet tramped on beside my horse
And creaking waggon up the hill.
I was the driver, so of course
I plodded on beside my horse.
I lost the butterfly, perforce,
Amid the sunset lights a-spill.
And so I passed, beside my horse
And creaking waggon up the hill.
vf v heart went with the butterfly,
iIf And danced beside it through thesbade.
The long, red road was hot and dry
MV heart went, with the butterfly.
The dogwood scrub was green and high,
And cool the shadow that it made.
tvtv hpart went with the butterfly,
M Ami danced beside it through the shade.
VICTORIA.

Special “HALLEY” LORRY
For Wool-Carrying and General Work
Halley Industrial Motors, Ltd., of Glasgow, Scotland, have designed a special
type of Motor-Lorry, ideally adapted for Australian needs. A model will
be shown at Dalgety’s Exhibit at the Royal Agricultural Society’s Show.
I his Lorry is the last word in Australian motor-transport, and really
demands inspection.
THE HIGH CLEARANCE, STRENGTH, and POWER, and the
sterling reliability of this Lorry enable it to operate successfully in the
roughest country.
Steel wheels and tyres of large proportions are employed. Winding
drums and patent reduction gear are fitted to the rear wheels. These
winding drums, together with a steel hawser, enable the lorry to pull itself
out of bogs, etc. They may be used also for general hoisting and loading
work.
The ground clearance is 18 inches under chain cases, and 27 inches
under rear axles and all mechanism. The front axle is pivoted at its
centre in such a way as to enable either of the wheels to pass over an object
16 inches high, without the least distortion of the frame.
To Garry 6 Tons and Trail 2 Tons
Gear Box, as in all models, is foolproof, and gives four speeds forward
and one reverse. Chains and gearing are completely enclosed in oil-tight
and dust-proof casings. Extra large radiator is fitted, ensuring perfect
cooling—also 50-gallon tank for reserve water. Petrol Tank has 25 gallons
capacity.
Any Type of Body can be Fitted
Do not fail to inspect this fine, highly-specialised model. The fullest
information will be sent, post free, to anyone unable to benefit by a personal
inspection.

TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.

By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty-
Six Shillings Sterling you can have The Bulletin
posted regularly to you every week for a year.
Cheques. Drafts, etc., which should be crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to The
Manager.
How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some
care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin/' is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the form
of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short
Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Australian sub-
jects-, and not exceeding two columns in length, or,
say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded with-
out stamps for return it will be understood that the
copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Personal
applications for return of MS. cannot receive atten-
tion. The Bulletin desires, further, those sending
MS. to distinctly understand that it is not respon-
sible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash, of
sufficient merit and interest and adapted for direct
reproduction, or any interesting photograph illus-
trative of a current event, will be paid for same on
acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea, but
unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn—will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY. MARCH 20, 1913.
THE TEMPORARY CAPITAL AT
CANBERRA.
the singing of the “Old Hundredth,” the
* blare of trumpets, and the boom of cannon,
the big swindle perpetrated upon the people
of Australia, in connection with the Federal
Capital, was advanced another stage last
week. There was much congratulatory
speech-making of a very ordinary sort, and
the name Canberra was allowed to remain
as the designation of the place that is to be
built. It is a better name than “Myola” or
“Pacifica,” or any of the numerous absurdi-
ties proposed, and is large and sonorous
enough to answer its purpose. A commence-
ment column was also started, and for the
moment it may be conceded that the site
question is closed, and that some sort of a
Federal settlement will rise upon the wastes
of Canberra. Whether King O’Malley can
succeed in importing his proposed rivers
and lakes into the landscape, and creating
even on the smallest scale the city beautiful
of his dreams, is not a matter of critical
importance. The politicians of Australia
have combined to promote the construction
of an official village rather than a great
city. A place with a population of 10 or
15 thousand is likely in the near future to
drink sparingly from the Cotter trickle, and
contain the Parliament and Governor-
General at intervals. Also, it will contain
buildings, streets and parks, more or less
fashioned according to the weird taste of
O’Malley, and that is about ail that is
likely to be achieved by the long wrangle,
the columns upon columns of print that have
been devoted to the question, and all the
cannon, trumpets, speeches and other mat-
ters which marked last week’s opening dis-
play. With a firm conviction that Australia
did not know what was good for it, the
statesmen of both parties have refused to
test the opinion of the people by Referendum
on the whereabouts of the capital, and .the
foundation-stones of our first memorial of
national unity are laid in national distrust.
It does not seem an occasion for three cheers
or for one cheer, but a time for asking some
very serious questions about democracy, and
demanding a very clear answer in the near
future. If our more important national con-
cerns can be conducted without the feeling
of the people being known or asked for, our
method of government has something very
seriously wrong about it, and our foremost
need is to remove from the politicians who
are unwilling to consult the people their
right of initiative by taking that initiative
directly into the people’s own hands.
* * * *
Whatever may be said in favor of the
Canberra capital, one thing is perfectly
clear—that as a seat’ of government for this
big continent it is neither in position nor in
natural advantages the best that could have
been chosen. Australia wants the best and
only the best; and when the people are fully
alive to that fact, it is quite inconceivable
that Canberra can remain permanently the
capital. For the satisfaction of certain parish
persons, and some Sydney boodlers,
who threatened to destroy a nation if
their petty interests were not sufficiently
regarded, the choice of a site was ridiculously
limited by the Federal Constitution; and for
the further satisfaction of the same people,
whose views in the meantime had grown
smaller and narrower, every effort was
made to secure a capital which should be
no capital, but merely a suburb of con-
gested Sydney. No one looking at a map
of Australia can be persuaded that the great
city of the future will rise at Canberra.
In point of situation the far and lonely ham-
let of Alice Springs has a much worthier
claim; in point of the natural advantages
which may enable a city to grow into a vast
hive of splendid industry, where white men
live under the best conditions, there are a
thousand places in the continent in com-
parison with which Canberra is an absurdity
and a jest. The choice made, if not the
worst possible, runs the worst possible very
close; and it is practically certain that
within the next 20 years one of the urgent
political cries to which the statesmen ol the
time will be compelled to give heed will be
for the shifting of the entire outfit to a place
worthy to be the heart and centre of a live
and earnest federation. Then a time will
come when Canberra will stand forlorn as
the. ruins of Palmyra, a monument of our
political folly and worse, and wandering
tourists will look upon its 27 feet of com-
mencement column on Kurrajong Hill, and
pelt verbal offal at the memorial which tells
of the cowardice of early politicians who,
elected on a Labor vote, were afraid to
trust the people with the choice of a site for
their country’s capital. And when Canberra
ceases to be the capital, the cash spent upon
it will be a more serious waste than even the
interest on Cohen’s loans, for the whole col-
lection of bricks, gardens and imported lakes
will be useless as the Pyramids, and hopeless
as a grave at the South Pole.
As marking a passing stage in Australia’s
national growth, Canberra may, neverthe-
less, answer its purpose. Reviewing the
work of the recent years, it seems too much to
expect that our present alleged statesmen
could grasD the possibilities of a great national
city. Nations do sometimes learn something
from the follies of their past when their evil
effects have been pressed bitterly home upon
them. The making of a city is a great
matter, and the O’Malleys and Fishers
can hardly be expected to rise to the height
of grasping their splendid opportunities. The
city of Canberra —an official village depend-
ing upon and in turn helping to feed the city
of Sydney—is perhaps as big a thing as
those now in office are capable of creating
with any chance of success. Larger states-
men must face the larger problem of the
future, and keener sight and deeper vision
go to the making of the white man’s capital
of the South. Canberra may then be some
sort of guide to the larger work, its failures
may lead to the larger successes, and a city,
clean, vigorous, sweet and wholesome rise
on the ruins of the former mistake. Nor
shall we be the first to up-camp and move on
to better things. The map of the Old World
is sprinkled thickly with the remains
of cities which failed because of their situa-
tions. Kings and emperors planted them
with blare of trumpets and solemn laying
of foundation stones to the strains of what
may have been the age’s equivalent for the
“Old Hundredth” ; but the land and the sea
and the rivers and the water supply and a
thousand other factors which go to the crea-
tion of a city spoke their word, and the places
were left a desolation. Canberra will be
made into some sort of a capital, and
the taste and wisdom of Barnum
O’Malley, the Minister whom even
the Tory press-barrackers for Can-
berra parade as a cheap joke, will
write itself into its shape and growth;
but Australia will march on to bigger
things, and the final choice of the
greater capital site is not yet. Meanwhile,
the guns and trumpets have ceased to sound,
the imports of cheap-labor goods continue
to . come over the low tariff wall, and the
voice of the unemployed will soon be louder
than the cheers that last week shook the
flags on Kurrajong Hill.
THE BLIND MAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Against a wall near the Temple a blind man has
stood for 27 years listening to the sounds of Lon-
don : waiting for coppers.
With his back to the wall of Empire, his hat
in his palsied hands,
Passed by in the roar of traffic, the Blind
Man shivers and stands;
With a whine to the earth for coppers, and a
curse when a rival’s near.
He has begged for the Empire’s largesse full
many a vagabond year.
When his sons in the Hungry ’Forties arose
and departed hence,
Bull strode like a purse-proud beggar in his
new-found affluence;
He mocked at the Yank and the German: “I
have sons at work,” said he,
“And they raise tae the golden plunder in the
lands beyond the sea!”
“You are strong,” said the nations straightly
—“why loaf on your sons for bread,
Till your ancient strength has vanished, and
your sons wax wise, instead?
Why strut like a self-made cripple, a shade
on each forthright eye.
Till your sight in your dotage passes, and you
whine in a noisome sty?
“Why batten on distant children in the lands
far oversea,
When the world has need of an England in
a strong man’s panoply?
Why halt when the world goes onward?
Come! March with the nations all!” —•
“Be off!” said the Blind Man, sourly. “Let
me stand by the Empire’s wall!
“I am rich with my children’s shekels, torn
forth from the sundered earth;
With my sons for the Empire’s helots, let me
laugh in my vagrant mirth!
March on with your upstart nations, with
the pelfless rank-and-file?
Not me!” said the Blind Man, proudly.
“That’s scarcely the English style.
“I am tired of the marching onward, I am
rich with my sons’ red gold.
So why should I march with nations that are
not of my purse-proud fold?
I’ve bought me the pitch of a beggar, and
this is my English whim—
To beg whilst my children labor far out on
the Empire’s rim !”
“I guess,” at last said the Kanuck, “it’s time
that we took a pull—
I can raise me a brand-new nation with the
cash that I pay to Bull !”
“That’s so,” said the tall Australian; “I’m
full of the Old Man’s game;
Let him work for himself and prosper; mine,
too, is a nation’s aim.”
Then up spake the Afrikander —he was bat-
tered and lame and sore.
“You fellows were fooled by the Blind Man,”
he said, “when you spilt my gore.
You were fooled by the stout old beggar, who
stands by the distant Bank ;
So we scrapped, but I bear no malice”—and
his voice was clear and frank.
Then answered the Maorilander: “There’s
something we won’t deny—
We were all of us over-hasty when we black-
ened your nation’s eye.
We were fooled by the distant Blind Man,
when we rose to answer his call,
And I reckon we Maorilanders were the
greatest fools of all!
“So, seeing our jobs are waiting, and seeing
our parent’s hale,
I vote that we give him a trifle, paid down
on the cheerful nail !”
“Agreed,” said the lean Canadian. “I vote
that his goods come in
On a cheaper scale than the German’s and
the Dago’s kith and kin!”
“That’s right,” said the tall Australian. “Till
his Bank-side pitch he leaves,
Instead of our total harvest we’ll chuck
him our choicest sheaves !
For the mines of the Roaring ’Fifties sing
small in Australia now,
And I work at the earnest anvil, and I follow
the patient plough !”
“That’s me!” said the Afrikander, “for the
Jo’burg boom is o’er;
But I’ll join with the rest as partners, till
the Old Man toils once more !”
So the voice of the Four Young Nations rang
out with a strong refrain—
“ Get away from those needless crutches- —
get back to your job again !”
And the Blind Man wakened slowly, for his
head was large and sore ;
But his eyes were blind and sightless ’neath
the fiscal shades he wore !
He stood by the Bank bare-headed, and he
felt in his Freetrade hat;
“Why, where is the Children’s Tribute?”
said the Blind Man. “Tell me that.”
Then the voice, of the children answered, and
the new note angered him—
“We are mates, and have yarned together out
here on the Empire’s rim !
And we reckon the times have altered, so we
offer you ten per cent.
O’er the Yank and the Russ and the German,
and the Jap .and the Belgian gent!”
“We offer”— the world spun round him, and
the Blind Man waved his crutch
As he yelled in a voice of thunder: “I reckon
this is too much !
What! Haven’t I nursed and kept you ; and
haven’t I raised you all;
And haven’t I spoken wisdom from my pitch
by the Empire’s wall?
“I’ve given the lean Canadian his land with
its wintry snows,
And I’ve dowered the tall Australian with
the soil where the sunshine glows !
I’ve toiled for the Afrikander—now the Blind
Man’s wealth is spent:
And” —he howled in a shrill crescendo — “you
offer me ten per cent.!”
Then the Four Young Nations answered—
and their tone was strangely new—
“ You collared our plunder gladly, and you
painted the Empire blue !
You have wasted the substance gaily that
we won with our distant sweat;
You have revelled and ouaffed and roystered
—now you prate of the Empire’s debt!
“But the views of the tall Australia
the lean Canadian, too allan an d
Show Bull as the Empire’s debtor to
muscle and pluck and thew ' t 0 OUr
So we urge that you strip instanter «,
shades from your British eves-. th ° 8e
That you turn to your job, and'labor lit*
parent strong and wise!” llke a
But the Blind Man cursed and thnna ,
and he whined and he howled ’Xf 1 '
Till the upstart nations hearkened ITk
marched in their stern array' they
So this is the blind old dotard,’’ sa i d th *
Yank to his German mate tae
“Whom we left swelled up with his snlenu
at his crumbling Empire’s gat(G”
And the German gazed in silence, and never
a word he said, ver
But he moved with his Empire onwards—Ki
eyes on the goal ahead ! S hjs
And only the Brown Man lingered—
breathed in the Blind Man’s ear- “ e
“Your pitch by the Bank is threatened-
draw your fleets and armies near!
“/ will watch in the far Pacific where the
tall Australian stands— tae
Where the-voice of the lean Canadian quotes
terms and shouts commands
Then the voice of the Blind Man quavered-
It shall be as you ask,” said lie
Henceforth on the rim of Empire the Brown
Man speaks for me.”
So the voice of the tall Australian cries loud
for ships and guns,
Whilst the Blind Man begs for Conner.
where the traffic of Empire stuns'
TV here he s stood since the Hungry ’Forties.
in the midst of London’s roar,
With his Freetrade hat projected, whilst the
Nations armed for War!
VICTORIA.
GRANT HERVEY.
plain English!
The Beautiful Doctrine of Non-
Resistance.
An English Quaker gentleman is in Aus-
tralia, observing at first hand the working
of the Universal Training System, to which
his sect is opposed, "for conscientious rea-
sons.” The Quaker folk object to the use
of force in either private or national differ-
ences, believing that everything could be
made nice and harmonious by a policy of
love and brotherliness and non-resistance
generally.. Since a student should scruti-
nise all sides of his subject, The Bulletin
commends this plain, unvarnished tale to
the Quaker student’s notice. At Seoul
(Korea) there recently opened a new trial
of certain Christians, previously convicted
(on their own confessions) of conspiring
to murder Japanese Governor-General
Terauchi. .Baron Yun Chi Ho (a former
Korean Cabinet Minister) had been treated
as head of . the conspiracy, and sentenced to
10. years’ imprisonment, and several other
prisoners had been gaoled for longer or
shorter terms. Some of the evidence at the
new trial (reported in the Japan Adver-
tiser, Tokio) was enlightening—it may
even enlighten a Quaker observer as to the
whyness of the Australian Universal Train-
ing business:—
Prisoner Kim Epng Pong, sentenced to five vears,
was examined.
1 he Court: At the police headquarters you con-
fessed that you organised your sympathisers into a
Semen Doshikwai (Young Men’s Association) for the
purpose of helping to establish a military school at
West Chientao, and assassinating prominent officials.
Prisoner: Yes, but this was all because I was
forced to say so under inexpressible torture. I was
under examination with torture for 15 days. I was
strung up, suspended in mid-air, flogged, with my
lower garments removed. At one time they said they
would flog me to death. I several times lost con-
sciousness, and was restored by having cold water
dashed over me. I was forced to crouch under a
shelf not higher than my breast, in such a position
that I could neither sit down nor stand up, and
beaten with the stove poker, until I thought I was as
good as dead. Finally, I said “ves, yes,” to every-
thing they said. If I had been asked under these
circumstances whether I had murdered my father I
should have said “yes” to that, too.
Another appellant, Kn, Cheng Hyen, also ex-
plained things: “I confessed, because like the others,
I suffered unspeakable tortures. I was 70 days at
the police headquarters. At one time I was hung
up bv the hands from 2 a.m. to 10 o’clock in the
evening. The examiner said: ‘lf you don’t confess,
we shall torture you to death. We don’t care if
we . do kill you.’ After that, they tortured me
again. At one time, on a very cold winter day, they
stripped me naked, and poured ice-water over me
from head to foot for three hours. They kept tell-
ing me to confess, saying: ‘Seventy or 80 men have
already confessed. You are the only one that holds
out.’ So at last I yielded.”
Cha Kyun Syel also had some experiences: “It
was very cold. They accused me of those crimes,
and I denied it. At one time very cold water was
poured over my head. I was beaten with a stove
poker and other instruments.... I was under exam-
ination in this way for 26 days. In the meantime,
they starved me. The examiner said: ‘lf you confess
we will feed you and stop this torture.’ I have the
scars on me now. They pricked my head and shoul-
ders with knives; they scon died me with fire. I
remember everything pretty clearly up to the 14th
or 15th day. After that, things are confused. After
they ceased torturing me, I could hear the agonising
cries of the others who were suffering.”
The gentleman of peace from England will
notice that the unhappy Christian Korean
prisoners carried out to the uttermost the
Quaker doctrine of non-resistance. They
might have resisted once ; but the time for
that passed when Japan challenged Korea
to produce its gun, and Korea hadn’t any
gun to produce. And because Australia
doesn’t want to be in the same shameful
position it is busy getting a gun now, and
learning how to use it.

The House Unbeautiful.
Randolph Bedford’s curse and cry:—
The ugly, inconvenient, uncomfortable dwelling
houses of the people may not be ignored, because
in them people spend most of their lives, and their
lives are made by their surroundings to a greater
extent _ than at first sight appears. The hideous
galvanised-iron “terraces” of Richmond and Colling-
wood and Waterloo and Newtown produce, many in-
stances of the class of people the thinker might expect
from a judgment of their dwellings. The working-
class wife who suddenly kills herself and children,
and the suburban man who commits deeds of violence
which are debited to his indulgence in one shandy-
gaff immediately preceding the outrage, are tio oc
less blamed than pitied when one sees the hovel-
in which a rentlord grinds them for dividends.
But Australia suffers in its buildings not so muc
from a desire to save money as from mere want o
taste, equally of the architects who plan the dwei •
ing and the ignorant person who pays for it. when
a savage begins building, his first instinct is w
build to his climate, his second to build it 81


materials as natural to his climate as can be. The
Eskimo builds his hut of snow, not only because it
is very much the easiest thing to his hand, but be-
cause of it he can build the warmest and most
draught-proof house. The Papuan builds his house
on stilts, and leaves it perforated like a meat-safe
to admit every breeze that moves. The Australian
aboriginal rarely built a house at all, and so was
condemned by the Australian pioneer for recognising
the Australian climate, which doesn't call for shelter
except during a very few months of the year. The Greek
builded to suit his climate just as the Indian
architect loved the domes Australia hasn’t got ; and
the Persian builded minarets and cupolas and flat-
roofed shady houses, surrounding cool quadrangles,
and the Italian copied the flat roofs and central
courts, and bettered it all by having the square of
the house only a room deep, so that the outer win-
dow permitted air to the quadrangle window and
across the inner court to the rooms on the other
side of the house. All these things one would think
the Australian architect would copy as soon as he
found a client with the money; one would expect,
after noting the Australian climate and the banking
returns and other evidences of Australia’s prosperity,
to see Sydney’s residential bays the dreams of
beauty they are not—to suddenly come upon a little
Palace of the Strozzi, to see a water-frontage house
imitating the Casa D’Oro of Venice ; a stone cupola,
or among trees a minaret green with the oxidation of
its copper. Instead, what does one see?
I live in a house perched on a yellow-brown sand-
stone rock, with a streak of red in it to mark the
stronger iron. The rock, a fig-tree and the morning-
glory that covers part of the cliff and has climbed
over the house, are the only beautiful things about
that dwelling excepting the bay, which does not belong
to it The house is all right on its ground floor,
though it has been built to hold the sweat and heat
of the climate, and not to part with it in a hurry ;
but the bedrooms —the important chambers for an
Australian house —have been sacrificed to the roof.
This is of the steen-hipned kind, much in demand
in a mad Countrv like Switzerland, where it is ad-
visable to have a roof like a church spire, so that
the snow will fall off and save the wages of the
Snow Shovellers’ Union. There is no snow in Aus-
tralia save in the Kosciusko country, yet all these
slavish copyists, the architects of Sydney who do
these things, rob the houses of snace and breathing
surface by making roofs for vodellers and for snow
to commit felo de se. As a result, this alleged bed-
room of mine has a door under the peak of the roof
where the bed may not stand. The bed is placed
under the lowest portion of the snow-shed, so that
the roof is within 2ft. of the bed head. The low
roof makes a mosquito harbor, but the ingenious
house-furnisher gives you a mosquito net to puzzle
him with. That mosquito net gives another roof
2ft below the snow-shed and 4ft. above the sleeper s
face and of hot nights the mosquito sings blood-
thirsty threats at the man inside the palatial
vacuum, and the man sleeps by jerks and wonders
whv he is so tired of mornings.
All around me are Queen Mart Antte houses, with
the precipitous roof of Switzerland, where the flat roof
should be holding thousands of diners looking out
over the wondrous panorama of Svdnev s night,,
white gables, shingle roofs, breathless places full of
angles for the love of angles mimicries of
old English farm-houses, where the death-rate was
five times as much as Australian conditions should
provide: dinky little windows that admit the winds
of Heaven at only one angle; small, iron-studded
doorsTn imitation of the vestrv entrance of some
little English church full of catarrh and other in
teresting diseases; glaring white small-entranced
houses like nothing so much as Noah s arks, so
that you could expect to see wooden animals come
out by two and a slate-colored Noah striking
Tapttfth witli fl Virisrlit pinp-TPPP. ...
The original builders of the English . church wi
the interesting diseases and the original farm-
houses of the high death-rate are
thev knew no better. They didn t know that qara

Ward, Cohen an Idea.
IotJSEPH Warp eonjin Dukedom'oriwarna,
deavors to capture the L . , p eerage he
&S=p h e.king loan-
tries, induced to that course by the example
of Australia, are just on the eve of gripping
the idea of local fleets for the joint defence
of the Pacific, when the great champion of
the pledge and the pawn-ticket again dashes
into the fray, and re-introduces his dis-
carded notion of a united plunge into the
cash-vats of Israel, in order to provide
Britain with some ironmongery to protect
her interests at our end of the world. At a
cost of 10s. per head per annum for each
white person in the countries which became
parties to the arrangement, 25 Dread-
noughts or thereabout could be sent cruis-
ing around all the waters of the world,
ready to let fly at anything that looked like
insulting the might and majesty of empire.
It is a showy sort of scheme to present at
the shirt-front of J. Bull ; and although
it has no chance of coming off, Bull is
expected to look pleased about it and do some-
thing for the patriotic gentleman who makes
the suggestion. Australia has already set its
course, and intends to have a fleet of its
own, under its own control, and mostly of
its otvn building, and manned by its own
sailors, and can therefore be counted out of
any such arrangement which may be made.
Canada is halting between two opinions,
but the Liberal crowd —and Liberalism will
have another innings there in due course —
is strongly in favor of following the Aus-
tralian plan, and undertaking the defence of
the North Pacific with a local fleet. Maoriland
is in doubt; but Ward is such an extinct
volcano in his own land that the mere fact
of his advocating a scheme ought to pretty
well ensure its rejection. The proposal is
full of bad spots, anyway. The borrowed
cash, with its 3 per cent, sinking fund,
would still be unpaid when the Dreadnoughts
w r hich it purchased were on the scrap-heap ;
and the scheme involves enormous and con-
tinued borrowing, which in the nature of
things could bring permanent joy to no one
but Cohen. The basic idea of the great
imperial splash is that Britain can manage
things ever so much better than anybody
else, and that the growing spirit of national-
ism in Canada, Australia and Maoriland can
count for nothing in the efficiency and
capability of their sea defenders. All this, is
tremendously open to question. Everything
seems to indicate that an Australian fleet
can do better for the defence of Australia’s
shores than a section of a British fleet in
Australian waters. True, Australians have
never been tested at the game, but. then the
same thing applies to the British, who
haven’t been seriously tried at sea since
Trafalgar, except in the American war of
1812, when they didn’t shine to any extent.
First and last, England has been tolerably
blind in all its dealings with over-seas mat-
ters, and trust in a fleet guided and directed
from London might be a very blind business
indeed. If our own statesmen mismanage
things they are at least near enough to be
goaded into right ways by the pieces of
jagged rock we throw at them; but .if
Downing-street erred to the extent of leaving
us altogether in the lurch, there would be
nothing left to us except to sit down and
let the raging foeman walk on us. From
no conceivable standpoint, except that of
Cohen, does Touseph Ward’s proposal.look
anything better than his late Maoriland
financing, and saying that we reach about
the limit.
Home Rule for Syria.
THE demand of SydlTor Home Role is a
natural result of the haxnmenng the Turk
ix; >*“i.srssi/sre'cu
ambition to be rr of the Bosphorus,
anpalnng misgo t , h j the Syrian
Forty years ago the. Arab o Band y
desert hoped for f"Sine ever since the
wastes. l° d fir^ e N APOLEON. has had a linger-
that' rratce miaht come in to ht.
rescue. But the Turk contrived somehow
to bluff Europe and maintain his iniquity in
most of his possessions until the final and
unexpected destruction leaped at, him from
Sophia, Belgrade and Athens. That he is
now completely knocked out seetns beyond
all reasonable question. Even the impreg-
nable fortress of Janina is down, and
Adrianople appears doomed. The Syrian,
therefore, judges that the time is ripe, and
is presenting his demand for autonomy. All
things considered, it is a very moderate de-
mand, for the Turk has misgoverned Syria
in a fashion that might well justify the
population of the ancient country which
Beniiadad ruled in taking the Ottoman
person by the beard, and sticking the tem-
pered blade of Damascus into him at many
points. Outlying villages beyond the direct
trading routes were for years systematically
robbed by the Bedouin of the desert; and the
Turkish Pasha at Damascus, far from pro-
tecting the peaceful citizens who paid their
taxes, took a toll of all plunder. Even on
the main commercial highways caravans
were constantly attacked. But no Turkish
soldiery were ever sent to shoot the offend-
ers ; indeed, the troops themselves when on
the march were not above taking anything
that Allah threw in their way. What the
outcome of the new movement may be it
would be hard to predict, Syria was in the
dim past a strong and. Compact little
kingdom under its own native kings. Later,
on the bursting-up of Alexander’s empire,
it became itself an empire, stretching at its
utmost limit to the borders of India, and
having a magnificent Greek-built canital at
Antioch, on the Orontes. Modern Syria is
not the sort of place to lean to any particu-
lar greatness on the map. of the world, but it
has reasonable possibilities as a State if it
can get loose from the strangle-hold of the
Ottoman. All evidences seem to point to the
fact that very soon the Grand Turk will be
only a name and a red fez and a green flag
and the memory of a large, unwholesome
curse.
The Case for the Blind.
From one of the delegates:—
An inter-State conference of the blind was re-
cently held in Melbourne, and was attended by repre-
sentatives from South Auß., Vie., N. S. Wales and
Queensland. Many things were discussed and many
conclusions arrived at. Among the most important
of the latter were these; —
fl) That the national invalidity pension should
be paid as a right to all blind people over the age
of 16, regardless of their income.
(2) That all institutions for the education of
blind children should he nationalised, and placed
under the control of the Minister for Education in
the same way as ordinary State schools, and that the
education of blind children should be compulsory.
(3) That, in view Of the high cost of living, the
wages paid to blind Workers in the various indus-
trial blind institutions is not sufficient to ensure
the ordinary comforts of life.
(4) That more harmonious relations would exist
between the workers in the various institutions and
their respective Hoards of management if the workers
were allowed representation thereon.
The idea of the pension being paid as a right was
opposed bv several of the delegates on the ground
that too much Was beine asked of a Government
that was already doing a very generous thing in
paving a pensioh to the blind, subject to the pro-
visions of the Invalid and Old Age Pensions Act;
on the other hand, there are many who are not will-
ing to come forward and declare themselves paupers.
And there are many others who are not entitled
because they are receiving £1 a week, or a little
more, but are nevertheless not receiving enough to
buv ordinary comforts, and can never hope to earn
enough to do it. And it is really an unfair propo-
sition to tttpect men who had been used to th £ ,r
£3 a week, or more, when sighted, to live on
a week, or very little more, after losing their sight
through no fault of their own. Such men do not re-
ceive more than £1 a week when they « CJ “
the institutions, and in most cases they get less than
£1 • and they are exceptions who can average
30S a week even after spending three years
or more learning a trade. Cases are some-
times cited of blind men earning 30s. and
35s a week in institutions; but it would be
safe to say that the average for men alone is not a
cent more than 255., and if the women are included
it is much lower. So, even with the pension the
blind community would not have much chance for
luxuries For these reasons, and others, the con-
ference 'at last adopted the first resolution quoted
Sb As 6 may he’ inferred from the nature of the other
three resolutions, discussion revolved chieflv ™una
various institutions for the blind Conditions
are far from satisfactory at some of them—a tact

TWO and a half years ago a Labor Ministry
took office in N.S.W. pledged to uproot and
abolish the position of imported State Gov-
ernor. Last week a new imported State
Governor arrived >^ G " a^.J clC p°“ ie? and
Xtf leader ofVKTp arty. Naturally
‘S lsr r a\d >s %. w terxrs
hflitiJlf Me
nnVtv has done, or attempted, since it has
heei in power are tolerably numerous, but
verv few of them have had any special rela
YfJ t 0 the platform on which it was elected.
The abolition of the State Governorship was
ca cvf +hp strongest of election cries, find
there has been ample time to at least take
the preliminary steps towards getting rid of
what is merely an expensive encumbrance
To the State. One of the great points about
Federation was that it would, if given a
fair chance, greatly reduce the cost of l 1 ] 0 ?
Government It has never been given a fair
chance, and the Labor crowd has piled ™P
fbo sstnfp expenditure with even more enthu-
siasm than its opponents. Political salaries
have* been raised, the Legislative Council
has been added to. instead of destroyed, and
the State Governor is. if anything, more
firmlv shackled on the back of the taxpayer
than ever through the refusal to let the
Governor-General reign on in Macquarie
street McGoWEN and co. have quite a
number of questions to answer dodge or
hedge, but the most difficult of the lot is
that concerning their undertaking to get rid
of a State figurehead which is without mean-
ing, and can be no manner of use.
Thebe is somewhere or other a saying to
the effect that in leaving a city one looks
only at the steeples. Perhaps the departing
Chelmsford, of N.S.W., saw only the
funnels of Johnson’s locomotives when he
sent the Chief Commissioner a gushiDg
letter of thanks for the kindness and atten-
tion he had received while trundling round.
Such compliments are, no doubt, very plea-
sant; but the test of a good railway service
is not the extra attention it gives to wan-
dering personages. The more-nearly ideal
service would be the one that shocked and
astounded the roving cocked-hat by treating
him just as it treated ordinary paying pas-
sengers. If he objected or looked dazed, the
manager might courteously explain that they
were kept so busy attending thoroughly to
all branches of their business that they
really could not afford the time to fuss un-
necessarily over any one traveller, however
illustrious he might be. On the same day
that Chelmsford’s complimentary remarks
were printed, a cattle-owner told how his
unfortunate beasts were kept jammed in
their trucks without a drink from 3 p.m. on
Tuesday till 10 a.m. on Thursday—43 long
clanking hours, with intervals of clumsy,
knock-down shunting. The wafting of Gov-
ernors hither and thither is, of course, quite
a different thing from cattle shifting; but a
well-run railway service would earn praise
for both jobs, instead of bouquets for one
and bricks for the other.

From T. L. Mills (president of the Aus-
tralasian Country Press Association), who
recently depreciated the Australian: —
“Maorilander” in a recent Bulletin accurately
condensed the pith and point of the article I con-
tributed to the daily papers of M.L. regarding my
impressions of Australia. Does it really matter
whether I come from Mangaweka or Invercargill.
And is it fair to condemn a 20 minutes’ stay m
Australia when behind that 20 minutes there has
been a period of at least 20 years’ study of Aus-
tralian men and affairs through a professional
reading of Sydney and Melbourne newspapers and
by coming in contact, as a journalist, with repre-
sentative Australians during their visits to M.L..
As a matter of fact, I was in Sydney three weeks
and Melbourne a week, and what 1 published were
my own impressions, over my own name. After all,
it is my signed and sealed opinion, not hastily written,
against “Maorilartder’s” anonymous contradiction.
It would be a queer world if we had all to take
our politics from the other fellow, and either see
eye to eye with him or get it on the point from
“Maorilandpr’s” punch.
To which “Maorilander,” on the case being
put to him, replied he was glad T. L.
Mills admitted that he (“Maorilander )
had accurately condensed Mills’s article.
That condensation might be repeated thus:
(1) The Australian is a loafer.
(2) The Australian is a selfish hog.
(3) Thank God the Maorilander is not as the
Australian.
If after “20 years’ study of the Australian
and his affairs through professional reading
of the Commonwealth’s press” (to Say no-
thing of three weeks and a week
in Melbourne). Mills arrives at those signed
and sealed opinions, not hastily written,
the only question that arises is as to what
sort of implement Mills uses for investi-
gating purposes. A dose of Knibbs s Year
Book wouldn’t do it much harm, anyhow.
“Don Q.”: The immigrant had just got
outside an Australian square feed and
lighted his pipe. He had come out as a
“farm laborer,” and he explained where he
obtained his experience, and, incidentally,
something about the roast beef and the ale
that made old England’s name:
I was a shop assistant, living-in in one of the
big drapery stores in London. I had eight years of
it, and if I’d had any more I think I would have
died. The food was of the poorest and shoddiest
description, the staple being bread and margarine
and weak tea. Once a day we had a cut of meat ot
the commonest and cheapest variety, usually as tough
as indiarubber. Expenses were kept down to the very
lowest mark, and in my last store I don t think 1
ever rose from the table with a satisfied feeling.
There was alwavs a void somewhere, and I could never
understand how the margarine (on Sundays we had
butter) was laid on so evenly and thinly to the great
slabs of stale bread until one Sunday I got a chance
to visit the kitchen. A large, flat board, studded
with tacks, was laid on the table. Slices of bread
covered this board, the tacks keeping them from
slipping. The butter was melted in a pot and the
cook, with a large, soft brush, like a whitewash
brush, skimmed each slice of bread with the deft
hand of a master painter. Sunday mornings we
regularlv had ham and eggs (stale eggs and faulty,
damaged bacon cuts). Our working hours were
8 a.m. to 8.30 p.m. on five days; on Saturdays till
11.30 p.m. or midnight. Twenty minutes were
allowed for dinner, and 20 for tea. Pay for a
hours working week was, for men, £3O a year, phts
the board and lodging, and for the girls, £l5.
Buyers and beads of departments were allowed to
marrv and live outside the working premises, but
marriage on the part of a member of the rank and
file meant discharge. Too many of the girls made
extra money in the usual way, the barrack system
being a breeder of prostitution.
I said to this man, “I thought everybody
lived on lashings of good roast beef and
foaming tankards of ale in England.
he said, “that was in Queen Bess s time.
Probably it was a lie then, too. I m losing
faith in these music-hall songs.

A significant pointer to the British Gov-
ernment’s view of naval defence, so far as
the outlying Dominions of the Empire are
concerned, was given in London iast week,
during a banquet at which Maonland s Min
ister of Defence, James Allen, was the
principal guest Allen’s view <>hich un-
doubtedly is also the view of the Massey
Government) is turned away from the
scheme of subsidising the Fleet that is
bottled up in the North Sea, and towards
separate local units —the policy, in shoit,
adopted by Australia. Allen has made this
clear on several occasions since lie arrived
in England not long ago. Simultaneously
with Allen’s expressions in this regard, ex-
Tremier Joseph Ward, who is also in Lon-
don, has been flogging the local-unit sc beme
as if it were a proposition of Beelzebub.
The argument between the two politicians
has been loud and continuous, and has now
clearly ended in favor of Allen. At the
banquet referred to, Harcourt, Secretary of
State for the Colonies, went out of his way
to publiclv thank him for his advice in
matters of defence.” Ward was not men-
tioned Of course, the opinion of the British
Government has long been known ; neverthe-
less the pat on the back awarded to the
official Maorilander for ranging himself with
the Australian policy was welcome—and all
the more welcome because it could not have
been much more definite in the very unusual
circumstances.
Our Colonial Capital: The Paean of the
Little Australians.
Scores of horsemen gave a true colonial flavor to
the scene. —S. M. Herald.
The true colonial flavor of the scene
Makes us rejoice, and lifts our hearts with
Remembering all the things that mivht have
Australia -our demands denied.
A colony we are, and shall remain,
The hanger-on of something far away; _
Our nationhood is but a dream, and vam,
To stir one dust-cloud on the landscape grey.
We made a Commonwealth, we chose a flag,
We spoke in large, loud tones of destiny;
But that was only politicians brag,
And went for what it was with you and mA
We cannot grow beyond the thing we were.
Our infant swaddling bands are round us
Behohl, U the flag of Empire takes the aif
Where our colonial horsemen front the mil.
The place and scene are suited to our views.
A shabby village full of dust and heat
Is what! by mutual consent, we choose
To wear the title of our I ederal seat.
This fits exactly our colonial taste -
To have the Commonwealth’s selected crown
An offshoot of congested Sydney, h lac f e(^
A half-day’s journey from the bricks of town.
A nation might have had a larger dream,
\nd built a city vast and clean and fair,
It might have taken measures so extreme
That no importer in the spoils could sliar ,
But we colonials understand these things,
And we have pulled the wires with some
Hear far-shrilling trumpet how it sings!
The game goes better than we dared expect:
Hail to Canberra!' King O’Malley, hail!
O Brother this is, well and wisely done.
Merchant"to merchant shall repeat the tale
Of how the fateful village was begun.
If some new capital we needs „
This is the sort on which broad Fat may
TfiusTre’the Constitution’s words fulfilled,
And all things done in true colonial style.
N.S.W.
GEORGE STREET.
