T.A.”: The coming elections for the
Sydney Lmversity Senate, under the new
Act, threaten to be strenuous. There are
l seats to be filled, and the retiring sena-
tors number about 15. Of these, High
Coiwt Justices Griffith and Barton and four
otheis are not candidates. The remaining
nine have been nominated as “the old
brigade ticket, and are being run bv the
conservative crowd, backed up by the don
element. Another lot who don’t want to be
frozen out by a block vote of the right-
thinking element have formed themselves
into ‘the progressive party,” and will run a
ticket on their lonely. If the warring facul-
ties or Law, Medicine, Arts and Science
can coalesce and nominate a real progres-
sive bunch, they will have a chance to
create a live Senate and galvanise the
\ arsity. About 100 nominees are clamor-
tor selection on the progressive ticket
and the city is at present full of wild-eyed
persons engaged in tracking down every
person who has graduated at the ’Varsity
since it started. If The Bulletin knows of
any hiding places of forgotten graduates,
their addresses will oblige.
To a few people who hope to bask in the
social smiles of the vice-regal establishment,
tiiG ai rival of !\la s now Governor last week
maj 7 have been of some interest, but to the
greater number, who merely pay for an abso-
lutely useless show, the thing obviously did not
matter the hoot of a taxi’s motor-horn. The
fact is, the game is played out. The people
a re just as loyal or disloyal or indifferent
as they used to be, but no sane person can
see any use for seven separate and distinct
imported officials representing the King
Ihe mediaeval adjuncts of the seven petty
courts may still have joy for the lady with-
out ah h who wants to queen it over her
ess fortunate sisters who did not have the
luck to marry the rising butcher, but her
gladness scarcely seems to justify the ex-
pense. In addition to the actual cost of
his court, the State Governor is apt to cause
serious loss of time to busy State officials
who, on his account, neglect the job they are
paid to do. For instance, the time McGowen
spent on Friday last with the new Governor
might have been spent thinking whether he
would resign the leadership or still hang
on; or he might even have been resting-up
after the fatigue of hearing that the heresy
hunt was off.
Orient, ’ of some people concerned in the
Ghia case in Melbourne:—
The first Chinese Consul-General for Australia
who arrived only three or four years ago, was a dis-
tinguished elderly Chinaman ; but he was so deficient
in the English language and in his knowledge of
Australian ways, that he very quickly subsided and
ong 1 became locum-tenens. Then the permanent
man, Hwang, came along; and he was soon found
to be all there. Educated in America, he might
pass for a man of Florida or New Orleans, with
rather a strong American accent. Fond of public
speaking, Hwang has a singularly impulsive oratory
which 1 never remarked in another Oriental. Chia
his former secretary, is quite dissimilar. Hwang
has the American stamp; Chia has that of France
Xou might take him for a fiery little Spaniard or
Italian. Hwang soon found a defect in him; he was
deficient in knowledge of the Chinese language!
Ihis is a frequent complaint in Hong 1 Kong against
young Celestials who return from abroad. There-
fore, the Hong Kong University is established Its
language will be mainlv English, but Chinese is to
be kept up. On the other hand, the Hankow Univer-
sity will adopt Chinese as the language, while keep-
ing abreast of Hong Kong in every point of Western
culture. There are two important languages in
China, and so important that the Bible has to be
translated into each. These are the Wenli for the
intellectual toffs, and the Mandarin for the commoner
people who read. The Bible, more or less, is also
m 15 other dialects, each spoken by millions. Chia
also made a bungle by hoisting the Dragon flag at
the Melbourne Embassy for a victory by the Im-
perial Army over the Republicans. Hwang was away
at. the time, and it caused him much annoyance,
bringing down upon him the execration of almost
the whole Chinese community, crystallised in a fierce
letter from the Melbourne National League. How-
ever, this is not likely to have had anything to do
with Chia s dismissal, an order for which dulv came
from Pekin. After his marriage with Unie Russell
they.lived at the Sandringham Hotel, but she has
w returned to her father and mother at Brighton.

The Australasian tour of the recently-con-
stituted Hamilton-Plimmer comedy co. was
commenced at the Palace (Sydney) on Sat-
urday night with “Dr. Wake’s Patient,”
played last in the city by Herbert Flem-
ming’s co. some few years ago. The theatre
was packed, as was to be expected with
such names illuminating the cast as Airs.
Brough, Beatrice Day, Geo. S. Titheradge,
Arthur Styan and H. R. Roberts, to say
nothing of half a dozen other popular stag-
ers. Indeed, since the days when Brough
and Boucicault offered the public the rare
talent of their people, no more attractive
comedy combination has gathered under the
same play-house roof in Australia. And in
these days of eternal musical comedy piffle,
unmellowdrama and other side-shows, a re-
vival of the Brough and Boucicault tradi-
tions is commendable, and (or should be) a
payable undertaking. On the opening night
the co., or some of it, was playing at too high
a nerve tension, and occasionally brimmed
over into the shallows of artificiality; but
this defect was merely the defect of new
machinery running above normal, and will
disappear rapidly.
‘Dr. Wake’s Patient” is a bright, whole-
some comedy, well-put together, excepting
that Scene 1 of Act 2 (a waiting room for
patients at the doctor’s residence) is dragged
out too far. Still, much fine work was done
in the scene. Mrs. Brough, the superior
Countess, flabbergasted before the cold, sus-
picions of the Lord Bishop, who, return-
ing from the. consulting room to the waiting
room, finds his hat hung upon a nude Venus
was at her best. Harry Plimmer was the
“DR. WAKE’S PATIENT ,” AT
SYDNEY PALACE.
Bishop Plimmer discovers his hat on a statue
of Venus.
Farmer Wake (Arthur Styan) makes the
Earl feel very small.
Bishop, and he was a credit to the see.
Beatrice Day shone also in the scene; but
Bizette Parkes had bad luck. With two
such players as Mrs. Brough and Beatrice
Day to keep up with, she had the stage for
some time. Titheradge made an excellent
character out of the mutton-brained Earl
ot St. Olbyn ; so did Arthur Stvan, as the
ponderous, self-satisfied English county
cocky, likewise, Kate Towers, as his homely
mother-wife; similarly, Sydney Stirling, as
the likeable Willie-boy (“absolutely”) ; also,
H. R. Roberts, as the fashionable doctor,
though an occasional touch of nervousness
tricked him into a trifle of over-acting. There
is no need to go right through a cast which
was so generally useful to a successful re-
vival. The audience was well pleased, and
its appreciation was distributed with even
hand and hoof. As for floral tributes, they
were served out by the barrow-load.
“Cuss in Boots,” the J. C. Williamson
panto which arrived at Her Majesty’s,
•Sydney, in time for the Easter cow’s At
Home, has had the benefit of several weeks’
training in Melbourne, and may, therefore,
be regarded as a finished product. As usual,
the simple old nursery story is only allowed
to emerge occasionally from a welter of
turns, some of them good. Its only uses, in
fact, are that it gives a homely label'to a long
evening’s variety entertainment, and affords
one man a chance of cleverly imitating a
cat. Violet Loraine, the tall principal
girl-boy, has the right figure, a robust sing-
ing voice, and so much youthful vim that
she consistently overacts. However, modern
pantomime being a thoroughly unnatural
sort of excrescence, overacting may be con-
sidered necessary. George Miller, the
Dame of the show, is less noticeable than
some others Australia has known. Among
his virtues, he is -not impossibly agile, and
he rarely assault the other panto people
with his umbrella; in the manner of the
Stone Age squaw. Byplay, seems to be this
earnest performer’s strong shit, and he is
often quaintly absurd without ever being
uproarious. O. E. Lennon, the Cat, puts
in a hot and strenuous evening, and holds
up the story when the rest of the vast com-
pany have forgotten it. Stewart Garner,
the Ogre, suggests Kink O’Malley dipped

in wine. Alice Russon (the Lost Prin-
cess) and Dorothy Firmin (the Fairy
Queen) are both gracefully colorless. “Pip”
Powell, who is King GrqhbUs, works very
hard for his laughs, and occasionally gets

■ are smart interludes, but, considered as

Leslie too often starts out with the intention
of composing a dance, and ends up by com-
piling a physical culture demonstration.
Queen Felina’s fairies once get fairly jigging
together, and convert the stage into a grove
of waving limbs and draperies; but the
dancing otherwise is neither very frequent
nor very noticeable. The last scene of Act
I. is a golden testimonial to the art of
W. R. Coleman and the lighting expert.
“At the Ogre’s Gates,” a Coleman back-
cloth representing walls and towers amidst
mountains, is another fine bit of painting.
The most joyous turn of the evening is the
burlesque acrobatic act of Collins and
Hart. The Dame, Leslie and Powell also
com hi "e in a knock-abqut railway-station
turn that is very ’ funny in spasms. Many
of the gags that were moderately fresh when
the show started are now stricken with age.
Others that are confidently wheeled out have
been in bath chairs for years. Taken alto-
gether, the costuming is not as fine as in
some past pantos, notably Nellie Stewart’s
glittering production of “Cinderella,” an
event of years ago; the same remark ap-
plies more or less to the marching and
music; and there is a smaller allowance
than usual of the jokes and jingles that
carry beyond the theatre doors.

The change of residence from Her
Majesty’s to the neighboring Royal has not
taken any of the glitter out of “The Sun-
shine Girl,” or thinned her train of Sydney
admirers. Her radiant smile and the catchy
tunes that move her nether limbs are now
drawing the crowd to another street, that’s
all.

Wirths opened on Saturday in Belmore
Markets, Sydney, to their usual big business
“Every turn a thrill” is the motto of the
brethren, and their show lives right up to it.
That large piece of ungainly African flesh!
Lizzie, the hippopotamus, and a mob of per-
fectly respectable elephants represented the
“wild” element in the circus programme - but
the flying feats of Alfredo and the Codonas,
and the really marvellous display of the
pretty Mexican girl, La Belle Victoria, on
the longest and slackest of wires, were wild
enough for the jungles of th<f Milky Way.
Hillary Long is a remarkable person who
balances upside down, on swinging and
spinning trapezes, walks down several steps,
using his head only for locomotion, and does
a long slide down a steep wire balanced upon
his astounding cranium. The top-notch sensa-
tion is, however, Mrs. Garcia’s hair-raising
ride in a car which turns triple somersaults
in the air without smashing the ladv. Thp
whole show,_ even to the decoration of the
entrance with the hindouarters of grey
ponies, is full of novelty, and if someone
■would drop an elephant or something on the
chief clown all would be well.
After all the hurry and excitement of
the Religious Editor, the sacred cause of
education suffered a setback at the National
Amphitheatre (Sydney) last week. It was
not until late in the week that the proud
\an Camp, new-risen from his sick-bed,
was able to conduct his intelligent swine
on to the stage and demonstrate what cul-
ture will do for hogs. The great advantage
of tutoring bacon in the raw is that, when
it has acquired a full know-ledge of the ad-
vantages of constitutional government and
modern explosives, it does not start to throw
the latter at you in order to gain the former.
The Clardy boys, the Lessos, Nellie Maher,
Seven Heraldos, Carlton Cooper and Eva
Mudge help to make the bill attractive.
“Oil and Water,” a dance-drama, has been
causing the Crystal Palace to do some extra
advertising this week. The “water” of the
yarn is a young man who habitually takes a
front stall, and the “oil” is a “classic” dancer
on whom the young man has his eye—and
there is a lot for the eye to be on, the lady,
as remarked before, being of the “classic”
species. Eventually he marries her; but
after a while she finds washing-up slow-,
escapes by the side door and gets back to the

* * * *
The Royal, vacated by the Asches, is
fasting for the moment. The Vic. Festival
Choir will have the stage on Good Friday,
and on Saturday the delights of “Mile-
stones” are to be revealed to Melbourne for
the first time. Already everybody seems to
have heard of “Milestones” and its recent
success in Sydney. Moreover, the name of
old friend Julius Knight is freely and
affectionately mentioned in connection with
the coming event.
** * *
Nothing has occurred to interrupt or
aggravate the financial success of “The Monk
and the Woman” at the Princess’s. The
local Orange lodges might very well have
written to the papers protesting against the
anti-Protestant sentiment of Marlow’s show,
but business is progressing favorably without
the assistance of an advt. from Brother
Snowball. Miss Elwyn Harvey is now the
lady who lodges in the monkery, and makes
goo-goo eyes at Brother Paul, the previous
vision having gone to Sydney to participate
in the joys and sorrows of “From Convent
to Throne;” By the way, a musical even-
ing, under Faulkner Smith’s direction, will
be given at the Princess’s on Good Friday.
* * -s *
At the King’s they are preparing for a
thrillsome production of “The Evil Men Do”
on Saturday night. The sub-title of this
Anglo-American melodrama —-“The Choir
Boys of St. Mary’s”—reminds one that it
was written by the author of “The Fatal
Wedding.”
** * *
The Yankee musical scream that started
at Melbourne Bijou last Saturday is neither
as piercing as a gimlet nor as infectiously
hilarious as Joe Cook standing on his dig-
nity ; but, like the sword-thrust that hap-
pened to Mercutio, it will suffice. The
prices are popular and the entertainment
worth the money. The stout comedian gets
laughs by being kicked on his inflated
stomach, " and that jest grows monotonous
after a while. Still, the kicker, one Bert
Le Blanc, is otherwise a legitimate source
of merriment, and he shares the principal
honors of the uproar with Miss Eugenie Le
Blanc, an eccentric little performer who re-
veals pale blue bloomers when she lifts her
skirts. Some of the characterisations in
“The Grafters” suggest _ Mrs. Wiggs;
some of the music is melodious in its rag-
time swing; and some of the fooling has
an air of novelty. Moreover, the work of
the supernumerary golfers and others who
supply the chorus, ranging from smart to
so-so, seems likely to improve as the : season
advances. With commendable disregard
for conventionality. “The Grafters” announce
two performances on Good Friday.
* * * *
The bill at the Opera House this Easter
time is long and level. Miss Dot-de-Vine at
the piano, and Keldine and Speed on bicy-
cles are the latest newcomers. Among the
previously mentioned are Staley and Bir-
becks, the Maxini Bros, and their dawg, the
Patty-Franks, the Concertina Prince, and
juggler Mozetto ; also George Bryant and co.,
who are about to go away. Claire Romaine
has already departed.
** * *
“When the Mask Falls” was first favorite
at West’s Melbourne house last week. Re-
vealing a long story of trouble and self-sacri-
fice with a gcheming bank manager as its
principal profligate, the film was curiously
interesting in re-introducing Asta Nielsen,
the fine Danish actress who is evidently
quite young and exceedingly A
very painful subject was shown in A Cura-
ble Disease,” a sort of health-lecture in
flickerdrama that might enliven the tenants
of a consumptive sanatorium. The Lady
Doctor,” the tale of a rich uncle who cut off
his nephew with the customary means to buy
four drinks in the front bar, was a neat vari-
ation of an old theme ; while ihe Fnend of
Birds” was a long strip of chirruping life
and beautiful scenery artificially colored and
most soothing to the eye.

During the last few days Arctic pictures
have been giving cold comfort to the numer-
ous supporters of the P ran Lyric (Vic.).
The frigid conditions set in with the screen-
ing of some notable Alaskan views, followed
by glimpses of Siberia, all explained by
young John Brunton, who spruiks anent the
scenery in language which he might have
borrowed from Kink O Malley. The film ,
however, were very good, as were also a
bunch of fictions ranging from the bustling
drama of “The Smouldering Spark to the
earlv-morning comedy of Time F lies, and
including “Kissing Kate,” a hugger-mugger
item of shrieking imbecility.

“The Mad Reporter”: A meeting of the Prima
Donnas’ Husbands’ Union was held at the Trades
Hall last night. Prince Adagio, who presided, said
the union had come to the parting of the ways.
(“Bravo!” and “Hear, hear!”) The managers must
be told that, nolens volens, they must agree with
the views expressed by the recent deputation of hus-
bands of prima donnas. The? demanded only their
rights. (“Hear, hear!” “BraVissimo!”) They were
necessary to the success of the opera-stage and the
concert-platform, because they were indispensable to
the happiness of the ladies on whom opera-stage and
concert-platform depended. (Great enthusiasm.)
These managers —(cries of “Ah, Briganti! “lief . )
—now demanded that the husband of a prima
donna should pay for his own travelling. (“Shame! )
Yet they knew that if husbands were left behind,
many a top note would suffer. He might allude to
the sufferings of Baron Havana de Muria, who,
despite his tears (sensation) —yes, tears I—was
forced to agree to his wife receiving her own salary,
when, indeed, he had been his wife s treasurer tor
many years, in happier climes —alas-—than these.
(Cheers.) Baron Havana de Muria in a pathetic
speech moved the following resolution: That unless
the managers of concerts and operas m Australia
agree to pay to the husband of a prima donna an
amount equal and in addition to the full salary of
such prima donna, together with transport charges,
we, the executive of the Prima Donnas Husbands
Union, give notice that we declare strike of all
leading ladies, and order it to be maintained until
the dispute has been settled in full. The motion
was seconded by Count Con Passion?, and carried
amidst enthusiasm. The strike begins as from to;
dav, and it is anticipated that the Chorus Singers
Union will join immediately. The executive of t
Incapable Bassoon Players’ Union has declared in
sympathy with the strike, and there is . dtle doubt
that all the affiliated noise merchants will refuse to
blow after this week unless the demand is granted.

“O’Blin”: Last year I blessed the Melbourne
Repertory Club with a hard-earned guinea, and now
I am questioning the wisdom of the investment. I
have a grievance against the club on account of its
furtive cultivation of intellectual swank. Ostensibly
designed to provide a cash stimulus to the Rep.
Theatre business, the club has become a refuge for
high-brows, lone ’Varsity professors and academical
scholars of huge mental displacement. These people
congregate at intervals at the Austral Salon, and over
a bun and a sip of coffee discuss soothing subjects like
“The Psychology of the Frog According to Aristo-
phanes” and “The Botanical Significance of Titania’s
Bower.” They manage to amuse themselves, but
to me, an intrusive stranger, they are inveterate
bores, creating such an atmosphere of classicism
that I feel I am breathing the sacred dust of Euri-
pides, or some similarly-deceased Dago, and ought
to apologise for my impertinence. This year this
brilliant, highly-tutored gang are going to plough
through a syllabus of lectures which, in addition to
including talks on matters having some conceivable
connection with the Repertory Theatre, will embrace
remarks on “The Women of Shakspeare,” the results
of o’Dowd’s researches on “Zenobia’s Counsellor,” and
“Fate in Shakspeare,” for which last Archibald
Strong holds an oratorical contract. Apart from the
facts that Strong is not an entertaining talker, and
is a writer who makes his prose a sepulchre for the
re-interment of long defunct scribes, what bearing
has his discourse on the repertory movement? It is
this shrouding of the repertory idea in the fogs of
scholasticism that drives me to charge the club with
the aforesaid swank. Really there is too much
swank, and swank founded on a drear and soul-dead-
ening Culchaw!
“Geofred”: “Hamer’s” par. re the Actors’ Union
(B. 6/3/’l3) gives one the idea that Broken Hill
and the other “industrial centres” are'biting off
their nose to spite their face, if they are really
adopting the attitude “Hamer” says they are with
regard to union actors. According to the showing of
the Actors’ Union itself, when applying for registra-
tion, its officers belong to practically every trade
except that of the actor. Newspaper scribes and
clerks (take its president and secretary, for in-
stance), plumbers, carpenters, etc., comprise the
committee. It is not likely genuine “actors” are
going to be ruled by this heterogeneous crowd —no
more likely than that a clerk would consent to have
his union affairs ruled by a wharf laborer and a
navvy. This writer believes in unionism, and that
is just why, to him, the idea of calling this an
actors’ union reeks of absurdity. It looks, there-
fore, that the Barrier Labor Federation has two
alternatives —-(1) to banish all but union actors and
Gertie Brown (tvpiste) in the rhles of Romeo and
pay 3s. or so to see Bill Smith (plumber) and
Juliet: or (2) to get some horse-sense, and patro-
nise the genuine show when it comes along, thus
eventually assisting to found k real actors’ union,
comprised of actors and not has-beens and camel
drivers.
Jan Sen, the American conjurer, has fixed up to
make a tour of the “smalls” of Australia, commenc-
ing in Bendigo at Easter. Frank Talbot (who, as
a partner in the now defunct Portus and Talbot
co., imported Jansen, and made much money with
him in Sydney and elsewhere) is in charge, and part
of the appurtenances is a brass band calculated to
shake up even Hobart if it gets there.
M. P. Adams, Australian athlete and frequent
Bulletin contributor, who went with the Olympians
as far as England, blew back to Sydney this week.
Adams came as advance agent for the “Ireland To-
Day” picture and concert co., of which a good deal
has already been heard from Melbourne and Adelaide,
including numerous breathless messages concerning
sworn-to samples of the soil of all the ancient king-
doms gathered rnostlv by Adams’s own hand, and
now available for the feet of the faithful to reverently
tread upon.
“Private Boxer”: Vive Leslie Victor! He is one
of the principals who did not have the advantage of
seeing either “Bella Donna” or “Milestones” in
London, and yet he has managed to make a big
(perhaps the biggest) hit in both plays. Which
proves, if it proves anything, that a brainy actor
can give»a good show without being a servile copy
of the original. Indeed, it is perhaps better for
himself and for his Art not to have seen the original.
“0.K.”: Satisfactory to find local mummer talent
now getting a fair chance in comedy sketches at the
Rickards houses, where hitherto sketch artists ha-e
almost invariably been imported couples—a sprightly
male with an amateurish lady, or vice versa. a
sober, stodgv little comedy called “Pot Luck,” the
acting of George Brvant, Ada Lawrence and Harold
Reeve is particularly good. No exaggeration or
music-hall coloring about it! The three principals
are native-born, George Bryant being the only mem-
ber of the co. who has seen much foreign service. It
is generally known that he was imported from
America by the Williamson firm a few years ago : but
it is generally unknown that he was one of William-
son, Garner and Musgrove’s stock actors in the
early ’Bo’s. Ada Lawrence has rarely had a show in
Sydney or Melbourne, and not one person in a hun-
dred who sees her at the Opera House (Melb.) these
nights knows that she is an experienced Australian
actress who can play the tearful heroine in Camille
and other emotional dramas with more force than
the average imported leading lady. Bryant and
Ada Lawrence, as Bill Sikes and Nancy, should be
able to make any metropolitan audience sit up.
Whv not relieve the frivolity of a vaudeville show
with 15 minutes of Dickens horrordrama?
“Melb”: Julius Knight, in “Milestones,” re-
appears at the Melbourne Theatre Royal after 20
years’ absence from that house. He came there with
the Clifton Alderson and Laura Villiers co., opening,
as second man, in “The Dancing Girl. Then he
ripened in Britain, and returned to Australia as star,
with the “Prisoner of Zenda,” “Sign of the Cross,
“Two Little Vagabonds,” etc.

The demise is chronicled of Geo. Hamil-
ton, of Capertee (N.S.W.), who, it is
claimed, was 110 years old, having, it is
alleged, been born on board the Silver Queen
in 1802 in Sydney Harbor. Unfortunately
there was not a registry office in Sydney in
1802, so official proofs of the exact antiquity
of Hamilton are missing ; and even the Oldest
Inhabitant won’t swear to the age of anybody
over 105.
Sydney Worker has gone mad with admira-
tion for the Canberra capital site, and last
week remarked hilariously that at the time
of the official dedication ceremony, six rivers
on the site were in flood, and sufficient
water was passing down the Cotter to fill
the ornamental lake in 24 hours. It would
have been a ghastly look-out for the place
if its ditches hadn’t been in flood, consider-
ing that the State had just been swept by
the wildest storm for years. The question
is not how much water is on the site after
a torrential downpour—every place has
water then—but how much there will be
half-way through a dry summer. Even
Broken Hill and Booligal have their floods ;
the trouble is that it is such a long time
between floods, and while they are waiting
sun and wind are capable of licking up a
solid 10ft. or 12ft. of the stored moisture in
a single summer.
The story of a tragedy
The spirit of White Australia is further confirmed
in its anti-Mongolian prejudice by £he story of T.
J. Chia, now in chokee in Melbourne on a charge of
attempting to murder the Chinese Consul. Chia
stood for the Asiatic bounder in an advanced state
of Europeanisation. Not that he ever stood still
for any length of time. He dodged about like a
parched pea in a fryingpan—a shallow little pre-
tender, bowing and fussing over the dear, beautiful
ladies ; raising his shiny belltopper and swishing the
tails of his long frock-coat; making always a pre-
tence of having profitable business to attend to,
when he was merely engaged in raising the wind
necessary for restaurant lunches, teas, dinners, sup-
pers and other incidents of a bounder’s brief career.
To most Melbourne men who knew him, or of
him, T. J. Chia was an unpleasant joke. A number
of women and girls took him seriously and thought
him a “charming personality.” The son of a
wealthy Chinese merchant (who had about 20 other
children), this particular Chia was educated in Eng-
land. where he failed to thoroughly master the lan-
guage, whilst contriving: to forget, it seems, a good
deal of his native Chinese. Ilis thin veneer of
scholarship and egregious “cheek” served only to
Thomas Jones Chia.
carry him into minor public
positions, which he failed to
keep. He claimed to have
been headmaster of a “Per-
petual” College at Chang
Choo, and Professor of English
for a year at Pekin Univer-
sity—a very unlikely thing for
him to have been, seeing that
his knowledge of both lan-
guages is defective. He was
probably a secretary for a time
to (he Chinese Commissioner
in Paris, as he stated, and he
certainly was secretary to the
Chinese Consulate in Mel-
bourne. Thus, he came
to marry a very nice girl.
Miss Eunie Russell, a few
weeks ago. As a wooer, Chia
was allee same as the typical
Eulopean adventurer, with one
point of difference. Undoubt-
edly he adored his girl,
Probably he infused his
adoration with the fumes of
opium; certainly he lived in
a dream, taking no thought
for the morrow, save as re-
gards the procuring of suffi-
cient ready-money to keep
his credit going. Months
before his marriage he lost his
billet at the Consulate because
his translation of Chinese
correspondence was unsatis-
factory, but his dismissal was
missprinted as a “resigna-
tion” on account of the death
of his father, the merchant.
Previously the reckless little
“squib” had been borrowing
money from his compatriots
in Melbourne, and now, on
the strength of his late
father’s alleged wealth, he
posed as the inheritor of a
fine fortune, and bounded more
than ever.
The bride-elect and her parents, and a few '‘repre-
sentative citizens” who ought to have known better,
were supreme in their ignorance of Chia’s real Posi-
tion. Everybody else who happened to be in the way
to hear about the young man knew that he was
owing money, buying jewellery and things on credit,
and rushing headlong to the traditional devil in
faultless Eulopean attire. Unfortunately for the
family he married into, the exposure of T. J. Chja’s
social fraudulency ("his father, apparently, having cut
him off with a Chinese shilling owing to his infatua-
tion for an Australian) was delayed by a windfall
of ready cash. After his father’s death he got a
loan of £l2OO from a brother in China for the
purpose of “going into business.” The fact has not
been published hitherto. This money he sDlashed
about in the same old way preliminary to ‘his mar-
riage, and soon after his marriage (last January)
he was un to his Mongolian eyes in financial diffi-
culties. The game of deception was all UP. The
Secretary of the External Affairs Department was
calling upon T. J. Chia to show cause why he should
not be deported to China, having neither official
position nor commercial purpose in this country.
His creditors had started to issue writs against
him, and his nominal reason for calling upon
the Chinese Consul with a revolver in his pocket was
to ask that hard, dry Celestial gent, to givp him
authority to say he was sfill connected with the
o<fice. This reanest the Celestial gentleman natur-
ally refused, and then Chia, so the Consul alleges,
fired the shots that burst his bubble reputation.
The local bounder who woos and wins a girl under
false pretences—incidentally spreading a rumor that
he has inherited a fortune from a relative, as Chia
did —is a common enough person. There are hun-
dreds of him even in this sparsely populated country.
But Thomas Jones Chia is Australia’s first experience
of the Fuloneanised Chow bounder, who maddens
his imagination, probably with onium. He is. in-
deed, a shocking example of the length to which a
nfee white girl will go in letting herself be deceived.
Whatever charm this preposterous little alien pos-
sessed for a section of the sex lav in his abnormality.
Now that he is charged with attempted murder, the
kindest thing his former friends can do for him is to
testify to the lunatically foolish things he did.
SPITE, NO DOUBT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Miss Violet Loraine, the principal boy of “Puss
in Boots, confesses to being greatly surprised at the
deep interest taken in stage artists by the public
generally. “It is wonderful,” says Miss Loraine,
how stories and rumors do get about concerning
theatrical people. Somebody wrote and asked me
recently if,” etc., etc.— Melb. press par.
’Tis very strange, it is indeed,
How facts are ushered into print,
Concerning ladies playing lead—•
In fact, the whole theatric breed —
Of paragraphs there is no stint
About the soubrette’s bringing up,
Her parrot or her poodle pup.
By all the common folk ’tis known
What Miss Smiqute could do at six,
What noble traits by her were shown
When thieves broke in her pearls to bone.
Or of her roguish private tricks,
How oft she’d trod the bridal path,
And when and how she takes a bath.
How sad it is the actress fair
Cannot repose in privacy
That butchers may enjoy ! Where’er
She goes the women stand and stare.
To hide her charmed identity
She always wears the simplest clo’es,
And stealthily she comes and goes.
The simple little acts of grace
That she performs, her stockings pink—
They all get mention. Rags find space
To praise her diamonds and lace.
How is it done? We almost think
Her managers employ a wight
Who spreads these items just for spite.

“Termite” : Fashion’s decrees have not in
the past troubled Darwin residents much —
at least, not the men folk. But things are
changing. After the arrival of Dr. “Jock”
Gilruth, an edict went forth that for Qovt.
House evening functions the correct thing
in future was to be a white hybrid dinner
jacket, an inartistic, inelegant offence, de-
signed to take the place of the time-honored,
neat, comfortable mess-jacket, which had
been in vogue here and in many other tropi-
cal places. Government Hqjjse habitues fell
into line obediently, and the Chinese tailors
reaped a harvest. But we are not finished with
innovations yet. Since his arrival church-
going has not troubled his Ex. over-much;
but last Sunday evening he graced with his
presence the local Methodist Church, where
Dr. Bevan was billed to hold a memorial
service in honor of the heroes of the Scott
expedition. The horse doctor, on this occa-
sion, showed his respect for the place and
the occasion by attending, accompanied by
his secretary, coatless. This naturally set
Port Darwin thinking and talking, and there
is much speculation as to whether “Jock’s”
example is to be taken as a fashion hint.
There is no reason, of course, why a man
shouldn’t go to church in his shirt sleeves,
especially if the weather be on the warm
side; but one shudders to think what would
happen to anyone who would bowl up to

wet season. If Josiah Thomas has any re-
gard for appearances, the sooner he sends
along that steam laundry the better.
Bird’s-eye view of a great occasion
The State entry of Governor Strickland and his
dame into Sydney was one of the most depressing
spectacles 1 ever witnessed. Wear the Governors
office, in Macquarie-street, a large herd of self-con-
scious policemen worried and shepherded a thin
crowd of women, children and street loungers, and
kept carthorses out of the block for fear they would
chew the greenery off the triumphal arch, the un-
necessary obstruction was situated in front of Gov-
ernment House gates, through which, in the old
State days, his newly-arrived Ex. used to drive like
a king or a circus. Probably departmental force of
habit placed it in the old familiar spot. A detach-
ment of the Australian Artillery with a painfully
scratch band clashed along, came to a halt, stood
easy and wearily mopped its collective brow. The
self-conscious policemen shooed the mostly-feminine
crowd back to the kerbstone. There was plenty of
room for everyone; but something had to be done
to fill in time. The crowd-herders were all large,
meaty men, who would have shown up to better
advantage if there had been a surging holiday mob
to hustle, and shove and threaten. The absence of
the surging mob put them at a great disadvantage.
There was nothing to do but order one another about,
take elaborate precautions against impossible stam-
pedes and interfere with scornful carters. Three utterly
miserable policemen stood guard over a patch of
green grass, upon which nobody wanted to trample.
These large, idle men, with white-gloved hands, had
a hunted, apprehensive look. They seemed to be
listening for a fretful voice in the crowd to bark.
The head of the procession appeared at last; it con-
sisted of more policemen—on horseback this time
and bearing naked swords. Behind them trundled
the Gaud Mayor in all his ridiculous millinery, but
without any gas strike shovel. A beadle, or some-
thing, with stripes on its back, sat on the box.
Behind came a stream of unimpressive two-crock
landaus, laden with men who were wearing bell-
toppers and frock coats, and couldn’t forget it. The
crowd knew they were aldermen, because a nuisance
inspector was perched on every box-seat alongside
the grief-stricken driver. The cortege climbed the
hill almost without a sound. A motor, containing
four silent, medalled officers, packed closely, face to
face, whirred past like a clockwork toy. Followed
another funereal horse-chariot, bearing four speechless
naval officers. Then their Excellencies —she, flushed
and excited, bowing deeply and continuously to glum
curiosity; he, gravely saluting stout policemen.
One felt suddenly and overwhelmingly sorry for
them. They needed the surging mob even more than
the unemployed policemen. The dreadful procession
of bell-toppered politicians, sorrowfully a-wheel in
pairs, finally drove me away. Round the corner
they were cheering, thinly and weakly, and the band
was battering out “Gorsave.” Hurrying down the
processional line, I caught an encouraging glimpse
of scarlet, and paused to look into two elderly closed
carriages, about the size of large packing-cases.
They were tightly stuffed with bewigged Judges, who
had been torn from their law jobs and wheeled
into the garish light of day for the amusement of
the crowd. They reminded me that the last person
I saw in a full-bottomed wig was Nell Stewart in
“Old Drury.” Away at the halting tail-end of the
cortege came the Church with its gaiters and spec-
tacles, and the quaintly absurd collars and clothes
that button up the back. Behind the Church, and
last of all, trundled —an empty two-crock landau.
I don’t know what it was supposed to renresent.
Perhaps it was intended for the use of the 24ft. goat-
swallowing python that used to grace Sydney. Last
Friday’s performance has convinced me d) that the
breed of carriage horses is extinct, (2) that the
breed of fine, old wooden-faced coachmen is extinct,
(3) that the workinar time of policemen, Judges,
etc., is often wasted to no purpose, (4) that the pro-
cessional instinct is dead in Australia, and (5) that
State Governors would be happier if they were
allowed to take up their duties with less parish
pomp. Personally, I would rather do a day’s hard
work than salute and bow to blank indifference and
idle curiosity. State Governors henceforward should
demand a guarantee that there will be a surging
mob to cheer them madly, and that the horseflesh is
horseflesh, not mere crockery.
The papers of virtuous John Bull and
wife have been giving a lot of prominence
to what is described as Maoriland’s suicide.
The business is serious enough, for the
birthrate per 1000 married women steadily
declined from 337 in 1878 to 209 in 1911.
Still, things are not quite as black as they
are painted, for while
the births per 1000 of
population fell from
35 in 1882 to 25 in
1899, there has since
then been an occa-
sional rise, and the
figures are certainly
not getting any smal-
ler. Also, owing to
the very low death
rate, the natural in-
crease per 1000 of
population is good,
and shows 17.61
against the Common-
wealth’s 15.02 and
England’s 10.75. So
if Maoriland is sui-
ciding everybody else
seems to be doing the
same, and the land of
John and Johanna
Rull is not far from
the forefront of the
hari-kari.
Melbourne, under Gaud Mayor Hennessy,
is doing things. It has abolished the news-
vendors’ stalls and most of the fruit-hawkers’
barrows, and is getting into the way of toss-
ing its rubbish into tidy wire baskets; also,
it lately fined a vociferous small newsboy for
calling his wares too loudlv. But the dust
fiend still rages, and there is no word about
suppressing the shrieking axle-brake, or the
barking mongrel, or the weird and varie-
gated toot of the unsober motor-horn, or the
rats —quadruned—which continue to pervade
the gutters from 11 p.m. to 4 a.m. So there
is still a large opening for Hennessy to bog
into.
HIS MEANS OF SUPPORT.
CHOLLY : “I say, Job kins, you never seem to do any work or pay any debts. What do
you LIVE on? ”
JOBKINS : “Fat of the land, deah boy—fat of the land."
If Sydney Evening News is right, Strick-
land, the Ma State’s latest cocked hat,
would make an ideal harbor master or direc-
tor of the ocean. When he set out from
Melbourne in the Cooma, “the passage of
the Rip was scarcely noticeable, and there-
after the sea behaved with remarkable gen-
tleness.” But Strickland evidently tempers
justice with kindness, for, directly he had
landed at Farm Cove, Sydney, and his
weight was off the respectful brine, “the
waters of the harbor danced in wanton blue-
ness.” The loyal wavelets, in fact, leaped
with all the careful abandon of the amateur
ballet in the suburban operatic show.
Melbourne has produced the youngest
Australian stowaway so far. Mrs. David-
son, of Richmond, took her little daughter
Zoe, aged six, by steamer from Port Mel-
bourne to Williamstown a fortnight back.
Zoe had not been on the sea before, and it
was very rough, yet she was in great exul-
tation. A day or two afterwards, instead
of going to school, she went off four miles
to Port Melbourne, There she clambered
up the ladder of the Otway, and hid some-
where aboard. The steamer left for Ade-
laide, and was outside the heads when Zoe
bobbed up on the captain’s bridge, munching
an apple. The wireless was set to work,
and in a couple of hours the mystery of the
remarkable young adventurer was solved.
A bunch of young Mormons from Utah,
experts in the sugar-beet industry, have
given the Vic. authorities to understand
that they desire to settle at Maffra; but the
powers, _ having considered the matter in
connection with the connubial effusiveness
of Mormons generally, have decided that
Maffra is too far off their beat, and the hopes
of the Gippsland girls are dashed once more.

The prose poems heaved off of late by the
daily papers in describing the Yassberra
Dry Capital site would have been beautiful
things with a little more sub-editing. As it
was, an occasional harsh note rasped across
the symphony. For instance :
It is doubtful whether so many people have ever
had such a dusty ride. The dustiness really added
to one of the peculiar characteristics of the far-
reaching plains which are enclosed in the city area,
for it is only when it is raining that the country
hereabouts is free from dust.—Sydney Sun
(12/3/’l3). 3
It was a perfect day, absolutely ideal for the occa-
sion. Fine four-horse drags swung along in the
wake of the motors, passing, in clouds of red dust
scores of less imposing equipages As the troops
marched past they continued on to the Duntroon-
road, and cantered back in a cloud of dust to the
camp.—S. M. Herald (13/3/’l3).
The water supply may be uncertain; but
the dust is dead-sure.

THE ANGEL'S PREROGATIVE.
“ Shame, young man! You were never made
to fly till you were dead."
“ALSO RAN.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Conquered at last, I yield my hand.
But even as your lips meet mine,
Tremulous twilight hides the band
Of predecessors, half-divine,
Who tiptoed to my heart, and proved
Its door was locked to all but you,
Who halted lightly as you roved,
Devised a dream, and made it true,
Dearly beloved!
Yet, though I rest in dear content
Within the arm that clove the way
To win me, one last thought is spent
On yesterday—ah, yesterday !
And, when I kiss, on lips removed
I ponder still, and lightly sigh
Regret for something still unproved—
The love of those who said good-bye,
Nearly beloved !

Chief Justice Madden (Vic.), in lately
sentencing a 32-year-old sinner to six months’
hard labor, remarked : “Some men there are
who sit like crows on a rail, waiting for
their chance to take advantage of the
weaker creature as soon as she reaches the
age of sixteen.” Madden, C.J., might have
also observed that some judges there are
who sit like Cyclops on seduction cases,
taking a one-eyed view of the situation.
The writer once heard a theatrical man
speak of the giddy ballet damsels at a theatre
where he was employed as “a lot of young
minxes comparing notes as to how many
days they had to go before reaching the age
of consent.” In their case, anyway, the
weaker creatures offered cordial invitations
to any crows that might have been waiting
around.
Ex-N. S. Wales Governor Chelmsford got
the bull’s-eye at the farewell feed; —
Are we in Australia giving sufficient prizes for
excellence? Is it not a fact that every year there
are many young men leaving Australia because there
are greater inducements in other parts of the world?
Are we doing all that we can to give an incentive to
our artists? And so I think also with regard to
engineers. It is in human nature to look for reward,
and it is good business, I say, on the part of those
in authority to induce the very best to stay.
Why, we import even our State Governors!
• * * *
The Melbourne public learnt lately with
surprise, and Wowserdom with sorrow, that
the Victorian Government owns a pub—the
Yarra Bank Hotel, just back of the wharves
on the south side of Queen’s Bridge. The
site was originally granted to Robert J. Hil-
liard, who ran a ferry across the river in the
early ’7o’s. He built the hostelry, and when
his lease reverted to the Crown the latter
leased it in 1905 to the Castlemaine brewery
for 21 years at £l5O a year. The brewery
sub-let to the present licensee, and now the
Reduction Board is considering the advisa-
bility of silencing the pump, the establish-
ment being described as “old, dirty and dilap-
idated.” Whether the licensee, the lessee or
the lessor was responsible for this condition
didn’t transpire. Anyhow, if the Govern-
ment wishes to continue in the trade, there
is now a fine chance to transfer its energies
from the Yarra Bank to that long-promised
State hotel at Wonthaggi.

N.S.W. Chief Judge in Equity was called
upon last week to solve a conundrum on the
financial aspects of matrimony, and he did
it without calling in the emergency gang.
One Wilson and his wife, by thrift and
amity in the household, had purchased seve-
ral properties, and, in order to save them
from the wolves in case of bankruptcy and
the death-duty in case of his decease, they
were put in his wife’s name. Unfortunately
for Wilson, amity flew out of the window
one day and his an-
noyed smarter-half
claimed the lot. Wil-
son promptly ran
her up to the Su-
preme Court, and
there explained that
the arr a n gement
was merely tempor-
ary or that it was
an honorable under-
standing or some-
thing. The Judge,
however, announced
that, while these hon-
orable understand-
ings to defeat credi-
tors or the revenue,
looked pretty while
they were observed
by both parties, he
had to bring the cold
eye of the Law to
bear on them when
the parties fell out.
In this case, the
land, having been
purchased in the wife’s name, was hers,
and there was nothing else to be said, ex-
cept that Wilson might call in as soon as
convenient and pay off a stack of costs.
From the Oldest Inhabitant: —
Another of the historic estates of Sydney is being
cut up and put on the market. This is Barcom
Glen, at Darlinghurst, the old home of the Wests.
Thomas West came to Sydney in 1800, and was over-
seer of carpenters, etc., in the State’s timber and join-
ery yard. (Ah, those good old times were dreadful So-
cialistic days!) After the establishment was broken
up he started on his own account as a miller, and in
1810 obtained from Goveriior Macquarie the land now
known as Barcom Glen, where he erected the first
water-mill in Australia. The mill was ready for
work in 1812, and was formally opened by the
Governor and his wife. For the name, Barcom Glen,
Macquarie is supposed to have been responsible.
» * * *
Events are proving, that Dowie’s Zion
was a first-rate business proposition. The
Jews knew that when they stood be-
hind him. In return he stood behind them
and forbade pork. Outside the Melbourne
Temperance Hall, where Dowieism,
or rather Volivaism, still holds forth,
there is posted a copy of the latest
issue of the Zion newspaper, only a
few weeks old, showing that biz is
good. To be sure, the place was nom-
inally sold up; but the mortgagees
continue Elijah’s former Australian
lieutenant, Wilbur Glen Yoliva, in
power. There is no humbugging
pretence any longer of Elijah-ship;
nevertheless, Dowie is honored as the
founder of an excellent shop. Yoliva re-
ports a favorable balance-sheet, with great
prospects of extension. Nothing can better
Zion’s position, on Lake Michigan, midway
between Chicago and Milwaukee. For the
rest, a branch tabernacle is kept up in
Chicago, and the White Robed Choir is
maintained in full fig.

“Harrison O.” : In Melbourne Pirate En-
terprise is sitting on the chest of the birth-
rate in a manner that calls for interference.
A number of house agents have come to a
dishonorable understanding whereby the
man who has helped to populate the State
is heavily fined for his disgraceful conduct.
For each child under 16 years a shilling a
week is added to the rent of his house. Thus
a man with only two “encumbrances” is
penalised to the £s 4s. a year, and
it is proposed to keep “this up for 16 years.
A person with four children can in this way
be robbed of £166. While this spoliation is
permitted, the offer of a baby bonus of £5
is like an attempt to bait an elephant with ;
a caraway seed. , ?
Extracts from a recent semi-heroic narra :
tive entitled “The Rescue of the s.s. Queens-
land off Wilson’s Promontory,” or “On a
Lee Shore”: —
One fireman taken at his home refused point-blank
to put to sea on such a night. Then an effort
was made to make up a crew from the other mem-
bers of the tug-boat crews. More point-.flank refusals
were the' result.
And again:—
When daylight came, the Heads were in sight,
and shortly after 6.30 a.m. the Nyora was abreast
of the signal station. There, to the delight of all
on board, fluttered the signal, “Nyora, return to
Bay ” Captain ‘Mcßain put the helm hard over with
a sigh of regret, and the Nyora went about. “An-
other big salvage job gone,” he muttered. The
men were equally disappointed, but their regret was
tempered with pleasure, etc., etc.
While the scratch salvage crew deserves
some credit for a very wild, wet trip down
the bay in the middle of a shrieking night,
the point-blankers who refused to be heroes
for any earthly consideration, and stayed
resolutely at home, deserve at least to be
treated with resnect. It is very doubtful if
the struggling Nyora could have survived
the boiling Rip, or helped the temporarily
crippled steamer that was depending on the
strength of its anchor chains while the engi-
neers toiled with the broken machinery. The
point-blankers who refused to take the
chances were no more than anti-gamblers who
preferred to stay safely at home, so that they
might resume work unsensationally on Mon-
day morning. The adventurous spirit who
sets out to gather in glorv and gilt is of
course entitled to honor; but there is also
plentv of room for the valiant stay-at-home,
who defies public opinion in bis busted slip-
pers, and states quite plainly his intention
of taking care of himself, his wife and his
children. If all of us were heroes, who
would do the cheering when the medals were
handed out? Also, who would contribute to
the relief funds?
The drivel of the dairymen who have been
yowling to the Food Supply Commission
that the milk standard of N.S.W. is too
high is not likely to deceive anyone. The
dairyman’s plaintive yarn is that he can
only sell milk as good as he gets from his
cows: he can do nothing to enrich it. Which
is true; but it is also true that he can get
better cows. The Australian dairyman, in
the mass, is bitterly opposed to herd-testing

but if it were instituted he would soon
know what cows to turn into beef, and what

dairyman’s feelings, in the interests of pub-

A defaulting cadet recently made the un-
successful plea that he was too tired at the
end of the day’s toil to go forth and drill,
and much sympathy has been publicly ex-
pressed for the allegedly weary lad. It may
sound pathetic, but it would have sounded
more convincing had the youngster produced
evidence that he invariably stayed at home
at nights and rested on the verandah, breath-
ing ozone. The youth who is too tired to
drill, but has unlimited energy to rage round
till close upon the noon of night, is the sort
that is likely to increase enormously in num-
bers once tiredness is taken as an excuse.
Had he justified his absence on the ground
that big brother Bill, who is a 10-times
better fighting machine and several times
stronger, is not compelled to drill, he would
{have made a lot of big skulkers look beastly
-■•mean. * * *
“0.K.”: Imported Orangeism will never
abate its rancor so long as Holy Church con-
tinues to assert an official relationship to
Irish patriotism, and stimulate Irish pro-
vincial feeling in the Commonwealth. Take
an extract from Archbishop Carr’s circular
letter to the R.C. churches in Victoria, re
St. Pat’s Day:—
This year the celebrations will have a special sig-
nificance. During more than a century these cele-
brations have kept Ireland’s faith and Ireland s claim
to legislative independence before the nations of the
w0r1d.... Here in Melbourne we have yielded to none
in the" fervor of our religious and patriotic demonstra-
tions .... Let us all, priests and people, unite m
securing: one united, great success.
Practically, his Grace the Archbish links
Irish nationalism with Roman Catholicism,
and thereby gives a color of truth to Brother
Snowball’s bleat of “Home Rule means
Rome Rule,” regardless of the fact that the
most effective champion of the cause within
the past 40 vears was Parnell, the Protes-
tant. Patriotism has indeed no religion,
and the true Nationalist wants no priest to
raise his banner for him.

RUM CAMP.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Liquor is on the prohibited list at the Federal
Capital military camp ; but owing, it is alleged, to
fears of pneumonia, the doctors eventually ordered a
ration of rum to be served out. —Military note.
The red dust made a choking haze where’er a squad-
ron rode;
The dry hills seemed to say no rain had fallen
for a year;
But still, according to the rules, no foaming liquor
flowed,
And resting soldiers drily cursed when comrades
mentioned beer.
Still, loyal at Teetotal Camp, they plodded through
their drill,
Advanced and scouted, charged and wheeled to
husky bugle notes.
The sullen thunder rolled and boomed behind the
driest hill;
The horsemen glimpsed the racing clouds and felt
for overcoats.
Small kitten gusts caught up the dust and threw it
at their eyes;
It played with manes, and tails, and tents, and
rolled things to and fro;
And presently some lukewarm drops came spitting
from the skies ;
The heated air began to cool —the wind began to
blow.
Then came the night, and, while they slept, like
Templars Good and True,
Cloud reinforcements hurried up and roofed wide
Heaven’s arch.
They woke to find a chilly morn, with drapings black
and blue,
And, wrapped in stuffy overcoats, began the usual
march.
The piper blew, the drummer rolled, and, stepping
anyhow,
They started on a straggling tramp across a land-
scape drear;
Damp, overcoated men fell in and splashed behind the
row
Raging beneath the moving sign that shouted “We
Want Deer” !
The O.C. grinned, the Doctor grinned; the Army
Service jumped,
And soon a rattling Service cart drove off towards
Queanbeyan....
With loving care a load of rum it later campward
bumped !....
Before the bugle wailed “Last Post,” ’twas rum
for ev’ry man!
God bless the Doctor and his fears! Pneumonia’s
bad in camp,
But Army Regulations can be made a d—d sight
worse!
Tea-meeting ordinances made for youth and drunken
scamp
May ripen moderate drinkers for the firing squad
and hearse.
But, thank the Lord, who makes His sun to ripen
hops and cane!
The warming liquor came to those who followed
pipe and drum.
Australian soldiers, rise and cheer the beating autumn
rain !
Lord, bless our arms with storms to break the rules
and bring us rum !

exceeded by 650 the total of the preced-
ing year, and the rate—B.o6 per 1000

enced in the State for 52 years.

The non-militant section of the
woman suffrage advocates has begun to
boycott tradesmen anti clergymen op-
posed to the suffrage extension. These
women have also started a movement
to divert charitable and other donations
to the suffrage cause, and to withdraw
money from the post-office savings banks

“Eddysou” : Tollman is worth £300,000 if
a penny. He owns several large junks of a high-
priced Melbourne suburb, besides great arable tracts
in the country, and lives in a fine demi-mansion, with
superb grounds and a pretentious drive through an
oak avenue. But Pollman himself never uses that
aristocratic entrance. The back gate and a right-of-
way with an asphyxiated cat in it, are good enough
for Pollman. Pollman is a tall, grim, raw-boned man
who lets his hair accumulate because a haircut at Is.
is ruination to a man. He clings to an over-exuber-
ant creeping whisker for economical reasons, too, and
his toilet consists chiefly of some odd boots, a pair
of over-worked pants, and an old roan shirt. The rest
of Pollman’s family lives up to the front view of the
habitation, and dodges about in a motor-car as com-
modious as a ferry, wearing clothes beautiful to be-
hold; but Pollman potters round the garden, putting
snails to death, or nurturing the early potato, or he
massages the cow, or does odd laboring jobs for the
hens. At times he steals out by the back gate,
pushing a crazy barrow with a stub-toed shovel on it,
to go and reduce expenses by executing menial tasks
on his other properties. One morning about a fort-
night back Pollman was slinking along a by-street,
pushing his barrow, when McMahon sighted him.
McMahon is a contractor. He had a building moving
up on an adjacent block. “Hi, you!” called McMahon.
Pollman stopped. “Kim ’ere!” said the contractor
peremptorily. Pollman obeyed. “You want a job,
said McMahon, with the air of a man that would
not be denied. “See here, I’ll give you five bob to
clean up this rubbish and stack it in the corner
there.” Pollman looked up the street and down the
street. “Five and six,” he whispered huskily. “Oh,
very well —five and six. Get down to it.” Pollman’s
family will never know, being away on tour that
dav ; but Pollman took the job.

“Yannatherra” : Puncher was adjectivally
eloquent as he gazed on the bogged tractor and
waggon-load of wheat. The 4ft. of gluey black-soil
creek-bed held the caravan in a devil’s
grip. Puncher poetically consigned
the mud to several unorthodox hells
-by as many unorthodox routes. After
carefully studying the pressure gauge,
he admonished the stoker-driver to
“try the swine now!” The screech of
released steam and a terrific straining
of the old scrapheap resulted, but
never a budge. Just then, I noticed
the pressure was only registering
101 b., and commented on the futility
of the thing. “ ’Sail right,” replied
the engineer. “I’ve screwed the
blanky valve down, and that’s the
second time round.”
“W. Kabe” : With certain reservations I
agree with “Cavalryman” (B. 20/2/’l3) that Aus-
tralia can’t ride. Frank Brown mentions the cattle-
country. Well, I have had a good deal of experi-
ence on perhaps the biggest cattle run in the Eastern
States, and I say that the average Australian even
there is not a horseman in the best sense of the
term. The trouble, in the majority of cases, is bad
hands. As for sheep country—and there is i little
else in N. S. Wales—a man can spend half a lifetime
crawling after the woollies and never learn to ride.
Such jobs as mustering, droving, boundary-ndmg,
etc., entail riding at a snail’s pace, and that is why
there are so many poor horsemen in the saltbush.
They get careless and ride anyhow. It is the last,
rough work, such as cattle drafting, Virumby-runm g,
polo, etc., that begets horsemen; and not one ma
in 20 now in the bush does any of it.
ability to ride buckjumpers is not in itse P
conclusive of horsemanship. As most men on station,
will teU you, rough-riders are, in most cases ™ ,
murderers. The best feature of the Australian
riding is that his seat is a natural and easy one,
due to the fact that he has been accustomed -
spend long hours in the saddle and has learnt to
quite at home there, which makes >t infimtcl
easier for man and horse than if he adopted t
rigid and artificial seat of the riding-schools.
8.J.5.” : Can Australia ride? Rather!
Take the members of the 11th and 12th batteries of
Field Artillery in Brisbane, for instance. Composed
of young Australians who slog a pen or tend a
machine in a big city, one would not expect them
town recruits ’ —to be able to handle horses de-
cently ; yet the writer, who has attended five annual
camps of training, has seen the young fellows hand-
hng horses in a manner which makes one feel proud
o >eing in their company. There were merry times
when the animals were first placed in a gun-carriage.
Hardly a team drew out but that a general mel6e
ensued. The lead horses bucked for all they were
worth, and then turned and mixed up in the general
fun with the polers. But the boys stuck, and always
managed to get into camp. You can’t beat the
young Australian. He comes up smiling every time,
and he of all men knows how to adapt himself to
circumstances. Last camp of training (1912), at
Tambourine, one of the finest and pluckiest bits of
horsemanship that one could delight in seeing was
shown by three boys. The gun was brought into
position, but in freeing the horses some of the har-
ness became tangled, the animals took fright, and
away they galloped. It looked certain death for one
at least of the boys. But they kept cool, and eventu-
ally brought their steeds to a standstill. Six mad
horses controlled by three boys! No wonder there
was a cheer. Then again when Kitchener was pre-
sent at the big camp at Lytton, a squadron of Light
Horse took up such a position after negotiating
some tricky country that the iron-jawed organiser
had to admit that he had never seen such an example
of horsemanship. Yet the outback horsemanship is
still more amazing. On the stations they breed a
race of men that no other country in the world can
show. They are in the saddle from daylight to dusk,
riding over the ranges and round the runs. Talk
about hunting! You can crowd more dangers into
one minute in chasing brumbies in the scrubby
ranges than you could get if you spent a whole life-
time chasing the fox; and I would lay my last
penny on a squadron of A.L.H. losing the most
crack squadron of cavalry there is in Europe.
“Mr. Ocky” : This place is over-run with
insane creatures. Since the first cat arrived (there
are now seven) the rats and mice have increased
enormously, and live in the utmost accord with the
purring maniacs who are hogs for milk. Their head-
quarters are in the front room. Five cockatoos sit
on the roof. To-day they pulled the spouting off
and dropped it on the milk-jug, uprooted the prize
dahlia, and dismembered the fence. I shall never
forget their howls of execration when I protested.
The horse refuses to be harnessed, and the cow has
made off towards the river to commit suicide. Also,
the three choicest fowls have wandered to the next
street, whilst the fourth-best is trapped underneath
the house and calling out like the very devil. Pro-
bably she has caught a snake. Since I came here I
have murdered four snakes, my three biggest
dogs are barking at 11 others beneath the wood-
heap. There are half a dozen magpies in the gar-
den ; they all rise at 4 a.m. to say grace over the
slugs, but as the slugs throw off the bedclothes at
3 a.m., the vegetable crop won’t be much good
this year. Every season I watch the cherries get
ripe, and so do the sparrows and starlings. There
are innumerable canaries, parrots, pigeons, etc.,
to be fed, and the increased cost of living does not
worry them. This is also the resort of cicadas,
crickets, youngsters selling concert tickets, hawkers,
Salv’army collectors, organ-grinders, mosquitoes, and
so many other pests that 1 begin to think life is
hardly worth the battle.

The story of a goldfields election: —
Howard Snips was one of the big men on Cool-
gardie, when he was nominated for a seat in the
Leg. Council. His opponent was a down-country
man; but the vote on the field was strong enough
to return Howard—-if the vote could be got to the
poll. This was the trouble; prospecting was pro-
ceeding ; and many men whose names had been placed
on the roll had drifted too far away to be got in
time.
On the day of polling it was fairly clear the fields
were beaten. The farmers’ vote would be polled
solid for the farmers’ man, and the miners’ vote
could not be got in. Snips was a sport, and if the
reasonable expenditure of money could have varied
the position as it presented itself at the last moment,
he would have played up the shekels like a prince.
About one o’clock on the day Scotty came to the
booth. Jack Moore was a scrutineer for Snips.
“Hallo, Jack,” Scotty called out. “How’s it
goin’ ?”
“No good,” said Jack. “We haven’t got the men
here, and we’re going to be left.”
“Let’s have a look at the list.” Scotty took up
a roll and saw a number of names on the first page,
ticked off as absent.
“Say,” he said, “these men are not absent. Most
of ’em’s workin’ down at the King Solomon. I’ll go
and bring ’em up.”
In half an hour he was back with 10 or a dozen
men, and a string of others following.
With the roll in one hand, and a pencil in th.e
other, he called a name for the first of his party.
“‘John Henry Smith, Accountant,’: that’s you,
Peter,” he said to the big Norwegian at his elbow.
“You go right in and vc <. ‘Henry Wilkinson, En-
gineer’—that’s vou, AlicK. When the Warden asks
you, just say ‘Henry Wilkinson, Engineer,’ and go
right along and vote.”
Scotty polled a big Snips vote before the after-
noon was fairly bitten into, and would have gone on,
but for the mislabelling of one of his force.
" ‘Reginald Waters, Civil Engineer,’ ” he read
from the roll. “That’s you, Hennessy,” he said to a
big Irish person across whose features “Pat was
written in large type, and “navvy” in smaller
letters.
The Warden challenged him: “Is your name Regi-
nald Waters?” lie asked.
“It is,” replied Pat.
“And you’re a surveyor?’
“I am that.”
“I wouldn’t vote, if I were you,” said Finnerty.
“All right, Warden,” was all Hennessy had to
say, before adopting the suggestion “ ’Twas Scotty
towld me Snips would be beat if I didn t put in a
vote for him.” , .. ... ~
But the Snips poll was, by now, so big that the
down-countryman could not have headed him in any
circumstances, and, by common consent, the auxili-
aries were drawn off.
Prior to proceeding to where the barmen were
believed to be operating, Scotty, standing up in his
cart his face shining with a great light such as is
seen’ sometimes on the features of a man who has
done the State good service, called out to Jack
Moore across the booth: “Ef there s any chance o
bein’ short on the figures, John, gimme some more
names, I got any God’s quantity o voters.
“Young Feller” : I’ve captured the King
Mug. He’s fresh from Middlesex (Eng.). They
brought him up here secretly, and put him on a bit
of land, with strict injunctions not to leave it till
he had made his fortune. The land produces an ex-
cellent kind of red gibber, invaluable for slinging at
policemen, but it is too far removed from the world’s
markets to permit of the crop being immediately
negotiable. ’Enery ’Awkins trotted over to my
place on Sunday. He has 16 pearlies on his best
coat, and walks like a man thrusting a handcart full
of herrings. I was dusting my spike-haired terrier
with insectibane, and ’Enery watched me with in-
creasing interest for some minutes. Then said he,
with the air of a man who could contain himself
no longer: “Gorstruth, cully, that stuff ain’t to kill
dorgs!”

THE IMMIGRANT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bill Hughes came out to Toodlembu«k
To cultivate the land.
He always spoke of soil as muck ;
He didn’t understand
The milking art, and sought to gain
A stomach pump “them cows” to drain.
It puzzled him to see a beast
Lie on the grass by day,
While with a swing from west to east
Her nether jaw did play.
He wouldn’t keep a single head
With them bad habits, so he said.
To Marsh, who sold the cows, in quest
Of good advice he ran.
Said Marsh: “Why, that is on’y jest
The cud they’re chewin’, man.”
“I don’t care what’s the brand,” cried Hughes,
“I won’t put up with cows what chews!”

Pa Pooa : The boss had intermittent
fever, and as that does not tend to improve one’s
temper we tried to look busy. Suddenly the boss
found an opening. “Smith!” he bawled, “what
the ? ? ? ?? ? ? do you mean by taking those
stores of Ellis’s back ?” Smith explained that Ellis
owed the firm a fair bit, and as he had done no
good on the field he (Smith) thought the best thing
to do was to take back some of the
stores, and reduce the debt that way. “Best
thing be damned!” yelled the boss. “What the
! ! !’s the good of the stuff to us now! It’s been
hawked all over the Waria and Mambare, and there
won’t be a label left on a tin, and the ’ll be
in for fresh tack to get down on to Cloudy Bay
with. Then followed a description of unsuccessful
prospectors and a final order to get the returns out
for inspection. Smith proceeded to comply, and
finally announced that the supplies were on the
counter. Unfortunately he failed to put the meats
out of reach of the stray sun-rays which came in
through the skylight. No self-respecting tin of
Luncheon- Beef can bear sun-boiling and fermenting
more than seven times, so three tins of Ellis’s
nd-urns burst with indignation and a loud report.
The boss grabbed his revolver, thinking that some
cutting playful, just as a semi-
solid mass of outraged meat shot across the store
and deposited some of itself in the office. I
beat the boss to the door by three inches. “Who
the *** did that?” he demanded, brandishing the
revolver under the nose of Smith. Smith explained,
and the boss swore afresh. “Where’s the store
‘boys’?” he bellowed. “They went out the back as
soon as it happened,” said the assistant. “Agita?
Agita ? Yes, Taubada. ’ “Where the roses-and-
lavender have you been? Here, get inside there and
clean up that mess!” “Me no want ’im, Taubada.
Bull-a-ma-cow he been dead long time, he stinnik.”
I admired the grace with which the “boy” dodged
the boot with the boss’s foot in it, and standing at
a respectful distance repeated, “Me no want ’im,
Taubada. Me no want ’im.” The boss looked as
if he was going to burst, and we all hung back
expecting things. Taking two strides to the door
he grabbed his nose and advanced the remaining
distance with levelled revolver. Taking hurried
aim at the fysol shelf he let drive. At first I
thought him successful, but the assistant groaned
Then I knew the bullet had found the whisky depart-
ment. Five more shots followed in quick succession, and
several bottles of pickles, a ham and a pair of boots
suffered considerably. The boss retired temporarily
defeated, and I felt rather uncomfortable when the
assistant animadverted on the markmanship. Re-
loading he again advanced with a determined look,
and revolver and nose held fast. Providence directed
the second-last bullet into a case of trade scee. r ,
and we breathed at last.
“Wal” : Poor old “Platman” has had his
leg extended by the aborigines of the Cook district,
or else he would never have made the rash state-
ment about the boomerang being unknown there
HALL MARKED.
“Do you think the old bloke can ride, Joe?"
“Ride? Why them legs speak for themselves.
I'd like to ' ave a quid for every buck-
jumper 'e's rode in is time , and charnceit."
(B. 13/2/’l3). Speaking as a man born in Cook-
town I can say with authority that the boomerang
was used most freely when I was a boy, and if it
is not in favor now it may be only due to the fact
that a tomahawk is easier to obtain. Many and
many times have I seen boomerang-throwing com-
petitions held on the racecourse outside Cooktown.
Whether the competitors were natives of the dis-
trict I would not say; but that the boomerang is
known by the aborigines of that district is positively
unquestionable.

“The Hager”: A note on the fecundity of
the death adder. At Six-mile farm, Milmerran
(Q.), Harry Simmons found himself the other day
in uncomfortable proximity to one .of the hated
vermin. The vermin attempted to crawl away, but
was pilled. Its extraordinary size aroused curiosity,
so an operation was performed and 22 young live
adders were found camped in the home paddock.

“Eryander” : “Mallarapoonie’s” bat-adven-
ture (B. 27/2/’l3) reminds me that I was supposed
to have cashed a large draft upon my imagination
when I introduced a similar incident into one of
my Malaysian yarns, and that much superior
“pooh-pooh!” was expended over the idea of a man
being capsized by a bat. Now, the kalong (Pteropus
javanicus ) of those parts—a gigantic cousin of our
own flying-fox—measures often sft. in
wing-spread, and when a few hundreds
of him —but here is what actually oc-
curred. Three of us had poked a good
way into one of the curious limestone
caves common in south-western Borneo,
and one of the party proposed to dis-
charge his revolver in order to get the
strange acoustic effects which would fol-
low. “Better not,” he was advised.
“You would probably bring out a hur-
ricane of bats.” “Bats!” he repeated
contemptuously and fired. “Down
flat!” I jerked out, and in a few seconds
they were upon us, filling the air with
stink unspeakable, flapping out our
damar-torches, and leaving us in pitchy
darkness, through which came groans
from the injudicious powder-burner. My
other mate and myself had escaped pretty
well, because the bulk of the tornado
had passed over us, but our over-confi-
dent friend had been bowled over like a
ninepin, and had hit his head hard
against the rocky floor. We said nothing
about a damfool, but the back of that
fellow’s skull was pretty eloquent upon
that point for weeks afterwards.
“McNorth” : Most of us who live in the
extreme north of Australia agree with the sentiments
expressed in “Gerald Barker’s” par (B. 23/l/’l3)
on the extermination of Binghi. Binghi is a para-
site and a nuisance. In his native state he is little
better than a dingo. He wakes in the morning,
picks up the tracks of a bandicoot or a wallaby or
something, runs it down, cats it and then sleeps till
hungry again. He has no habitat, no gardens, no
anything. As a workman he is, at best, a makeshift.
We who live in the outposts, away from civilisation,
have enough to contend with without having our camps
robbed and our cattle speared by irresponsible or
other niggers. The doctrine of their gradual exter-
mination by letting them alone is not as harsh a
creed as the uninitiated who live in the South may
think. We in the North know. A Biughi can never
be anything but a Binghi. How many times
has the experiment of educating him up to the
white man’s standard, by missionaries and others,
been tried with the same result —-failure!' He
absorbs the white man’s vices but not his virtues.
A race belonging to the palaeolithic period cannot be
raised in one lift to the conditions of the present
time, and if the process is made gradual the original
species is lost and a new breed evolves. Mornington
Island, the only terra incognita in Queensland, is
to be the scene of the next educational experiment.
When a missionary edifice has been there for a few
years the nigger’s course of trouble will commence.
Better let him alone. He’ll go to his destruc-
tion fast enough without tobacco, clothes, European
tucker and religion.
“Lou” explains how to get rid of rabbits:
This recipe is mostly for the strenuous grafter
with a large family on a small holding.
First to be dealt with are the single rabbits, who
live m unpretentious burrows, with one, two per-
haps three openings. Get up early, and, arrived at
the first burrow, locate yourself on the roof. Send
the missus out about three chains. Get her to buck
about, waving her arms wildly, screeching her best,
and working in a circle round the underground
airair 11ns will scare any wandering rabbits, who
promptly make for cover. As soon as the last one
is in, rush home, seize the shovel I forgot to tell
you about, gallop backhand fl.ng it at the rabbit
that is coming out again. But mind the missus.
W hen they are all in, fill in the holes with the loose
earth that is outside the burrow, ram it well with
the shovel handle, and hean loose earth on top of
each hole. Treat all the smaller homesteads alike:
then go and get a feed. While you are away the im-
prisoned bunnies commence to scratch for daylight.
1 , ,! 00 ® e falls in; some smother, some get out,
but having filled in their own domiciles, are obliged
to hire a room with the married rabbits.
Now get some common tar in a kerosene tin, and
gather up the straw envelopes that were on the
bottles of beer. Try and remember where you left
the shovel, and sally forth with the missus to deal
row’s the m ° re pretentious and thickly- populated bur-
„7,Arriy,ed the first, let the missus go mad again.
When they are all in, fray the wide end of the straw
envelopes, dip them in the tar, shove them down
into the holes, and fill in around each with earth,
treat all the big burrows alike. After a while the
inmates commence to scratch, pick up first the tar
and then the sand, and come out with their feet
asohalted. This reduces their speed a good deal and
prevents them cleaning out their homes. Leave the
nussus to fill in the holes when the last man is out,
dash home,. shove the steed into the spring cart, fling
a pig net in, swoop down on the township, gather a
pack of dogs, dump them in, fasten the pig-net
securely, give the steed a (climb in yourself first)
terrible fright, and make back for the selection.
Arrived home, the first thing to do is to get out
of the cart. Do this carefully. As soon as you hit
