“Henry Horsecollar” : I am a-wearied of
this clamor for the preservation of “aboriginal
names.” Nine times out of ten there weren’t any.
Just consider the reason of names. They are wanted
to define cities, towns, villages and hamlets. They
also serve to locate on the map roads and cross-
roads, castles and fortresses, great battlefields, farms,
cattle and sheep stations, the sites of fairs and mar-
kets, rivers, fords, mountains and sundries. The
aborigine had no map. He had some rivers, fords,
mountains and sundries, but he had none of the
other things. So he probably had very few names.
Take the Sahara as an example. It is a
vast place, but the name of most of it was
just plain Sand. The French are sinking wells, and
finding water, and creating scraps of fertility, and
raising towns; but it is no use asking the native
for the aboriginal name of the scrap when it is
created. You might as well ask him to state the
name of one particular square foot of the sky or
one particular square inch on the top of a big table.
The theory that every place had an aboriginal name
even before it was a place seems to me like sheer
foolishness. Of course there is always some wild
humorist who comes forward with a ready-made
legend, which he gets some wild myall of Ballarat
or Rockhampton to endorse for the price of a pair
of beers, but the wild humorist isn’t to be taken
seriously.
“Polish” : Re Frank Brown and his “out-
back horse-breakers” (B. 20/2/’l3). Has he ever
bought animals out of outback mobs sold as broken?
Not 5 p.c. of such animals can be caught outside of
a “crush” ; practically all are “halter-breakers”
when you get them on a halter; and
directly the girths are tightened, the fun
(for onlookers) commences. As to pick-
ing up a leg of one of these nags, or
combing a tail, I’d sooner stand a round
with Johnson than attempt any one of
tue three. No! Brute strength of thighs
and calves, combined with recklessness,
does not constitute horsemanship. I’ve
seen a monkey in a circus remain on a
pig-rooting Australian outlaw, but was it
a horseman? Attend outback pubs, semi-
outback picnic meetings and shows, and
study equine education, and the further outback you
get the worse it gets. Bring a real “outbacker"
inside civilisation, and you’ll find nine times out
of 10 that has to be re-broken, and, worst of all,
re-mouthed.
* * * *
“Condamine”: In the interests of suffer-
ing animals I’m with those who have been trying to
wean a congregation of Outback fools from the
barbed wire habit. I have had experience (too much
of it) with all kind of wire, an-’ I say that three
good plain strands are as effec:r, e as two of the
barbed variety. The barbs are necessary in three
places only: (1) On top of a netting fence to keep
dingoes back ; (2) Underneath netting to remonstrate
with nosing pigs and ’roo rats; (3) Round the
water-holes along the Cotter bed to keep the rabbits
from drinking the water with which Brother Den-
man will christen the Federal capital.

If you start to stick the Truth upon the Tomh
Just think of all the trouble it might lead to
You’d have to say the fraud was just a fraud ’
And gi'iP the facte about him you would need t,,
You’d have to say the lawyer-man who left ’
With clients all a-weeping in the gloom
Was a grim and greedy shark who would’ skin v

If you start to stick the Truth upon the Tomb
There's scarce a man among us who could bear ir •
The petty tricks we did and the wickedness we hid ’
How should we like the scoffing world to share it?
The skeleton inside the closet door
We’d have to give his case a little room,
With an outline of his frame and the tale of how he
came—
If we started with the Truth upon the Tomb!

Would be carven on the marble—would thev
tremble ?
If our granite should reluctantly confess
The means that made our money in the boom,
It would make the buried knaves surely turn within
their graves—
If you started with the Truth upon the Tomb.
If you started putting Truth upon the Tomb,
The wight we all so publicly lamented,
Would have it written there (how the characters
would glare !) :
“He left a grimy record, unrepented!”
If all the narrow virtues of our Age
Were printed with the follies of our bloom,
We should pray the day would come when the crema-
torium
Would displace the wretched Thing upon the Tomb.
If we started putting Truth upon the Tomb,
How happy were the simple and the fameless!
How many a noble name that the Annals proudly
claim
Would count among the shady and the shameless!
How many a stately marble monument
Above the graveyard fellowship would loom
And betray upon its face, “He had neither Grit nor
Grace!”
If we started with the Truth upon the Tomb!

The Salv’army in Adelaide claims to have
made its champion convert in Sarah Fran-
cisco, aged 72. Her police-court convictions
run to 295. nearly all for drunkenness. The
P.M. and the force simply did not know what
to do with her. The Army took her in hand,
and asserts that she has not touched liquor
for nearly a vear. The local War Cry,
boasting of the case, cheerfully labels Sarah
‘ the worst woman I have ever known.”
From Burke, junior :
Apropos “the unhappy Chaipberlain” (B. 13/3/’l3)
who had to decide where to place the knife-and-fork
at Melbourne Government House, for the incoming
and outgoing N.S.W. Governors. It is worth taking a
look at the status of the parties indicated. The first
Chelmsford, Frederick Thesiger, was a son of Charles
Thesiger, Collector of Customs at St. Vincent, an
island in the West Indies—a little place 18 miles
long and 11 wide, about the size of a large bed-quilt
Columbus found it in 1498. When Thesiger I. was
Collector of Customs it was a slave State. The salary
of the Collector would be small. To-dav its Chief
Justice gets only £650, and the Chief Clerk in the
Treasury £2OO. The Collector’s son, first Baron
Chelmsford, was a middy, and served at Copenhagen,
but he left the sea and became a lawyer, was lucky,
and got into Parliament in 1840 for a little place
called Woodstock, which does not now return a mem-
ber. Luck followed him: he became Solictor and
Attorney-General, and in 1858-9 and 1866-8 was
Chancellor in the Tory Governments of Earl Derby.
When Disraeli came into power he did not take
Chelmsford into his Councils, but allowed him to
enjoy his pension of £5OOO a year as an ex-Chan-
cellor. He died in 1878, and was succeeded by his
eldest son, Frederick Augustus Thesiger, who, from
be'ng an ensign in the Rifle Brigade, became a full-
blown commander. In that capacitv he directed
affairs in the Kaffir and Zulu wars (1878). The story
of Rorke’s Drift and the awful slaughter of the
British makes interesting and gruesome reading. Thi=
warlike peer died in 1905, and was succeeded by the
late Governor of N. S. Wales, who at the time of
his appointment to the Governorship of Queensland
was a barrister. The wife of the present peer is a
daughter of the first Baron Wimborne, whose family
name was Guest. They are prettv well-to-do. The
Wimborne peerage was created in 1880. Now for
the Stricklands. Sir Gerald was in the Roval Malta
Militia ; he was born on that island, and had various
official positions. His wife, Ladv Edeline Sackville,
is a daughter of the seventh Earl De La Warr. That
family was ennobled in 1299 by Edward I. Sir
Gerald Strickland’s family dates back to the same
period. Members of it for seven generations repre-
sented Westmoreland, from the first English Parlia-
ment until the reign of Elizabeth. An ancestor of
S : r Gerald, Sir Thomas Strickland, carried the banner
of St. George at Agincourt. The seat of the family
Sizergh Hall, in Westmoreland, has a “Queen’s
Room,’’ which is said to have been occupied by
Catherine Parr, one of the wives of Henry VTTI.
No wonder, faced with two such diverse records, that
Lord Dicky Nevill found himself with a tough
problem to solve when it came to laving the table.
The singing of the “Old Hundredth” at
the christening of Canberra recalls that
before he came to Australia and took to in-
surance drumming, Kink O’Malley was in
business as an evangelist in America. A
man from Australia who recently called in
at Portland, on the Pacific Slope, was inun-
dated with inquiries about the former Bible-
thumper, news having spread in those parts
that he was nowadays a Cabinet Minister in
the Commonwealth. As a “revivalist” show-
man of the true Yankee pattern, Portland
still remembered the Kink as just It.
“T. 0.1.”: Apropos the lady chemist (B.
6/3/T3). N. S. Wales has had two —one

South Aus. has definitely recognised the
claim of John Ridley to the invention of the
wheat-stripper, and has put up a marble bust
of him at the Roseworthy Agricultural Col-
lege. Ridley was in Australia only from
1840 to 1853. He brought out the 'State’s
first steam engine, and ran its first flourmill.
His claim to the invention of the harvester
has sometimes been questioned, but he
always declared that 4he only help he got
was a hint from a Roman machine described
in an Encyclopaedia. His original con-
traption (which was pushed by two horses
irom behind) has since been improved out of
recognition, but in 1845 it was thought a
marvel, and it straightway brought down the
cost of harvesting from 2s. a bushel to 4d.
Excellent judgment was displayed bv an
immigrant’s offspring that first saw the light
on board the Themistocles. The interesting
event was taking place while the boat was
cautiously feeling its way through the dark-
ness towards Cape Otway. The three-mile
limit had just been passed when the denoue-
ment was effected, which meant the addition
ot one to the native population of Australia
and the paying out of another bonus. Had
the infant elected to be born a few minutes
earlier it would have been a native of
btepney (Eng.).

Steam navigation on the Murray was offi-
opened in 1853 by Captain Gaddi’s
0000-mile trip in the Lady Augusta. The
lady after whom the boat was named has
just died in London. She was wife of Fox
Young, Governor of South Aus. at the time
and of _ Tasmania later. Her son, Aretas
loung, is one of Adelaide’s best-known share-
brokers.
Novelist Rider Haggard is the right sort of
xJritisher to send on a trade commission to
the outer rim of Empire.” Although he
earns a decent pittance as the writer of‘‘best
sellers, ’ he farms a good piece of land with
success, going in for the scientific devices so
common in France, Holland, Ireland and
Scandinavia, and almost unknown in Eng-
land , and his book on “Rural Denmark” is
a mine of information on the ways (co-
operative, mostly) by which a small and
naturallv poor country like Denmark has
made itself comfortable and even prosperous.

A KNIGHT SANS PURE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Knight, the runaway narson, said that he had left
England with the churchworker, Miss Grimes, because
scandal had resulted through their working together
m . ~r e ™m en from vice His relations
with Miss Grimes were quite pure During
the voyage Mr. Knight took lessons in farming from
an expert agriculturist on board.— News item.
Once, when converting burglars,
And with a sneak-thief singing hymns,
Showing him ways beyond the stars—■
Celestial paths the Bible limns,
To chase all scandal from my way,
I killed a Bishop every day.
Chaplain was I to the Condemned,
Who waited for the awful rope;
’Twas I his long despairing stemmed,
’Twas I who spoke of heavenly hope,
’Twas I who took my splendid stand
With him, the murderer, hand in hand.
And, when calumnious tongues assailed
Me for the company I kept,
Though conscious virtue unavailed,
I neither pled, nor prayed, nor wept;
I laughed at every scand’lous cur,
And—killed the executioner.
And, so you see, my ways are pure.
In our small cabins did we pray
That our simplicities endure—
Our god-like virtues wax each day :
And when I think what in me lies,
The salt tears come into mine eyes.
Yes! when I th-'nk how good I be—
How bravely I did run away,
And left my brolly by the sea,
And left my wife to say her say—
How bravely faced anew the years,
My goodness fills mine eyes with tears!
So, in this sunny Southern land,
Vn here comes all good—and evil too—
I’ll take my partner by the hand
To face the work we have to do.
Careless of scandal, lies or sport,
I’ll grow tomatoes by the quart.
For, on the ocean, did I take
Lessons from one who, I insist
Was wise as I, and free from fake—
A high-seas agriculturist.
Sister! the Lord shall walk with us,
And we will grow asparagus.
Full virtuous shall our onions be,
And passing true our artichokes;
Honest our budding broccoli,
And grown but for high-minded' blokes;
And, when the evening light is dim,
I’ll fork manure, and sing a hymn.
For I am good, and not as ye,
And scandal shall not blight the spud ;
My sins all washed away at sea,
I rise triumphant from the mud:
And in my melon-patch I sit,
A nice, imported hypocrite.
N.s.w.
MARTIN LUTHER.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The N. S. Wales Government, which must
Oorrow or burst its boom, has arranged with Uncle
Bull for another £3,000,000 at 4 p.c., to be issued
it 98, and repayable in 10 years. Last year the
State Mieawber borrowed £2,000,000 on 12 months
bills, and no doubt some of the present little lot
will go towards repaying that temporary loan. In
addition to paying £4 a year as interest on £9B
and undertaking to pay back £lOO at the end of 1 0
years, the desperate State also agrees to pay a full
half-year’s interest in October, though the final in-
stalment of the principal doesn’t have to be put up
until May. These concessions, plus underwriting
and other charges, will bring up the cost of the
money to about £4 7s. p.c. —a terrific price to pay
for accommodation, seeing that it is repayable in
10 years, wherefore the security is far better than
that of long-dated loans. If there is going to be a
composition and a smash, obviously the Cohen who
has to collect in 10 years will stand a far better
chance of escaping the catastrophe than the Levi
whose bill isn’t payable for 30 years. And on top of
the high rate payable to Cohen there is the high
rate payable for almost everything that will be
bought with the borrowed coin. So the public is
going to get unusually poor value for very expensive
money. However, the bogus Labor crowd in office
in N. S. Wales seems to realise that its one hope
at the coming general election is to borrow no
matter at what cost—and maintain the artificial
prosperity. The trouble is that the farther the pen-
dulum swings that way, the farther back _ will it
swing presently ; and that backward swing is going
to be a very painful business for a lot of people.

If only for the fact that year after year it puts by more
than half its profits, this co. earns this paper’s bless-
ing. Also, for the same reason, it deserves the pub-
lic’s business, because every addition to the funds
laid by gives the insurer further assurance that the
till won’t be empty when he calls round to collect
his money after the fire or the wreck. And the
position is really all the stronger because the rapid
accumulation of reserves hasn’t been accompanied
by any furious increase of risks. Premiums paid
are the only available measure of risks taken;
theoretically (and the theory is based on long ex-
perience) a year’s premiums cover a year’s risks,
plus expenses and a bit over for the shareholders.
Vow this shows (among other things) what the
premiums are:—

Capital is £75,000 and reserves £126,250; and
together they are equal to 22 p.c. of the premiums
—-'which is a position much stronger than the average.
Put in another way, if the co. were at once hit by
.a disaster as great as all the big and little disasters
of the past five years put together, it could pay the
■ claims, assuming, of course, that the investments
■ realised 20s. in the £. As for those investments,
they certainly don’t please this paper very well. Of
the co.’s funds, no less than £106,705, or about
■one-half, is out on mortgage, £50,630 is in un-
specified debentures and £20,549 is in properties;
and that doesn’t leave much liquid—about £40,000.
It is ample, no doubt, if nothing abnormal has to
tbe met; but an insurance co. can’t afford to build
<on that. The bait which lures the co. on to tie up
its money is the high rate of interest earnings.
Last year the investments brought in a shade over
•4 i pc. all round, and provided enough for an 11
p.c. div. —a very cheerful result. All the same,
•there must be risks in this sort of investment; and
the balance-sheet would look all the better for a re-
serve against the depreciation of assets. Otherwise
there is no visible smudge on the accounts.

lludoart Parker, Ltd. :• —
Dividends.
, ' , To Re-
Dec., Profits. 6p.c. Pref. Ord. serves. Deficit.
1911.. £3,565
Reserves.
1912.. £93,016 £28,125 7 p.c. =£33,125 131,766 28,201
Steamship cos.’ accounts generally leave a good deal
to the imagination ; and this new concern—it was
a private co. previously—isn’t going to differ from
the rest. The prospectus offered the outfit to the
public for £750,000, payable in shares—half of
them 6 p.c. cumulative pref. ; the other half ordinary.
The public was invited to subscribe 125,000 prefs.
•■and 125,000 ordinaries, all of £1 each; and there
\w&« a scramble to get in. On these ordinary shares
10s. was paid down and 10s. on July 1. The assets
were valued at £751,404. That provided a reserve
of £1404 to start with. On the other hand, pre-
liminary exes, ran to £4969; so there was a net
■deficit of £3565 when the new co. took down the
shutters. Net earnings for the year are alleged by
(the Profit and Loss account to have been £74,358.
But a reserve fund of £3354 appears in the balance-
sheet along with an insurance fund of £16,709. In
the infant table printed above, these amounts have
been added to the profits, although, admittedlv, an
insurance fund isn’t necessarilv profits laid by.
Sometimes it is an amount to the credit of a fund
created to cover risks which are still current; just
as often, especially in good times, it is a stocking
into which profits are dropped furtively. Then
there is the big question of depreciation. Five per
cent, or so of the cost of steamships ought to be
written off every year to provide for replacements;
and obviously a co. by writing off more or less than
5 p.c. can whittle down the profits in the one case
and inflate them in the other. It is impossible to
even guess at what this co. writes off. Ship pro-
perty is dropped into the accounts “at valuation” ;
also there is a reserve for “contingencies, deprecia-
tion of property and shares in other cos., repairs
and renewals, superannuation fund,” and other
things. This so-called reserve totals £30,849.
Some of it was taken over from the old proprietary
co. A part of it may be a genuine reserve, but since
another part of it certainly isn’t—the superannua-
tion fund, for instance—it isn’t shown in the table.
Nothing very definite can, therefore, be said about
the co.’s first year. It certainly looks as though the
dividend has been very easily earned (on the ordin-
aries the co. paid 6 p.c. for the first half and 8 p.c.
for the second). But of the surplus of £31,766, a
good amount may not be genuine profit. On the
other hand, the co. has no less than £432,046 of
“Government and other debentures, Government
stock, fixed deposits and other investments,” in
addition to the value of “shares in other cos.” and
current account money ( £11,054). The size of
this more or less liquid item suggests that the co.
has a big amount of capital which may be put to
more profitable use presently. The liabilities total
£124,080. About half of it is deposit money; no
doubt this will be paid off as the deposits become
due. A lot of repayment has been done already out
of subscribed capital; when the co. was formed
£201,642 was owing to shareholder-depositors. The
co. has thus the means available for the considerable
expansion of business which was forecasted in the
prospectus. No doubt things have been unusually
good in the inter-State and Maoriland trade; and
even if things keep as good, which is unlikely, costs
have lately been running up. Nevertheless, the
general tendency will be in the direction of a bigger
trade. Take this into account; add the fact that a
large amount of the co.’s capital can’t have earned
much ; and the size of the profits for the first year
seem then to justify shareholders in thihking that,
they have struck something pretty good.

There are two classes,of shares —“subscription” and
“paid up.” You can pay 3s. lOd. a month for 15
years, or larger amounts up to 14s. lOd. a month for
five years, and thus purchase a £5O share; or
£39 3s. 6d. paid now will buy a £5O share in five
years, or £l7 18s. lid. will buy one in 21 years—a
fine example of the sort of busy bee that compound
interest is. But this is not all. After putting by
enough to provide for the agreed-upon interest, the
directors consider whether they can’t give a bonus.
Last year £751 was made available for that pur-
pose out of the surplus, and that meant 4s. per
£5O share, payable at maturity—not a big bonus,
but satisfactory considering the security, which looks
sound. Reserves, certainly, are not prodigious; and
they have to protect a good deal of deposit money
as well as the share capital. This shows one or two
things
1992. 1999. 1910. 1911. 1912.
Capital ..£51.910 £47.794 £47,377 £43,927 £59,444
Deposits .. 75,760 58,844 68,645 63,673 60,389
Mortgages.. 125.267 112,540 114,5«2 110,916 113,316
A total of £15,558 to cover the risks incidental
to £113,316 of advances doesn’t look great. Many
a slump has knocked more than 14 p.c. off the top
of property values. But, in the first place, it may
be fairly assumed that the society doesn't advance
anything like the full value of a property; and as
all or most of the advances are made on building-
society terms, the margin of safety is gradually
widened. For a steady lay-by, the society has a
lot to recommend it; and, anyway, it and its kind
do a great deal of good in showing what compound
interest can do for the little man as well as the big
one.

*This includes “premiums purchasing paid-up poli-
cies allocated from cash bonuses not drawn.”
(This includes “cash bonuses”—£l7,22l for
1911 and £19,512 for 1912.
In 1911 this society adopted a new practice, which
threw the table out of gear. Before that time it
(like others) didn’t include the annual bonus with
its expenditure on the one side, or the insurance
bought with the bonus additions on the other. The
new practice is, theoretically, right enough; but it
inflates the figures, and makes it useless to compare
some of this co.’s returns with those of other offices.
For instance, if the very rough and ready method is
adopted of dividing the premiums and other income
into the expenses to arrive at the expense rate, it is
obvious that the rate in this case will be curtailed,
because of the addition of the premium income de-
rived from bonuses uncollected in cash. To make a
comparison possible with other offices, this premium
income would have to be shown separately. And
there is really no reason why this office shouldn’t
adopt that practice, because its true expense rate is
not high. Take the rough and ready method first,
deducting, however, from the income the £18,796
received as premiums from cash bonuses:—
Expenses Compared with Total Income.
1902 .. £23 3 2 p.c. 1908 .. £25 17 lpc
1903 . . 22 3 9 „ 1909 . . 27 7 7
1904 .. 21 13 7 „ 1910 .. 23 16 4
1905 . . 22 4 7 „ 1911 .. 20 13 10
1906 . . 24 5 6 ~ 1912 . . 20 14 0
1907 . . 22 18 2 „
Probably that is a few pence over the mark, because
a few pounds may have been received as single pre-
miums apart from those derived from the cash
bonuses. But one wants to know, when consider-
ing an expense rate, what amount of new business
.was got; for whether a £2O rate is good or bad
depends upon the new business that had been roped
in, since to get a first premium costs anything up
to 20 times as much as to get the average renewal
premium. Following this paper’s practice of charg-
ing renewal premiums with a fixed 7 p.c. and
single-premium business with 3 p.c., it will be found
that the balance in this case is £24,459, which may
be taken as the cost of getting new business that
brought in £26,105 of premiums; so the new-
business expense rate was 93.6 p.c., whicn is dis-
tinctly moderate. Actually it was less, because in
a small office like this the cost of getting in re-
newal premiums is probably rather more than 7
p.c.; also, the investments cost something to look
after.
With the City Mutual the cost of looking after the
investments is no doubt higher per cent, than in
most offices, because it lays itself out to make ad-
vances in small as well as large amounts —in fact,
it seems to prefer the small amount and the security
which, though frequently quite good, takes some
looking into. The result is that last vear the earn-
ings of the funds averaged £5 9s. 6d.‘p.c. Further,
a profit of £5500 was made on the sale of invest-
ments. The insurance office, as a rule, doesn’t look
to make a profit on its investments. It wants safetv
above all things, and it aims at getting the best rate
of interest it can without risking its principal. A«
a matter of fact, even when it thinks it isn’t taking
a risk, it often takes something substantial in that
way, as the large amounts written off after the boom
20 years ago prove; and the banks that bought
Consols at or about par and now find them worth
£7O or so have perhaps modified their opinions
about gilt-edged securities. The City Mutual appar-
ently declines to be governed by any fixed rules. It
believes it has some brains, and it tries to use them
for the benefit of policy-holders. It is therefore to
some extent an investment co. as well as an insur-
ance office. “Debentures of public cos. figure in
the balance-sheet as an asset for £10,106; “stocks
and shares of public cos.” for £29,842. Mainly the
success of this kind of business depends upon the
keenness of the management. And though it has
succeeded so far, the management knows that acci-
dents may happen, wherefore the £5500 of profit
made on investments this year is wisely put to a
contingency fund, which now totals £15,500. But,
after all, the amount in the securities with a pos-
sible sting in their tail is small ; 17 p.c. of the
total assets are Government securities, 42 p.c. mort-
gages, 8 p.c. policy loans, and 13 p.c. freeholds.
The energy which the average insurance office de-
votes to getting big business figures thus goes in
this office in other directions. Besides chasing up
investments that give an extra 1 or 2 p.c., it sniffs
out its policy-risks with a good deal of care. For
some years past it has also gone to the length of
paying hospital fees in case of sickness. The policy-
holder who falls ill and fades away doesn't live to
pay a premium another day. Somehow or other it
certainly is a fact that the society's death rate is
exceptionally low, and that means bonuses. Insurance
bonuses flow in from three sources: (1) favorable
mortality experience, ('2') interest earnings in excess
of those assumed in the original calculations, (3)
low expense rate. From its excellent showing as to
(1) and (2) the City Mutual gives results far better
than would be expected by anyone who has got into
the bad habit of assuming that big business and
big bonuses mean the same thing.

jump in the expense rate:—

If the business had been run at the same cost last
half-year as it was three vears ago, the expenses
would have been £42,375 instead of £47,847—a
difference of £5472. Profits have not moved up
correspondingly, with the result that the bonus on
cash purchases, which was put up to 2s. 3d. in the £
in 1907, has been lowered for the past six months
to 2s. in the £. In addition shareholders draw
10 p.c. p.a. on their capital: but if reserves are
included the division represents only about 2£ p.c.
The bonus on purchases is solid; but there is nothing
at all flash about the investment. As a matter of
fact, practically nobody buys these shares as an
investment: the great majority of them are held in
ones and twos to qualify the holder to pass his
plate up for the bonus on purchases. Paid capital
is only £13,884; but as the co.’s trade is nearly all
for cash, and as nothing is nailed down in freeholds,
the liabilities are comparatively small —£12,950 on
mortgage and £43,566 to trade creditors, the bank
and shareholders. The total debt is much larger than
it was until recently; but something like £25,000
has of late been put into the improvement and fitting-
up of premises held on long lease. This asset is
being steadily and it seems adequately written down
out of profits. The increased costs are significant.
As was remarked here when this co.’s accounts were
post-mortemed six months ago—“ Last year and the
year before showed extra good results, because the
trade got a spurt on ahead of wages, etc. ; but the
spurt that wages, etc., have now put on will probably
outlast the spurt in trade, and that won’t be good for
the look of profits.” This is going to be true of
plenty of other concerns besides the Svdney Civil
Service Stores.

A great mistake, when erecting new works or re-
arranging old ones, is often made in constructing the
building and installing machinery ere looking round
for means of transport of the raw material, half-
manufactured and finished goods. Messrs. Aaolt
Bleichert and Co., of London, have issued various
catalogues and leaflets, illustrating and describing a
few of the many transport systems and plants which
they have supplied, and which show the solution of
the transport question and the advantages of em-
ploving such appliances. W. and J. Lempnere,
Melbourne and Sydney, are the Australian representa-
tives, and would be pleased to hear from those in-
terested. A specially trained engineer is retained to
consult and advise.*

Trade has been brisk at Newcastle; but this old-
established co. is drowsy. Its mine manager, Alex-
ander Ross, retired a while ago after nearly half a
century’s service; and John Wheeler, who got out
of the secretary’s chair a month or two back, had
been helping to look after the financial side for 43
years. It is that sort of co.—highly respectable,
but not up-to-date in some of its methods. Profits
suffer in consequence. Look at the change in things
in 10 years: —
Dec., 1902. Dec., 1912.
Liabilities £1,473 £6.902
Mining and fixed property . . 83,901 93,467
Liquid assets 91,417 75,561
That shrinkage in liquid assets is significant; it
suggests pretty strongly that the dividends have not
been fully earned. Admittedly, a lot of the liquid
assets that have disappeared have gone into fixed
property; but, unfortunately, the expenditure
hasn't produced more profits; and it is more than
possible that there ought to be a still further large
expenditure to put the mine on an equality with its
competitors.
One can pretty well guess at the size of
Sydney’s land-subdivision and building boom from
the fact that the Intercolonial Investment, Land and
Building Co., which in 1910 had a capital of
£51,000, has since the beginning of that year paid
its shareholders £49,036 in bonuses and added
£21,500 to its own reserves.
Batchelor and Tait, Ltd., who run a cold-
storage business in Sydney, lately issued their first
balance-sheet. Shareholders are not entitled to be
very jubilant about it. Starting with a deficit of
£6333 (goodwill, £6215), a trading loss of £l7l
has now to be added to that amount. Further, as
the auditors point out, “no depreciation has been
written off any of the assets of the co.” The paid
capital is £18,768; the liabilities, including £BllB
of a mortgage debenture debt, amount to £14,564.
To represent this money the co. has £ll,OOO in free-
hold and buildings, £14,491 in plant, and a few
pounds in sundries. The managing director says that
“we have more than carried out our estimate of busi-
ness forecasted at the commencement of the co.’
It is early yet, no doubt, to speak, but it is to be
hoped that the estimates of profits will also be “car-
ried out” in the future. Business alone isn’t much good
to the shareholders. Meantime the big burden of
debt (including a generally-dangerous mortgage-
debenture debt) must be viewed with a considerable
amount of anxiety.

Some years ago the Broken Hill Prop.
considered the question of manufacturing white lead ,
but nothing was done, and though Australia is one
of the world’s biggest lead producers, it has con-
tinued to import practically all its painters goods
from foreign parts. There seems a prospect of this
ridiculous anomaly being ended. A process (Banes s)
has been locally evolved for producing white lead
of so good a quality that painters have spoken with
enthusiasm about it. The product is very similar to
American sublimed white lead ; that is ® ay> JJ*
chief constituents are lead sulphate and lead oxide,
with a small quantity of lead sulphite and zinc
oxide —the last-mentioned constituent (which is no
drawback) being due to the fact th f* .®, rok ™ ?'
concentrates are used as the raw material Elabor
ate tests have been made of the pigment, and
covering qualities and resistance to sea-air are pro-
nounced first-class. The process is patented widely
and a co. is being formed in Sydney to S° lnt °
business. Every doubt as to the quality of the goods
having been set at rest, the future hangs now mainly
upon the cost of production ; and on the figures sub
Sed, that seems all right. The process of manu-
facture consists of feeding the ore (mill concern
trates) into a furnace through a blow-pipe flame, fed
with producer-gas and air under pressure. The par-
ticles pass into a hot oxidising atmosphere, charged
with sulphur from the ore, and the mineral is con-
verted thus into fumes, which are collected in cham-
bers The residues (gangue) fall into a coke bed in
the furnace. A S t he sulphur in the concentrates
fwhich are, of course, derived from sulphide ores)
w"ll supply most of the fuel, it ought to be a cheap
process • and the well-tested product proves that it
is effective. The experimental P la nt, as mentione

* * » *
Shares in the Great Cobar have had a
bad slump, and are now down to 58s. (£5 paid).
Nothing new has apparently occurred at the mine.
The primary trouble is the very heavy debenture and
other debt; the secondary trouble lies in high costs
and comparatively poor recoveries, which combine to
make it almost impossible for the properties to pay
anything to the shareholders after wear and tear
has been provided for and the debenture holders
attended to. There is a rumor that General-manager
Bellinger, who is out of the country, on leave, will
not return; but if there is anything in that yarn,
he has changed his mind since he left. Most likely
there is nothing in it.
Lloyd’s Copper, Burraga (N.S.W.), is
already up against its old trouble, viz., scarcity of
firewood. The recent rains have affected the new
tram line, so the mine and mill stopped from Friday
till Monday. Labor is somewhat scarce, and stop-
pages like this will not improve the supply, more
especially as winter is approaching and no reserve
stock of fuel is visible.
Some of the boom reports from the Ardle-
than tin field (N.S.W.) will make sad reading for
someone. For instance, one appeared in the Herald
giving the value of some 25 p.c. ore as “ £56 per
ton.” That’s £2 4s. Bd. per unit, or more than
ingot metal is worth landed in London.
* * * ■*
Captain Richard, late of Mount Morgan,
more or less settled down nowadays in Brisbane as
consulting engineer, has been having a hurried look
at Ardlethan. He says what nearly every other well-
informed mining man has said of the field—rich,
though small, surface bodies; permanency as yet un-
proved. As prospecting ventures he thinks, however,
that they are excellent, and he has big hopes that
some of them will make good.
* * * *
Significant remark from the report of
the visit of N.S.W. Minister for Mines Edden to
Ardlethan: “Mr. Pittman, Under Sec., visited the
field about five months ago, and since then only
about 10ft. of sinking has been done.” The specu-
lators who hold most of the ground know they have
something on and near the surface. It looks as
though they are not keen on probing the properties
to see whether there is anything of much value down
lower.
* * * *
As was expected after Leverrier, K.C., had
torn the B. H. Junction North’s witnesses to pieces,
the big Equity suit between that co. and its neighbor,
the Junction, has ended wholly in the Junction’s
favor, so far as Justice Rich can end it.
It was all about ventilation. The law says
that two neighboring mines may be required
to connect underground workings, and keep
the connection open for purposes of ventilation.
A connection was made between these two mines,
and the Junction North then, it is alleged, used fans
and turned the Junction shaft into an upcast: in
other words, it gave the Junction its bad air, if it
had any, and it seems to have a good deal. The
Junction put up doors to stop the current. The
Junction North people kicked at that, and brought
this action; and the Judge decided against them
with costs, and costs may be anything up to £4OOO.
There is pretty certain to be an appeal; but the
Judge seems to have had no doubts in his mind,
whatever anybody else may have.
* * * *
An important deal was concluded last week
when the Zinc Corporation took over the old Sunny
Corner mine (N.S.W.) from the people who got
command of it about a year ago. The new owners
will, it is understood, form a subsidiary co. to
work the once well-known property ; the capital will
probably be £225.000. It is 13 or 14 years since
the mine was worked; but that isn’t because it
wasn’t worth while. A verv exhaustive examination
has been made, and if the Zinc Corporation had not
exercised its option there were others ready to grab
it. The Sulphide Corporation, which was looking
on with much interest, has got the next best thing
available, having acquired a working option on the
Nevada, a smaller property in the same neighbor-
hood. _ »

Mount Elliott stock had a cataclysinal
fall from £6 17b. to £5 12s. 6d in. 24 hours and
of course, after the inner ring had got out, the
ordinary shareholder is told that Corbould is shutting
the mines and smelters down for three or four
months. One tale is that the Hampden Consols fire
is serious ; another that the Elliott itself must make
a “creep” safe. The subsidence at the 300 ft. level
or thereabouts last vear, was big enough to be called
a “creep” by Charters Towers miners, but Broken
Hill men would laugh at it. There are other tales
of poor ore at the lowest level, and these may be
truer than this alleged necessity to close down the
mine in order to make it safe. There are nearlv
148 000 of the 150.000 shares issued and this fall
means a lower market value of £375.000 on the
figures of a month ago. France was feeding heavily
on this stock a while back, and its table manners
were quite gentlemanly, the shares being tipped for
£2O on the more or less sanguine reading of the
general manager’s announcement that the mine could
pav present divs. for three or four years. The
Frenchman will nrobablv declare war on the sellers
from whom he bought and the wild tmsters; some

Republic lost Strasburg. Of course, it doesn t h irt
a producing mine to shut it down for four months.
Nevertheless, the Board’s statement that the finan-
cial position of the co. is so strong that its interests
will be practically unaffected. is so much guff.

A circular which tells a tale of struggle:
“The directors of the Great Fitzroy Mines, Ltd.,
have decided that the existing option to share-
holders over the unissued shares shall be extended
until May 31, 1914.” Alas, there are so many once-
optimistic shareholders who have regretfully come
to the conclusion that their affection for big low-
grade copper deposits was misplaced.
* * * *
Mount Morgan produced 137,1690 z. of
gold (fine) last year, which makes it the most im-
portant producer of the yellow metal in Queensland.
Charters Towers makes a good second with 98.3720 z.
( £417,857). For the past six months Mount Mor-
gan has treated 145,643 tons ore for a return of
4441 tons copper and 60,5020 z. gold. The best re-
turn was in November (at the close of the half-year:
855 tons copper and 13,0790 z. gold. The lowest
return was in the following month, which gave 590
tons copper and 77480 z. gold. For the half-year
ended November 31, 1912, Mount Morgan produced
5004 tons of copper. The base metal was sold for
forward delivery, at boom prices, and every effort
was made to force production. The copper produced
in December, January and February was 1923 tons,
containing 27,0440 z. gold ; rverage, 14.060 z. of fine
gold per ton of copper, so the latter at £6O per
ton is a shade more valuable than the gold. There
are very few mines which produce nearly equal
values of gold and copper like that.
* * * *
Papuan Brilliant and Victoria, Charters
Towers, is crushing another lot of stone from the
Moonstone. Shareholders keep their pecker up on
the strength of the last aggregate return of £6013
from 546 tons and dividends amounting to £2400.
From the same shoot the New Queen Cross, which
is a close neighbor, crushed 425 tons for £4754.
So far, this hanging-wall reef, off the Moonstone, has
given an average of £ll 17s. per ton from 971
tons. Encouraged by these results, the S.E. Day
Dawn is driving on a formation within its Persever-
ance lease towards the Cross. This drive is at a
vertical depth of 120 ft., and has some 600 ft. to go
to reach the boundary. The New Queen Cross shaft
is to be deepened when the new haulage shaft —which
follows the underlay of the reef —is completed.
Trucks will be run up the shaft to the surface.
Prospects appear sound enough, but it must be re-
membered that these Moonstone workings are all
very shallow and the reef irregular. The revival on
the Moonstone is due to the discovery of a com-
paratively rich reef branching off into the hanging-
wall, and its continuation in length and depth is
highly speculative.
* * * *
The Crooked Creek mine, on the Chilla-
goe field, owned by E. B. Torpy, has a small lead
mill; and plans have been prepared for a zinc
plant to handle the tailings. A flotation process is,
of course, contemplated.
“Brightite”: The condemnation of gold-
dredging, especially as regards the Bright (Vic.) dis-
trict, goes too far, as a rule. I ioin with those
who object to the destruction of good land along the
Ovens flats at Porepunkah, Eurobin and Mvrtleford;
but the damage done at the two first-named places
is almost entirely due to one co., which has proved
a sad failure. This is accounted for by the high price
of land acquired and excessive managerial and other
costs. On a capital of £66.500 the co. has paid
shareholders back in dividends onlv £37,469. and
the end is in sight now. From Porepunkah upwards,
along both the Ovens and Buckland valleys, very
little harm has been done; in fact, for many miles
rather the reverse is the case, as thousands of old
shafts have been filled and the country made fairly
level. From the last available report of the Mines
Department, the following returns show the amount
of gold won, capital invested and dividends paid since
the industry was established. Several small concerns,
which are “sick,” or have passed out, are omitted,
but the figures quoted will serve to show the im-
portance of the industry to the people of the dis-
trict: — .

No one is enthusiastic about radium min-
± the ironstone-carnotite lode near
ment. The Radium Prop., whose lease adjoins, speaks
glowingly of the re-concentration experiments. But
it seems ridiculous to value the stuff at £l2O per
ton on the assumption that so many milligrams of
pure radium bromide will be produced and sold at
what may be regarded as famine prices. The purity
of the radium product and the price received for it
have to be firmly established before the value of con-
centrates can be estimated.
From Manila (Philippine Islands) : r lhe
Paracale River Dredging Co. was formed in Mel-
bourne to mine three parcels of land, one held by
the Philippine Gold Dredging Co., the other the
Mellville claim, and the third the Nueva California
group of claims. The title to the Mellville claim is
now disputed. The locators of the ground hold the
land under the mining laws in force in the 1 hilip-
pine Islands, and it is this title which has been
passed on to the Melbourne co. But the original occu-
pants of the lard claim to have old titles from the
Spanish Government. According to decisions ot the
Philippine courts, confirmed by the Supreme Court
of the United States, such titles are perfectly sound.
As the people who claim the old titles cannot come
to terms with the people who transferred the land to
the Melbourne co., the Paracale River Co. will be
under a cloud until the question of titles is settled.
Further, in the Paracale River District many more
disputes are pending as to titles, and much valuable
property will be tied up indefinitely, so it is a good
district to leave alone.

A
WOMAN’S
LETTER
Sydney, March 17, 1913.
My Dear Arini, —
Governor Strickland stepped ashore at
Farm Cove on one of Sydney’s most dazzling
days—a morning all blue and gold, whipped
by a few cool catspaws that just lifted the
stacks of bunting, then dropped them on to
their moorings with a flirty shake. The offi-
cial landing, as a dressy side-show, was
much better stage-managed than is usual
at these episodes in the lives of Governors.
The Encounter banged its guns as the
Governor’s steam wherry cut across the sap-
phire waters of Farm Cove. The police
band blared forth a cheerful supply of mixed
music till the new Excellency got his feet
on the creaking pontoon, when “God Save”
smote the listening earth.
Acting-Town Clerk Layton, draped in a
long black toga, with a trencher-board
perched on his benign brow, dashed forward
with an official tome under one arm, and,
presumably, took the new arrival’s name
and address before he had time to defend
himself. Mayor Cocks, who carries the
ermine of office with the air of a duke in a
congenial billet, stood bare-headed to disclose
the identity of a long string of aldermen.
Fremier Jim IVlcGowen, very carefully
groomed, extended the glad hand, and his
friends in office also circled round the brand-
new Strickland with friendly intent. Mini-
ter Trefl6 stuck to bowler hat, but two or
three of the others, including Premier Jim,
beamed under the brim of shiny things in
silk.
• , Edeline » by the way, is not so well
in health as her friends would wish her to
be, so she stuck to the Osterley right through
riom the TV est. This knocked one possible
complication as to precedence off Melbourne
Government House’s list of worries last
week.
Governor Strickland’s first public utter-
ance in N.S.W. shows that he is a person of
tact He mentioned our “unrivalled har-
bor, and followed his appreciation of life
under the Southern Cross with the post-
script that his youngest-born is a little Aus-
tralian.
toddling down the stone steps
to the official landing-place, in their scarlet
petticoats, made coy snatches at their red
robes, as the breeze blew the garments away
from their ankles. Horsehair curls dangled
round the face of Justice, as the wearers
tripped under the little awning, like relics of
mediaeval splendor when barons bold gnawed
the breast of a chicken under the watchful
eye of red-robed retainers.
At the swearing-in the Gentleman Helps
were the glory of the autumn morn, which
sent shafts of golden sunshine on their heavy
buHion trappings. Captain Forbes, of a
Highland regiment, was clad in plaid trou-
sers, fairly tight, and of a pattern that was
large enough to encase each leg before it fin-
ished one Highland square of tartan. His
manly chest was firmly buttressed inside a
scarlet jacket, hung with gold cables thick
enough to tether a mailboat to a wharf stan-
chion. A red top-knot fluttered on his In-
dian helmet. He walks from the waist, after
the manner of an infantry regiment, which
being a good deal on its legs, is trained to
style in moving its feet.
The slender and rather delicate-looking
Private Sec., Victor Hood, who was so popu-
lar during his previous Victorian sprint in
that capacity, wore his head in a sombre
black-cocked hat at the swearing-in episode.
Very neat, cutaway coat-tails and raven in-
expressibles completed his lingerie.
Mrs. Forbes, wife of one of the new Gov-
ernor s staff, Captain Ronald Forbes, created
a commotion on the Osterley by the exten-
sive size and variety of her wardrobe. Other
dressy ladies did their best to keep up with
the dashing lorbes dame, who is tall, hand-
some, and wears a fine color in her cheeks,
but their achievements, according to a passen-
ger who was an eye-witness of the sartorial
struggle, were not up to their hopes. The
Osterley s decks were, however, sw’ept each
evening by new and gorgeous specimens of
the latest train—a thin, curly streak of
diaphanous drapery that makes a note of
interrogation on the floor.
Sydney is so full for the great Easter
horn and hoof parade that the question of
hotels with elastic-sides is again before the
public mind. The architect who can invent
automatic beds that will climb up to the
roof (which should be flat) when you press
a button, and tavern walls that will extend
snven the Pressure of a few extra tons of
lugg&gc, will only have to claim a vast for-
tune to get the reward that is awaiting his
ingenuity. 6
For the first time since Federation, Sydney
is shorn of the money-scattering presence of
a Governor-General, and the shops are emit-
ting cries of distress at the dearth of large
orders for frills, hats, socks and frocks. The
sudden access of thrift on the part of the
Smart section of local Society is probably
due to two causes. Some of our better-off
dames have only recently returned from an
extended gallop through foreign countries
and heavy purses of Australian-made cash.
Others are packing up for a flutter in Lon-
don, Monte Carlo and similar places, which
supply every incentive to the suffering rich
* xca through their surplus coin at the rate
or xoU an hour. Hence a generally flat ob-
servance of the Easter dress parade, so far
as huge private displays of pelf are con-
cerned.
The and his lad y> to say nothing
the OMalley and Prime Minister Fisher,
nad the luck to escape the sudden and rare
outbreak of much water at Canberra. The
luckless Light Ilorse were probably about
bait-dry for the opening blast of the chris-
tening band-music, having been almost
washed out of their camp a few days before
the Federal baby blinked in the presence
of sub-royalty.
Australia had a lucky escape from the
h amily Herald name Myola, which was
threatened. The native inhabitants of the
bush hamlet gave a whoop of joy when
they heard the familiar Can-berra escape
from Lady Denman’s keeping; they
hadn’t taken to a Federal city too
kindly at best, and “Myola” would have
been the last straw. Queanbeyan is the
centre of a conservative “oldest family”
sheep-patch, where the original pure merinos
bleated in rich folds which they believed
were theirs in perpetuity. Of one dead-and-
gone autocrat, who once ruled at Duntroon,
old settlers used to relate that he occasion-
ally objected, with a stockwhip, to mere
yokels wearing hob-nailed boots. If they
walked across young sheep-fodder in the
Duntroon pastures, they had to mind their
ways. The intrusion of artisans, builders
and Labor politicians into these once exclu-
sive. mutton-lord’s paddocks has made the
foolish Federal infant mighty unpopular in
its own teething ground.
The death of Mr. 11. Marcus Clark, at
Mount Wilson, where he was full of the joy
of his new possessions up that way, is one
of the unexpected strokes of the Man with
the Scythe. Ten or 12 days ago I saw him
motoring along the hilly steeps between
Mount Wilson and the little railway station
at Bell. A tall, rather slim man, looking
much younger than his years, which were in
the early ’so’s, Mr. Clark seemed good for
another half century of life. Founder and lead-
ing member of the well-known firm of
Marcus Clark, Ltd., he recently made his
country home on Mount Wilson, one of the
beauty spots of N.S.W., as I told you last
week. As he was genial and open-handed,
the inhabitants up that way expected a re-
vival of the old-time bustle. He re-named
the home he occupied, and practically re-
built Sefton Hall. Rumor said that he also
contemplated giving each of his three sons
a property up in those fern-draped heights.
Prof. Warren, of Sydney Uni., just back
in the Osterle.v, was taken for exercise on
the decks of that craft by a stupendous bull-
dog. Whether this is the priceless canine
which habitually leads the professor about in
his N.S.W. holiday jaunts I don’t know.
Glasgow gave the wanderer an LL.D., and
he spent part of his absence from Sydney
peering into Egypt’s water supply.
The veteran artist Piguenit has just
passed his 76th milestone. He won the first
helping of the Wynne art prize over 20
years ago. Piguenit has a clever way of
handling indigo blue and faint shades of
mountain mist. The National Gallery owns
one of his finest samples of putting illusive
distance on mere canvas —a big oil-painting
of Kosciusko.
G. R. Taylor, the six-foot father of Syd-
ney lawyer Dolf Taylor, a mere dwarf of
6ft. sin., took a holiday from his job of
reigning over Dubbo last week, and he and
his son cast lengthy shadows in Castlereagh-
street. The Dubbo man is over the 70-year
mark, and was born in Elizabeth-street, Syd-
ney. His father, George Taylor—also a long
man if not as thick a one—was a pedagogue
in Fort-street, and opened St. Philip’s Gram-
mar School in 1848, or thereabouts. The
Dubbo king prizes the big silken banner
which flaunted over the St. Philip’s school
foundation stone-laying by Bishop Brough-
ton. He has lived in his western kingdom
for 50 years or so, and has for 40 years been
president of Dubbo hospital—a position he
hangs on to with great tenacity, resenting
hotly any attempt to put him out of work.
The French-Canadian singer. Eva Gauthier,
who blew into this city from the “near East”
the other week, made a sensational success
in her recent concerts in the Concordia Hall.
With Laurence Godfrey Smith as pianist,
and Eva warbling in full-throated mezzo-
soprano lays, these first music-bursts of the
concert season belong to the infrequent, all-
star class. Harold Whittle’s reliable fingers
were on the piano as accompanist to the
Voice.
The new German hall, the Concordia, is
probably better in its acoustics than any
other concert patch in town. In this it beats
the vast Albert Hall (London), which until
lately supplied an echo that used to rap back
maddeningly to voice and instrument. Re-
cently the authorities have circumvented
this fiend, by tying it up in overhead wires.
In its decorations the Concordia stage is a
welcome relief to the dull dais which British
taste associates with Art. A little on the
gaudy side, perhaps; still, its Noah’s Ark
door, cut out like a doll’s-house exit, goes
well enough with the gilded proscenium
Behind this door flitted the German waiter.
As he is German, and wears a short, white'
coat (with other garments), I suppose he is
a waiter. On Thursday night he mostly
waited on the English language, which he
didn’t understand. Two candles flickered in
the artist’s pantry or small loose-box. Laur-
ence Godfrey drew the attention of the Teu-
ton to the eclipse of the electric light in this
retreat dedicated to the powder-puff and
hair-brush, and Mi«« Gauthier addressed
him in rousing Dutch. But the light failed
just the same.
In consequence, perhaps, ef her hurried
descent into the Dutch language, and in part,
also, because she was nervous, the Voice met
a frog in its throat and didn’t attack the first
extract from “II Barbiere” in its opening
bars with the perfect precision of the singer’s
otherwise faultless style from that moment
till the end of the evening. Dramatic, and
obviously born to welter in heavy operatic
roles, Eva Gauthier is the finest artist, in her
way, Sydney has heard since another French-
Canadian, Paul Dufault, roused this town to
a tumult of applause. The Gauthier vocal
organ is very young, with the round, rich
notes of some feathered warbler singing its
matin song to the early worm. Unpuffed
before her arrival, and practically unknown
to Australians, the charming young woman
lifted a high C at her first concert in so cap-
able a manner that it caught the musical ear
of Henry Bracy, who knows the vocal art
from A to Z and home again.
I ianist Uaurence Godfrey Smith, after
having kept the pledge to abstain from teach-
ing for 21 months, has returned to Sydney
even better than his former best.

big legal firm of Minter, Simpson and Co.

Under the high roof-tree of the Kodai-
Ivanal Observatory Mary Proctor noticed
many things, including spiders, during her
stay with Mrs. Evershed. In the middle of
an interview with the telescope, a huge
spider descended from his residential wlb
and made a careful trek for the star-lady’s
shoe-leather. “I jumped aside,” savs she
and forgetting that the Hindu won’t kill
these creatures, expected the attendant to
aid- Instea d, he smiled as the
weird thing took the centre of the floorway!
and said softly, ‘These things are most
precious . So they are on that mountain
top. Their web is the finest line an astrono-
mer can stretch under the glass of his tele-
cope, and he uses it to make two lines that
cross m the centre. When web ran out
Director Evershed was once driven to use
a hair from his wife’s locks. She is quite
a beauty in a gentle Madonna style, and
gladly gave her fine silky strand to help
science. But it couldn’t compete with the
gossamer thread from a web, and was only
a desperate remedy. So the spider seems
conscious of his importance at Kodai. and
stalks leisurely across the premises, unafraid.
Ihe Director-General of Indian Observa-
tories paid a visit To the Eversheds’ moun-
tain bunsralow while I was there. He was
delightful company, and we had a joyful
chatter about the starry hosts. India is'the
land of Spoilt Women, by the way. The
most ordinary-looking European woman who
stays at remote bungalows is almost over-
whelmed with polite attentions from the
majority/ ma ”' Who is in a mcek

“Tamumu”: Recent experiences in two of
them have convinced me that Maoriland
hospitals want shaking up. In the two in-
stitutions I refer to—one in a large ci v
the other in a small country town—con
sumptive patients were treated in the samp
ward with medical cases of a different
nature. It is true that the sufferers from
phthisis slept on the verandah when space
there was available; when it wasn’t thev
camped in the ward with the other inmates
In both hospitals I saw tubercular patients
expectorating over the verandah, although
they were provided with sputum mugs. In
certain Cold Country sanatoria the ’ con-
sumptive who expectorates anywhere except
into vessels provided for that purpose is
severely spoken to, and a second default
means summary discharge. It should be so
here also. Another matter that absolutely
shrieks for attention is that table utensils
(knives, spoons, mugs, etc.) used bv
the victims of phthisis are washed
up with the other ward dishes. In
the big city hospital the washing-up
was done by convalescents. Often the
water was only lukewarm—of a tempera-
ture quite inadequate to destroy the tubercle
bacillus. The tucker weapons of the con-
sumptives were differentiated from those oi
the other patients by a red thread attached
to them, but no attempt was made to cleanse
them separately! Altogether, if these hos-
pitals are typical, then the unfortunate
Maorilander who is undergoing treatment
in one of them for some other ailment, and
who is predisposed to phthisis, is in exactly
the right place to contract the disease
which, in such cases, he is usually in most
dread of.
“G.V.W.”: The strike of the Maoriland
slaughtermen ended as usual. Receiving
2os. per hundred, they struck for 30s. The
cos. offered 275. 6d., and called for men at
that figure. _ They also guaranteed that
learners, until proficient, would be paid not
less than 15s. per day. The response was
good. Just here, the “slim” leaders of the
men fell into. a blunder of the Waihi tyne.
On their advice a number of the old hands
went back as learners. Killing less than 50
sheep per day, they were paid 155., thus
getting more than the 30s. per 100 asked
for._ The leaders made a public boast of
their cleverness, whereupon the union opera-
tors were sorted out and promptly sacked.
Disgusted, the South Island men broke away
and w r ent back to work on the co.’s terms.
The others, having plenty of time on their
hands, are searching the dictionary for ap-
propriate terms for the leaders who advised
the foolishness. In between times they are
searching the Wanted column for signs of
something that will bring in bread, to say
nothing of beer.
From an Australian in England :
Llovd George’s Insurance Act, if it has done nothing
else, has unearthed two ghastly facts: first., the unbe-
lievable number of people in Merrie England who
have never in their lives been able to afford to see
a doctor; second, the callousness of charity-run,
doctor-owned hospitals. The day the Act got to
work, the doctors, at any rate in the poorer districts,
were over-run with the halt, maimed and wounded,
who paraded sores and excrescences that would have
done credit to a Barcelona mendicant. “When did
you last have this attended to ?” would shriek the
astounded medico. Mostly they never had had it
attended to, and never would have had but for the
legislation which is still a red rag to all “right-
think'ng” people. But while these poor wretches
were learning something for the first time concerning
antiseptic methods, the “charity” hospitals were re-
fusing to dress any patient who came under the
Act; no matter how long he had been attending,
he was turned from the door, and told to shift for
himself. An Act of this size can hardly come into
being without some sl'ght confusion, and poor, invalid
out-cases are not the ones to battle with it; conse-
quently a few wounds got septic, a few people died,
and the Right Thinkers shrieked again. Somehow
no one remembered to hang the doctors who were
responsible.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, March 17, 1913.
My Dear Tarrie, —
The Fuller lady supplied a domestic touch
to the frivolous group in the blue plush box
at Flemington on the day of the Champion
Stakes. The vice-regal youngsters had been
to tea with fair hair flying loose over blue
serge coats. When the tea procession arrived
back at the pen, her Ex. seized the manes of
her progeny and plaited them tightly, tying
them up with ribbons fished from some secret
maternal pocket. The deft nursery turn,
given in full view of the crowd, gave great
joy to the lawn walkers.
The polo outbreak finished in a wintry
rainstorm, and the Denman lady, peeping out
from Polar mufflings of fur, presented the
Tournament Cup trophies to the mud-spat-
tered but happy men of Camperdown. The
Fuller lady, Lady Barttelot and Mrs. Frank-
ly* 1 supplied applause that was filtered
through fur motoring gloves, and a neighbor-
ing group of large fur motor-coats contained
Mrs. Russell Grimwade, Mrs. Alister Clarke,
Mrs. Faulkner and Mrs. Boulivant. A choice
assortment of young Western District girls
helped to cheer the district teams.
Strickland, the new cocked hat of the Ma
State, showed up at the fray for a chilly
hour. I don’t think he polos, but he would
rather be on a horse than in a motor almost
any time ; and his girls are the same.
Mrs. Garnett, wife of one of the British
Commissioners at present asking questions
m Maoriland, and mother of Mrs. Geoff.
Syme, is living in a cyclone of suburban en-
tertainment. Mrs. de Bavay, of Kew, gathered
all the joyful neighbors to auction bridge last
week, and the Wrixon dame held a reception
m the new chum’s honor at the Riverside
house. During race week, Mrs. Agar Wynne
buttered the hospitable muffin at the Quamby.
For this function the visiting lady rustled
in clothes that thrilled all beholders.
The social world at present simply seethes
with farewell parties to intending travellers.
The functions are all of the tea-and-bun
order. The nobility and gentry of Toorak
whiz here and there “doing” two or three
farewells in an afternoon, and getting bun-
struck and tealogged in the process. On Fri-
day, Mrs. Dill invited her friends to drink a
parting cup with Mrs. Mcßae, who, with her
elder girleen, is hurrying off to England on
hubby’s arm. He intends to mix a little holi-
day with a certain amount of business.
Owing to the disappearance of the first-
class gangway fully a half-hour before the
Medina cast off, a crowd 3000 strong strug-
gled together upon the planks of the Port
Melbourne pier in a brave attempt to reach
the remote decks. A few said or shrieked
good-bye, and fled like rabbits from the mad-
ding crowd and the railway trucks. The
mourning Scott widow, hidden behind a sable
veil and a rampart of floral gifts, was an
object of interest to the less preoccupied of
the crowd. One large area of the deck was
sacred to Western District nobility, who lined
up clannishly to wave the last farewells.
There were Tom Millar’s handsome wife
(daughter of Toorak’s late sporting parson,
Fellows), the W. Cumming matron and her
sister, Mrs. McKellar. All three were laden
with presents and flowers. The younger
Baird sister waved a white-gloved hand at
the elder Miss Baird, who is to join her in
England in May.
Last Friday evening, the Quamby Club
spread itself in a musical au revoir to the
Fuller lady, who shortly departs with her
small family to England, where the young-
sters will get their educational drill. Her
Ex., attended by Help Curwen, was received
on the doormat by Mrs. Baldwin Spencer and
Mrs. Sherwood, and by them loaded with a
huge vegetable offering on behalf of the club.
The Rennie sisters, wearing their nicest
smiles, fluttered with mamma in the back-
ground. Her Ex., in silvery attire, with a
tiara skewered on her golden locks, was con-
ducted to the reception rooms, where jets of
osprey, shot up in every direction amongst
the lilies and roses. The music was good
Hugh Hardy (a nephew of Miss Hood) lifted
up a pretty tenor, and Maggie Sterling
polled the profoundly devotional “God Be
With You.” The accompaniment was sup-
plied by Hattenbach and Amadio.
Mrs. Power is taking her Reggie to Eng-
land, and their South Yarra home, father
Rupert s present to the young matron, is
now to let. Mrs. Reggie has been down in
the dumps since Mother departed, so she
and bubb - v and her pet dog are off to join
the Grey-Egerton party. There is just a
chance that our only home-grown baronet
may accompany his daughter part of the
way. He has returned from Papua, and
will presently join his lady at Cairo or Monte
Carlo or somewhere.
Mrs. Robert Chirnside recently held some
sort of gangway party to say au revoir to
her sister, Mrs. Clarke, who is one of the
fashionables booked for foreign travel.
The Vivian Highett chick is just now a
popular target for tender farewells. She goes
to join her parents in London.
A Lniversity Hostel for women is the next
deserving object that holds out the confident
bazaar paw for charity. Queen’s College has
presented the ground, and the hostel to be
built is intended to house 300 blue-stockings.
Irinity Hostel, the only place of the kind,
shelters a mere 20, and students attending
advanced ’Varsity lectures have to go out
into the highways and byways for board and
lodging. The Denman lady will open a fair
in aid of the proposed building on April 11,
when the Town Hall will be transformed
mto an old English town of the time of
Chaucer. She-brigands will dress to match
the scenery. Mrs. Peterson is drilling her
orchestral forces in ancient English music,
and the sweet, old-fashioned strains will help
the painless extraction of cash.
The Repertory Theatre Club did homage
to Oscar Asche and his Lily with an after-
noon party at the Austral Salon. Mrs.
Alfred Cornish, a charming amateur, was the
dove-grey hostess for the afternoon, and G.
H Kmbbs made a speech of welcome to the
Shakspeanan producer. Lesly Madden, with
smoothly-brushed hair and very shaggy bear
furs, came along with Mrs. Norman Army-
tage. Ex-President Wrixon’s lady, Mrs.
Knox and Mrs. Gurner herded with the
Severely Intellectual wing of the party. Vida
Goldstein, with a Votes-for-Women gleam in
her eye, looked brisk in short-haired furs.
Long-maned Shakspearian mummers flocked
round and sipped tea to the rippling of
music from a far corner. Lily Brayton, who
wore cherry-colored trirpmings and spread-
ing clothes with fur margins, was hemmed
in by women all the time.
Last week J. L. McMillan, of the Lands
Department, sealed his matrimonial bond
with old John Zevenboom’s grand-daughter
—the fair nightingale whom he lured from
the nursing staff at the Homoeopathic. The
newly-weds are now honeymooning at Gipps-
land Lakes. The officers of the Lands De-
partment, with secretary Macgibbon in the
chair, speechified the bridegroom-elect on the
eve of his translation, and presented him
with various costly eating anoliances.
Singer Rebottaro’s sister Aida, a pretty,
black-eyed _ girl with an Italian style of
coquetry, is engaged to Mr. John Gargure-
vich.
Louis D’Antoine, an indent merchant of
this city, is engaged to Norah Field, a hand-
f 0 ™® -Tasmanian she-squatter who does a
lot of Toorak visiting. The poor girl de-
veloped measles just about the time the
announcement was published, so she is
receiving all congratulations through a reek
of disinfectants.
Alice Jackson, daughter of the Collins-
street doctor, and Bobby Bruce got through
their wedding with the record quiet for a
Sassiety joining. The bride faced the altar
m a natty blue tailor-made, with her hair
tucked away under a smart little black trav-
elling hat, whilst two small nieces held any
extra paraphernalia such as gloves and
flowers. After the sedate ceremony Dr. Jack-
son motored a handful of friends back to
his loorak house, to clink a glass with the
newly-weds.
wMi e - slie the elder son of squatter
Wilham, of Mount Talbot, Balmoral, is
booked to marry in the near future pretty
Miss Flowers, of the same township.
Dot Noseda, of the Bert Bailey co., has
started a new collection of trinkets. The
last lot, valued at f 100, disappeared with
the family teapot when the burglars called.
Uie lady is now wearing the first of the new
senes. It is a sparkling engagement ring.
Ernest Cecil Adams, of Auckland, is the
lucky man.
The condemning of Mawson and six com-
rades to an unexpected and extra year’s
imprisonment in the South Polar ice seems
directly attributable to an extraordinary
event not on the programme. The Aurora
was lying anchored at Mawson’s base, and
in the expedition secretary’s words—•
The dogs seeing the ice cliffs in close proximity
howled piteously to go ashore. Penguins and seals
were discerned on the rocks. Later a strong gale
blew and the windlass being negligently left open
the heaviest cable glided into the water, and not
having been made fast, left us dependent on a much
lighter cable. This was a serious loss, as events
subsequently proved.
The loss of this anchor caused the others
to go. The Aurora had arrived to find
Mawson and two others unaccountably ab-
sent. Not daring to delay dashing to the
rescue_ of Wild’s party, 1600 miles away,
the skipper left the main base a day before
Mawson staggered in with the news that
Ninnis and Mertz were dead. A wireless
recalled the Aurora, but a gale of wind was
howling when she returned, and without
anchors the skipper could do nothing but
leave the leader and six volunteers to
another year’s gaol in the frozen South.
By strenuous work, Wild’s party was reached
in time. Mawson didn’t allow for such an
extraordinary event as the “negligent” loss
of the Aurora’s anchors —a happening which
would have been quite inexcusable on even
a lumber schooner in the bay.
Cisneros and her Count have been figur-
ing hugely in American papers—but not
in concert notices. The pair were asked to re-
tire from the Plaza Hotel Cafd, and finally did
it to the accompaniment of much wrathful lan-
guage. It appeared that the large lady
dressed in a riding habit, strolled into the
Cato for a cup of tea. She was told, with
all the subservience of a doubled-up waiter,
that they did not serve people in riding
dress. The lady expostulated; then her
husband came along, and expostulated some
more. But the uproar was in vain. The
rules of the Caf£ could not be broken. Still
the Cisneros couple refused to retreat
until the argument had degenerated into a
plain, unlovely row.
On Saturday, the disgracefully rich George
Fairbairns dealt themselves a bridge party,
by way of welcoming son Clive and his bride,
just back from a Tasmanian honeymoon.
The function also served as a farewell to
daughter Mrs. Stewart Balmain, who is
gathering up her young family and steam-
ing off to India in the wake of her military
husband.
The Mount Schank Clarkes, who have now
adopted Melbourne as a place of abode,
finished up the race-week frivols with a
smart, exclusive dance, which was a riot of
extravagant chiffons. Mamma, big and
handsome in white brocade, made a foil for
the two slender girls, who pumphandled
guests on either side of her. Roma had
cloth of silver panniers on her cream satin
clothes, while Vanda, dark as a gipsy, had
a startling corsage of flesh pink gauze in a
black satin dress. Little sister Nesta was
allowed in to the party, and floated about
in pink roses and blue chiffon. Aide Cur-
wen and Secretary Wilberforce escorted the
Barttelot lady to the frivol. Rupert Car-
rington brought himself, and Mrs. George
Chirnside brought the very latest thing in
coiffures.
Cattle King Kidman’s daughter, after the
brief taste of wedded joys usually allowed
to. a sailor’s bride, is wandering about
this city in a lonely and rather clothes-
destitute condition. Her tons of trousseau
are all at sea. They were sent afloat
by her lieutenant-husband, wbo, when sum-
moned back to duty on the destroyer War-
rego, wanted to save his bride all the luggage
bother, she being under contract to follow
on immediately. However, the mind that
directs the doings of cruisers is about as
variable .as the moon —not to mention
woman in the matter. The little Warrego
was duly ordered to Hobart for a stay; but
the word was soon given to heave up and
away. Which accounts for Mrs. Nelson
Clover trying to dress on the contents of a
suit case or so.
The Rubber Edwards pair recently set out
to motor to Sydney, but, taking a round-
about route which included Canberra, their
car had a breakdown, and they completed
their journey by train. Mrs. Edwards was
then on the sick-list, and had ever so many
medicine bottles, which she shelved while she
tried some early-morning surfing. After a
little of this treatment, she took those bottles
one by one and threw them two miles out
to sea.
Margaret Cooper has been sending long,
copy-plate screeds to friends she made in
Australia. The latest are full of South
African tour gossip. She murmurs rather
pettishly that South Africans are not vet
educated up to the art of songs at the
piano, so the veldt journey must be a trying
one.
Norman Bayles has tripped over to the
Speck to help nurse his sick wife. She was
at Mount Wellington, sampling its special
brand of icy air. when she ran on the rock of
ill-health, and has been stranded there for
many days.
The Rupert Clarke lady writes enthusi-
astically to some Melbourne friends of her
frocks and doings. The frocks are the usual
“dreams”; the lady who wears them is at
Monte Carlo, after “doing” the Continent
in company with a most distinguished
woman, whose illustrious name I have mis-
laid. . At “Monte” she. awaits her Rupert,
who is busy in Australia at the moment.
“Char” Crespin, the local, curly-haired
young warrior who is attached as A.D.C.
to State Governor Fuller, finds some of his
Gentleman Help duties most embarrassing.
On the last day of the Flemington races,
two fair-haired Fuller children clung lovingly
to his hands during the lawn parade. The
Captain, who is a young man of the aggres-
sively bachelor type,. blushed a fine scarlet
and stalked along like a petrified martyr
with his two clinging charges. A married
Help would have done that nursery duty
easily.
Hal Lewis, whose External Affairs experi-
ence here helped him to the billet, is now
the London agent, or something, for the
Chinese Government.
The Austral Salon, awakened from its
summer doze, started proceedings in a busi-
nesslike manner with a grocery tea, pro-
ceeds of which will be used to sustain the
Salonettes during the coming season. A
few musical items were perpetrated. The
collection will be broached at the forth-
coming welcome to members Thompson,
Boyd and Straub, who have been fluttering
over the Old and New Worlds.
The new Golf Links at Gisborne, which
are to rival the Macedonian caddie-walk on
the other side of the Mount, are to be de-
clared open by Lady Denman on April 5.
All golf links are distinctly open, but the
openness of these is to be affirmed as afore-
said. By the way, has any personage ever
opened the sea?
Australia isn’t the only place where a
Socialistic Government interferes with the
liberty of the subject and kicks Private En-
terprise in 14 places. In Paris they are
actually talking about putting a legal limit
on woman’s dress allowance. The horrid
idea has not even the merit of being new:
it dates back to the dim, man-tyrannicai
ages when real chivalry was a lot deader
than it is now.

CHILD WAS CHOKING.
“For the first six months of my daughter’s life
she was continually attacked with croup, and was
often black in the face from choking,” writes Mrs.
Elizabeth Davies, 19 Browning Street, South Yarra
(Vic.). “We did not know what to do, the lodge
medicine having failed. I then gave her Chamber-
lain’s Cough Remedy, and we have had no trouble
since.”*
Mr W. U. Goodall, of Messrs. T. M. Goodall and
00., Ltd., is back from England. He has been
absent for about nine months on a business trip
that included a tour of the Continent. A good deal
of his time was spent in visiting the works of the
large machinery manufacturers for which his firm
ff e , a ?, en * :s * n Australia. In this connection Mr.
Goodall went thoroughly into machinery matters
with Messrs. Garrett’s, Ltd., Broadbent, Ltd.,
makers of machine tools; and John Stirk’s, of lathe
fame, were also visited. The trip resulted i'n
several new agencies being added to Messrs. Good-
all s list. Among these are tbe Campbell Gas Engine
Co. ; Messrs. Booth and Co., makers of steam and
electric overhead travelling cranes; the Yorkshire
Boiler Co, ; and Burymann’s “Hanos” engine pack
ings,*
