POLITICAL
POINTS
Australian sawmill hands are up against
trouble. They are paid from £2 9s. to £3
per weekj in Norway the same labor
£ets £l, and there are many female workers
in the business who get still less. Further,
freight from Norway is very cheap. Hence
timber for all sorts of packing cases is being
landed in Australia cut to sizes and all
ready to knock together. Meat cases by the
hundred thousand are being imported, and a
Norwegian rabbit crate is being sold at 7fd.,
whereas the Australian rock-bottom is 10id.
The old yarn was that distance from the
populous, low-wage countries offered Aus-
tralia sufficient Protection where bulky goods
are concerned; but the serious fact is that
cases cut to sizes can be landed here from
Norway for 2s. 4d. per 100 ft., while the
freight from Maoriland, where most of the
timber used in Australia comes from, costs
2s. 9d. Tudor, of the Customs, was appealed
to some time ago ; but before he had time to
move, the meat and other packers howled
like 10,000 dingoes, and nothing was done.
Somehow the poor Australian Protectionist
has got into the habit of dropping his creed
overboard as soon as a manufacturer yells
against his raw material being taxed. The
meat-packer and the fruit-merchant conveni-
ently overlook the fact that their goods are
somebody else’s raw material; in fact, every-
thing that everybody produces is somebody
THE NEW NIGHTMARE.
Mr. N. J. Sievers, a young Australian, until re-
cently of New College, Oxford, delivered a striking
address on “Syndicalism.” at the Trades Hall, on
Friday, under the auspices of the Political Labor
League.— Daily paper.
THE LITTLE BEAR : “Fancy the tiger coming
in those togs!”
else’s raw material or tool of trade, and
nobody would ever get any Protection at all
if there was anything in the argument which
was used to flatten out the Australian
sawyers.
“Dumper,” in disgust:—
Instead of fooling about with a six-hour day, the
Labor party would please a lot of its supporters better
if it provided eight hours of employment for them per
medium of the Tariff. Take the moving picture
business. The importation of films other than
scenic and educational is absolutely unnecessary.
They can all lie produced here at practically the same
cost to the picture showmen, and their production
would earn bread-money for hundreds of Australians.
But the big film exchange proprietors are almost
as patriotic as theatrical promoters—that is, they
will not give local goods a trial until they are forced
to. For instance, a local crowd recently produced
a splendid picture of the whaling industry at Jervis
Bay. The big city showmen unanimously gave it
the cold shoulder. Not to be discouraged, the local
crowd sent the negative to London. There it has had
a warm welcome, and the English people are
anxiously inquiring for more of the same sort. If
all the dramas, etc., which are now imported were
produced here, which is not a very large order,
think of the celluloid film that would be required.
With a decent dutv the manufacturing of this film
would be a gpod leg-in for big works. And so the
ball would roil on. But this would mean work, in:
stead of copying invoices, etc. ; so what’s the good ?
Uncle Sam is finding out that population
is not everything—that the thing to be
desired is population of the right kind.
Hence the Barnett Bill, which when the last
mail left had passed Congress and awaited
the President’s signature. This measure re-
quires that every immigrant must be able to
read and write his own language. Taking
the immigration of 1911, it would have
blocked three-fourths of the 158,000 from
Russia, half the 182,000 from Italy, one-
third of the 159,000 from Austro-Hungary,
and one-half of the 70,000 from elsewhere in
Southern Europe. And while it is getting
this crowd of low-grade illiterates from
foreign countries, the U.S.A. is losing
100,000 first-rate people to Canada every
year—the cream pushed out to make room
for ignorant Dagoes and the like, who cling
about the overcrowded cities. The English
press pays the “Oversea Dominions” the
backhanded compliment of saying that this
Barnett law will be a grand thing for
Canada and Australia. It seems to be known
that the education test is practically only
applied here to Asiatics.

If Greater Sydney had been an actuality,
instead of a mere vision in the air, the
suffering suburbs would not have been so
utterly dependent, the other day, on a few
Private Enterprise gas cos.
and a few hundred super-
heated unionists. The sur-
plus electricity of the Ma
municipality cannot get out
to the suburbs, because the
jealous rabbit-skinned
mayors are in the way; and
the wretched little councils
are themselves too small and
too numerous to run separate
lighting plants. So people
had to do camp-Cooking in
the yard, and potter about
with candles, and while they
did it, The Bulletin hopes
they swore to henceforth and
for ever be solid barrackers
for a unified Sydney.,
“Demo’s” bark : u.
Pastor Cape, an English visitor to the Wesleyan
Conference in Melbourne, rapped out a brand-new
idea. He has just been in Fiji, where he was
enraptured by the beautiful Hindus. His idea is
that if Australia is attacked, the Sepoys' from India
must be fetched in. Our own flesh and blood, vital,
integral parts with Us of this glorious British Em-
piah. One hundred and seventy-thousand loval Brit
ish Sepoys rushing to our help. (There’s, only one
dislovalist in India, the chap who flung'the Hardinge
bomb.) Not aliens like Japs or Chinese, but Aus-
tralia’s own brethren-! What a defence they would
be against Asia! Ghollah and Chunder as good as
English. It is a brilliant idea, so new and so re-
assuring that I pass Gape a vote of thanks, and at
once send my subscription to the Pice Society.

THE CONSTABLE : “Doesn't matter what you
call yourself, Joe, the records ’ll tell what
you ARE.”
Georgie Fairbairn, M.H.R., who disputes
Willie Kelly’s claim as the ideal Johnnie
Boy in politics, tells his elec-
torate that he has no use for
the maternity bonus. “The
Liberal party wants to put the
father of a family in such a
position as not to require the
bonus.” For which reason that
large section of Fat, of which
Georgie is a plump ornament,
has for years instantly granted
every increase of wages that
the workers have asked for,
and has proposed all the meas-
ures in existence which have
improved their social and finan-
cial condition. It all reminds
this paper of the old Lancashire
mill-owner who declared: “My
hands want for nowt. They
wouldn’t have the danged im-
pudence.”
Melbourne is still thudding with the pro-
found silence that followed the report on the
Infectious Diseases Hospital. It was a damn-
ing document, and the public now awaits two
events: (1) A reply by the committee of
management, if one can be devised; and (2)
summary action by the Government to put
the institution on a wholesome, business-like
and trustworthy basis. When he does start
reforming, John Weary had better begin by
re-casting the policy that permits the Gov-
ernment to provide half the cost of main-
taining the institution, yet gives it no say in
its management. To that piece of blastifer-
ous stupidity a lot of the bother is due.
Some casual person who took a hand in
the arrangements for the bv-election in Yass
(N.S.W.) appears to have forgotten
that a polling place was gazetted at
Black Range. People turned up there
to vote, but there was no booth, and
so their opinions on Labor and Wade-
ism remain unknown. Just how the
affair can be settled without going
over the whole business again is a
great mystery; appearances must be
attended to as much as if the election
really mattered.
One of the humors of the Sydney Gas
Strike was the spectacle of Joe Hector Car-
ruthers lecturing the men for having tried
to do the right thing in an unconstitutional
manner. Nobody, of course, remembers
the wire-netting case.
Speaking at Ballarat against the Refer-
enda proposals, Deakin pretended to be hor-
rified at the idea of the Commonwealth
appointing a court to which railway em-
ployees might go for the settlement of their
disputes. Said Alfred :
A Government which had not an interest in the
railways to appoint a tribunal to fix the
wages to be paid to railway men. If the proposal
were agreed to the States would practically cease
to own their own properties.
For some years State Governments have
appointed tribunals to decide what wages
should be paid to the employees of boot
factories, pickle mills, etc., in which
they (the States) have no interest; but there
has been no indication that the manufac-
turers have lost their property, or even their
corpulence. And the folly of Deakin’s resur-
rected observation is all the greater because
the very same people who constitute the
States, and therefore own the railways, also
constitute the Commonwealth.

cheers for Unification :

and the two races, British and French had liv h
together in peace and harmony. Canadians eenerain,
had a fine respect for the law, and a nur P

Up to date the same James Bryce, who also

ters, hasn’t had anything to say in their

Senator Millen, gibbering at the Referenda
at Kiama (N.S.W.), the place where the
ghosts come from :
The British race occupied its present proud D osi
tion owing to the fact that under its flag it haH
been allowed to carve out its own political affairs
without interference, and the neople of Ns \y
should be left to determine their local government
for themselves.
In other words, the British race occupies its
present proud position because Yorkshire
didn’t waste time projecting bricks at Corn
wall, and Essex didn’t blue-metal Berkshire
when they met in a dark lane. Likewise
the whole boiling of the British counties did
not join pushes to mob the Central Govern
ment. They just attended to their own small
affairs, and allowed the Central Government
to attend its big national affairs. And the
futile Millen reckons that if Australia does
likewise it will be damned.
A bitter wail from Sydney Evening News
(27/2/T3) :
The customs duties are like the taxes of old which
were farmed out to tax-gatherers. The customs
officers collect the money from the importer who
passes it on, with something added, to the wholesale
distributor, and the merchant tacks on a little more
when he sells the goods to the retailer, and the
shopkeeper adds still a bit more to the price which
the consumer has to pay. Each one, importer dis-
tributor, retailer, not only adds the customs duties
to the price, but also something more for interest on
the outlay for duty and for the risk of loss, and also
something for his profit on that amount. The final
result is that the consumer has to pay at least twice
the amount of duty, if not more.
Even with all these drawbacks Australia is
importing about £15,000,000 worth more
goods per annum than it can honestly pay
for, and is running enormously into debt for
its foreign luxuries. The Freetrade paper’s
remedy for all troubles is to abolish the exist-
ing ineffective duties, so that the country
may import still more, and run further into
debt. Another device, which is not that of
the Freetrade paper, is to make the wretch-
edly inefficient duties prohibitive in many
cases, and stop the importation, and obliter-
ate all the swindling middlemen, who,
according to the Freetrade publication, are
the curse of its blessed importing system.
Many big Australian manufacturing con-
cerns already make goods locally, and sell
direct to the retail customer. The process
cuts out freight, marine insurance, commis-
sion, exchange, the importer’s profit, and the
wholesale distributor’s profit. In short, it
brings the maker and the user bang up
against each other. Also, it stops the export
of absurd sums of money, and it gives the
Australian a job. And a job is better than
carrying a faded banner in a procession of
unemployed, and clamoring to the Govern-
ment to borrow another £10,000,000, and
build an unprofitable railway from Dead
Horse or Mad Ox, so as to make a bogus
temporary prosperity.

THE MISSUS: “You! Why there's nothing
but wind in YOUR basket!”
Hans Irvine, M.P., is full of optimism, but
it would be better if he took a dash of logic
with it. The other evening he spoke to his
Western constituents (Vic.) and assured
them that the Labor Government was going
in for “frenzied defence.” “There was, he
complacently asserted, “no fear of invasion
here, as we were too far from the base ot
other nations, and Germany and America
would also see that we were safe from
Japan.” So we are too far from Japans
base for invasion, but not too far from Ger-
many’s more distant base for that nation to
rush to gratuitously defend us!
CROMWELL CARMICHAEL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
What is such legislation but tyranny, fanatical
tyranny, worthy of Oliver Cromwell ?
—Archbishop Kelly, on the education question.
Ca-ampbell Carmichael, your name should be Crum-
mell;
Your fingers are red with the blood of the blest;
You are riding through slaughter, and tied to the
pommel,
The baby is shrieking that’s torn from the breast!
You wade in the gore of the faithful with laughter,
And gay are your pennons, and loud are your
drums ;
But the wraith of our vengeance shall follow you
after:
Campbell Carmichael, the reckoning comes.
Y*u light up the land with the flames of your fury,
Our shrines they are sacked and our saints they are
slain;
But black is the curse with which all will adjure ye
Whenever your name shall be mentioned again!
Your thirst it is slaked with our martyrdom holy,
You butcher our brightest and baton our brave;
But the spirit that’s in us is freshening slowly,
Not even Carmichael can make it a slave!
Thus speaks the Archbishop, at least something like
it,
When the member for Leichhardt comes into his
view,
So I clutch at my harp and I hopefully strike it
With dreams of what Cromwell Carmichael may do.
To miles of orations I’ve lately been listening,
And I wish that this Crom., like his namesake of
yore,
Would come with his pikes and his cuirasses glisten-
ing,
And hustle our mob through the Parliament door.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
At time of writing punters are not being
sorely tempted to pick the winner of Sydney Cup.
Everything with a long-distance reputation and a
favorably-handicapped appearance is at a short price
on the lists, and even little Almissa, who looks some-
what overweighted on the Bst 111 b. mark, is quoted
at only 10 or 12 to one. Uncle Sam (Bst. 61b.),
Hallowmas (Bst. 41b.) and Jacamar (Bst. 91b.) are
the nominal favorites. If the chestnut Uncle could
be guaranteed on his Caulfield Cup form, he would
look the pick of the handicap. For that matter, the
trainer seems quite satisfied that Uncle is in great
buckle. But his recent performance at Flemington
did the big horse no credit, nor is it certain that
he will take kindly to travelling the Randwick
course. Up to now Uncle Sam has been a nervous,
awkward customer, anyhow, whereas Hallowmas has
already won two good races on the A.J.C. ground.
The imported horse has not been unduly penalised
for his second to Piastre in the Melbourne Cup ; he
is probably improved by further acclimatisation ; and
if W. H. Smith has the mount next Monday, the
chance of Hallowmas can he reasonably preferred to
that of Uncle Sam or Jacamar, though the latter is
suspected of powers which have previously been more
honored in the rumor than the performance. With-
out considering the dozen and one chances of an
unexpected “turn-up” for the rich Svdney Cup, this
onlooker puts his half-dollar on Hallowmas.
“0.K.”: The Footscray Council is pro-
testing against the V.R.C.’s belated proposal to
charge Is. for admission to Flemington Flat on race
day®. One humorless councillor said that “ever
since he had known the flat it had been open to the
public free of charge, and he thought it was mon-
strous to make a charge now,” which intensely Tory
argument seems to express the opinion of all the in-
dignant democrats who have written The Bulletin
on this subject of free race-going any time during
the past 20 years. It is clear as daylight that
Flemington racecourse is no more of a democratic
institution than Randwick or Caulfield, or any other
expensively appointed show-place where the mini-
mum charge of admission is Is. The public pays
11s. 6d. for seats or standing room in the Grand
Stand enclosure (dress-circle or stalls), and 3s. 6d.
for ditto on the Hill (amphitheatre), as though
Flemington were an open-air theatre. Their money
mainly provides the entertainment which the “flat-
ites” have been seeing for nix all these years. The
army of members at £6 a year (plus entrance fee)
can be classed as dress-circle season ticket pur-
chasers. Owners of horses gamble among them-
selves in entry fees, and bookmakers pay something
for the privilege of living on the punters ; but the
management’s receipts are mostly gate money. If
nobody paid to go in, there’d be no show worth men-
tioning to attract the dead heads, on whose behalf
the councillors of smellful Footscray are now blath-
ering. One of these illogical persons said, “Hundreds
of families made a picnic day of Cup Day on the
flat, and did not take any interest in racing, and
he would ask, ‘Why should these people be charged ?’
The revenue would be used to build more palaces
for those who patronised the lawn.” The gall of
that councillor! Cup Day is a public holiday be-
cause the money of the paying nublic has made
Flemington what it is, and given a great attraction
to its principal race. Otherwise there never would
have been a Cup Day holiday nor any picnics. The
alleged hundreds of families who take no interest in
racing are indebted to the Cup for their annual out-
ing, and they go to Flemington Flat to devour sand-
wiches and things not because the stinks that blow
o’er the Saltwater River from Footscray give a
charm to that open space in an ordinary way, but
because the providers of gate-money for the V.R.C.
have supplied them with an exciting scene. The
Flat, considered apart from the racing surroundings,
is the last place on God’s earth where anv wise
familv would go to picnic, but if those hundreds of
Cun Dav visitors are dead struck on it, they can go
and brood on its flatness 340 days a year, free of
charge. There is a terrible lot of picturesque picnic
space to spare within a few miles of Melbourne
GP O. Nobodv needs to rush to Flemington Flat
in order to take no interest in the racing. Nobodv
should ever have been allowed to go there gratis if
what the Wowsers say about the gambling evil is
half true. Flemington on race days is a gaming-
place ; and with the example of dear old England to
warn them, the legislators of Victoria ought not to
have licensed a metropolitan racing club that gave
free admission to impecunious punters. No good
argument—sporting, social or judicial—can be ad-
vanced in favor of a free racecourse. The public
that is bound to’ pay for its melodrama, its circus
and its cinematograph show should be the more cer-
tainly hound to pav for its dissipation on a race-
course. Why should the punter who goes to the
Flat to bet escape the payment of a reasonable
charge—that he mav have one more shilling to drop
into the maw of Moses? Whv should the person
who takes no interest in racing want to go where
racing is on Cup or any other dav? Whv should the
horsey-minded sport, who doesn’t bet (such people
are possible! he granted au intereetiP 0 ' entertain-
ment free of charge on the flat, when others of the
same sort must pay to keep the entertainment
going ? * * *
The Bushy Park stud Ilerefords, the best-
known herd in Maoriland. go to the hammer at
Wanganui on April 24 and 25. Australian breeders
are bound to reach out after some of the famous
cattle.
* * * #
Some of E. E. Oatley’s winnings on Re-
lievo went to buy Gillamatong last week. The four-
vear-old son of Trafalgar’s mother was probably a
bargain at 800 guineas. As a youngster he was at
the top of the tree; last season he ran only once
(nowhere) ; and the other day he won a race, his
first for two years, in a decidedly coming-on style.
If the half-brother to Trafalgar stands training he
may develop into a stayer.
* * * *
An interesting demonstration of the influ-
ence of currents was given by the mast of the lost
Sydney yacht Thelma, which was wrecked about a
mile and a half inside Port Jackson Heads. On
Tuesday last a man observed some wreckage at the
Dee Why beach, about eight or 10 miles away north
on the ocean front. When the pilot steamer picked it
up it turned out to be the mast of the Thelma,
which had been carried out through the Heads and
swept along the coast. So far the body of the lost
vachtsman has not been recovered, and the nautical
folk believe it, too, has been swept out to sea, and
that if ever it is cast up it will be on the ocean
beach north of Manly.
* * * *
Saturday was another bad day on Port
Jackson, with a vicious westerly tearing crevasses
in the water. It was the Prince Alfred day out, and
the objective was the King Edward VII. trophy.
The only yacht to come to the line under full sail
was Culwulla 111., all the rest blowing along under
restricted hamper. The early starters made terrific
pace to Manly, and so did Culwulla 111. when she
shook out that huge sail and caught all the winds of
Heaven in it. Sunbeam was the first to appear at
Shark Island, followed afar by Mag'C, behind which
Yuelba and Petrel were as usual duelling. Behind
them again were the three big yachts, Sayonara,
Culwulla 111. and Rawhiti, in that order. Sunbeam
was about 20min. ahead of Sayonara. Then the
wind chopped round to south-east, which suited Sun-
beam so well that Yuelba retired and Sayonara
didn’t trouble to round the fort. Culwulla held on,
but before she made the turn at Fort Denison, Sun-
■beam was rounding the Pile Light on the home run.
Magic had a mishap and dropped out, and Petrel
and Culwulla alone continued the vain pursuit. Sun-
beam had bolted altogether, and won by a margin of
7min. 14sec. from Petrel, and 12min. 20sec. from
Culwulla 111.
What a wonder old George Holmes is
with his equally wonderful old boat, the Donnelly!
He was the only skipper to finish in the previous
week’s bad blow in Sydney Harbor, and last Satur-
day he again demonstrated his prowess by beating
the very best of them in the final of the Flying
Squadron’s handicap. The first heat had looked a
sure thing for Advance, which, excellently handled,
made a splendid run over the first half of the course,
only to come to grief on the second and be compelled
to make way for Arline, Nimrod and Eunice in that
order. In the second heat. Kismet, the champion
boat, quite easilv defeated Donnelly, and so did
Golding, which ran into second place. But in the
final the veteran opened out his whole hag of tricks,
and though Arline fought him every inch of the wav,
the old man won, by swinging his boat more smartlv
round the corners. Kismet, the champion, could
finish only third in the final.
» » * *
Port Jackson just missed by a hair’s-
breadth another tragedy on Saturday. Mr. Wads-
worth, owner of the yacht Aoma, was steering his
own craft in the P.A.Y. Club race, when a stranger
ranged alongside and gibed. So close was the
stranger that the heavy boom swept Aoma’s deck,
and, knocking Wadsworth down, pinned him to the
deck. It was a miracle that his skull was not
smashed in or his neck broken. As prevention is
better than cure, something ought to be done to keep
careless or unskilful yachtsmen from prancing over
the face of the harbor, endangering the lives of
other people; if it hasn’t got one already, the
Navigation Department should frame a set of regu-
lations to put down the dangerous capers.
Arnst has announced that he will not row
on the Richmond, but he has not withdrawn his de-
posit, so he apparently believes the Paddon crowd
will give way. It is hard to see why they should.
The rules give them the right to name the
meeting-place, and it is a piece of presumption on
Arnst’s part to talk as he is doing. He would have
been perfectly within his rights had he said when
issuing his challenge that it was impossible for him
to row anywhere else than on the Parramatta. But
he challenged in the usual way, and kicked
up the bobbery afterwards. Thie chances are
certainly that, in spite of all the non-payers
who will cover the river with their motor-
launches, and all the people who will look on
from the shore for nothing, the Parramatta will
yield a bigger, gate than the Richmond. That river
is just as easily overlooked as the Parramatta, and
it is hard to see where the boats to carry a decent
“gate” can be got from at Richmond. Arnst’s re-
quest has, therefore, something behind it; hut, like
the gasmen, he’s going the wrong way about the
job.
* * * *
Saturday saw the commencement of the
12th round of First-Grade Cricket in Sydney. Some
of the batsmen were in form, Bardslev heading the
list with a neat 121 for Glebe against Redfern. The
other century-maker was Bull, of Middle Harbor,
who smacked up 107 against Petersham. Maclean,
of Gordon, had hard luck —he had got to 98 when
stumps were drawn. Trumper in the same match
hit up 73 before being clean howled bv Noble. Bar-
bour was eroing great guns for University, and had
piled up 72 when the light gave out. Scores from
30 upwards were thick as blackberries at Bulli.
Coomev, of the University, was top howler, with
four Burwood wickets for 37. Coffey, of Svdnev, got
four of Central Cumberland’s for 40, and his club
mate Rowe three for 33. In spite of Bardsley’s 121
Glebe only oiled up 288 against Redfern, which,
however, did not show much promise in reply,
promptly losing two wickets for eight runs, Bur-
wood is in a had wav with University ; dismissed for
144, it has had 148 smacked off its bowling for three
wickets. Central Cumberland was at home to Syd-
ney at Parramatta, and hit up 234 bv way of wel-
come. Svdney lost one wicket for 34. Balmain
plaved all over North Svdnev, and hanged 312 for
eight wickets, so North Svdnev is again in a state of
gloom. Gordon slogged the Paddington howling to
the tune of 298 for nine wickets, and Middle Harbor
smote Petersham to glorv with 381. towards which
Bull’s 107 was a valuable contribution.
* * * *
In Melbourne on Saturday the windows of
Heaven were opened and the waters were spilled with
violence upon the earth. The result was that all
pennant matches, save that between Prahran and
North Melbourne, were postponed; and there were
some people who wished that had been postnoned
also. They mostly live round Prahran way, for North
Melbourne, with Brascher 63 and Stuckey 32 not
out, smacked up 113 for the loss of only two wickets.

“C. J.W.”: Re spectacled cricketers and
footballers (B. 6/3/’l3), and the query as to
whether there has been a spectacled player under
Australian rules. Dr. Bennie, who played for Mel-
bourne in its championship days, when the game was
very much rougher than it is at present, always wore
specs., and was one of the popular cracks. I believe
there were two brothers Bennie in the team, but
“Specs.” is a certainty.
* * * *
Somebody writes that in the years a-rear
E. Rees, of the Parnell Club, Auckland (M.L.), in-
variably wore his glasses while playing Rugby. Rees
played a strenuous game at half-back and was an
Auckland “rep.” in 1891-1894.
* * * *
Jim Barry, the Yankee bruiser who fought
a few fights at the Svdney Stadium some time ago, got
himself disliked at Brisbane on Saturday night. He
was matched with Sam Langford, and just before
entering the ring demanded and got his purse. It
is quite common in the U.S.A., where managers and
promoters spring up like mushrooms in the night,
for the gentry who supply the stoush to get their
cash before delivering the battery, but the practice
is unknown in Australia. However, the management
yielded, and lived to regret it. Half-way through
ithe first round Langford put one on the ribs and
Barry sank down, took the full count, and then rose
and explained briefly that his rib was broken. With
that he hurried from the ring. The audience de-
clined to believe his statement and howled epithets
at him, and when it was known that he had col-
lected his cash in advance, the genera,! opinion of
Barry’s genuineness grew no better with lightning
rapidity. Unless the young man produces medical
testimony that his rib is broken, he can confidently
rely upon having no more fights m Australia, wot
that he will be missed. His fighting was alway
painful to watch because he is so gloriously ignorant
of the rudiments of boxing. He can do most good
for the game by making way for better men.^
Wednesday night saw the Stadium pro-
duce two scraps. The preliminary was a 10- round
bout between Cox and Burns. There was said to
be onlv 21b. difference in their weights, which shows
how deceitful appearances are. Burns was a fair
youngster with a shock of sandy hair, while the
other was dark as a sloe. Both were fierce as tiger
eats; also, Cox carried a deadly wallop in both
fists, and was particularly quick with thei left. Last
year saw Burns at the Stadium among the amateurs,
and he seems to have started fighting before he
learned to box. He is dogged as they are made, and
worries on to his man all the time like a bull terrier.
But he doesn’t know how to hit, and is not alto-
gether sure how to protect his face. He is, how-
ever, a very iron-faced youth, and took a series of
wallops that would have made an iron pot feel
weary. But in boxing, as in international warfare
ferocity goes for nothing unless there has been
solid and careful preparation to make it effective.
* * * *
An Australian fighter of whom the world
is likely to hear much more in the near future is
Gordon Cogill, the artilleryman. Fresh from the
amateurs, he has had only three fights, and won
them all in short order. On Wednesday night last week
he smashed up Ewars in less than half a round and
did it so thoroughly with three wallops that it was
several minutes before Ewars realised what had hap-
pened. The new-comer is of the Mace style, shoulders
square and head thrown well back. He hits like
lightning, and the force of his blow is as the kick of
a mule. But he will have a bad time if ever he
attains championship honors; he will be hunted to
death by wild women. His physique is easily the
most handsome ever seen in Sydney Stadium, while
his action is the acme of easy grace. As a specimen
of Australian manhood somebody ought to take nim
to Nelson Illingworth, and have him cast m bronze
before his ring glories dower him with a broken nose
and a cauliflower ear or two.
* * *
.On Wednesday last two car-loads of
pressmen went out to Hunter’s Hill to see Johnny
Summers at work ; and Johnny worked his mightiest
—except at hitting his sparring partners. He was
very tender with them, having had a sad experience
during the week, when, in a moment of abstraction,
he caught one under the chin and outed hirn for
quite a while. It was an accident, but that and his
work with the punching bag suggest that Summers
has a much harder wallop than when he was here
before. The small boy was present, having slipped
in between the legs of the big ones, and afterwards
this scribe proceeded to interview the kid. Well,
what did you think of it?” The small boy never
hesitated. “Not much! He didn’t make nobodv s
nose bleed!” he said with fine scorn. Then, taking
a sudden dislike to publicity, he went out the way
he came in, between the legs of the big ones. The
small boy is very Roman in his tastes.
* * * *
The fight between Dave Smith and Les
O’Donnell on Saturday was characterised by pluck
and endurance that made it one of the Sydney
Stadium’s most stirring battles. That an unusual
scrap was expected was made plain by the crowd
which attended; the great enclosure was absolutely
packed. O’Donnell had the benefit of the difference
of about 51b. in the weights. The fight started with
a bound and went with a rush. Both men were evi-
dently in perfect nick. The slogging was heavy,
too, but up till the 10th round it would lave been
hard to say which fighter had the advantage. It was
a scrap calculated to make a sympathetic soul weep
for the referee. But in the 10th a sudden change
came o’er the ’scape. Smith got home a few that
knocked a lot of the stamina out of O’Donnell, and then
sent in a couple that hurled him against the ropes.
He rebounded, to fall and stay down for nine seconds;
staggered up to meet one on the chin that caused
him to sink to his knees for another nine; then he
got up and fought like a tiger at bay. But Smith
never gave him a moment’s respite, and crowded
right in, hurling blows all the while. One of them
caught O’Donnell on the chin, and sent him stagger-
ing across the ring. Smith came down like a bliz-
zard ; but, somehow, O’Donnell went to meet him.
Another right dazed him; a shower of blows literally
beat him to the floor. But he got up again, and
came back still unconquered. Another shower of
blows and a vicious uppercut rocked him, and a
heavy right floored him for the fourth time in the
same round; but the gong saved him. To say that
the crowd went mad conveys nothing. It literally
howled. The way O’Donnell pulled himself together
during the minute’s respite was simply marvellous.
He came out and attacked Smith with dogged grit.
But he was done; so when he slipped in the 13th,
apparently from sheer weakness, Inspector Matthews
called a halt; and Smith, of course, was declared the
winner. It was an amazing fight, and if O’Donnell
can bring his skill to the same high standard as his
grit and stamina —well, he’s a world-beater, easily.
* * * *
Billiardist Geo. Gray, when passing
through Fremantle recently, told an interviewer that
the old idea that it was unwise for a cueist to in-
dulge in anv form of exercise was exploded. Speak-
ing personally, he went in strongly for physical
exercise, but was careful about the use of his arms.
He made it a rule never to carry a weight in his
hands, not even a suit case. When George G. breasts
the waves of matrimony, he will probably find that
the small voice which tearfully complains of the
large pain during the wee hours will demand to be
taken into the paternal grasp regardless of the risk
of spoiling a losing-hazard stroke.
*** * \
At a recent open hill-climb for motor-
hikes in Adelaide (4i miles') the fastest time went
to a machine known as “The Kent,” the engine of
which was made in Adelaide and not from im-
ported parts. It was designed and manufactured
by a young local engineer, and it is the second time
he has made the fastest time against a crowd of the
best imported machines, although on formula he
could not win.
* * * *
A loud groan rose from the motoring world
recently when the news came through that the
American yield of petroleum, from which petrol is
obtained, had during 1912 decreased by 250,000
barrels of 42 gallons each. But the Yankee oil
pirates managed to squeeze £3,200,000 more out
of the citizens of the planet for the reduced output.
However, there was a gleam through the murk when
the cable announced that one of the London motor-
’bus cos. had invented a new fluid which it can retail
to its drivers at lOld. per gallon, and hoped thereby
to end the strike of the jehus. Presumably the new
fuelwill shortly be placed on the market, and, also
presumably, it is the coal-tar product benzol, men-
tioned in this column about-two months back. May
it come quickly, is the prayer of the righteous.

The Inter-State Motor-Car Reliability
Test is now an assured thing, and will start at Syd-
ney on Wednesday, March 26. Four days will be
allowed in which to reach Melbourne, the stopping
nlaces being Goulburn, Gundagai and Wangaratta.
The prizes are trophies valued at £4O, £2O and
£lO. The manner of scoring is peculiar. Each
contestant will start with 900 noints (500 for reli-
ability, 200 for petrol consumption and 200 for hill
climbing), and what he has to do is to keep those
points intact. Deductions will he made for mechani-
cal repairs and adjustments, broken spring v,
axles, etc. The man retaining the greatest „ , at
of points at the end wins. st nu ®ber

* * * -
The latest from the United States is the
“automobile house.” It is built like a drawn-out
motor-’bus, with a body 25ft. long. Most of the in
terior is taken up by a “state-room,” with sleeping
berths for five people. Another room has accomno-
dation for four more people, while two servants and
a kitchenette are stowed away in the tail. It sounds
a bit cramped, but the wealthy seekers after a new
sensation are rushing the idea and taking to what
they are pleased to term the simple life in the
scenic parts of the country.
* * * *
That weird little contraption, the cycle
car, is coming along at a great rate, and has already
made such an impression that it is to have a place
in this year’s French Grand Prix. Of course, it
won’t compete with the big, giant-powered roarers,
but it and the motor-cycles will divide the day after
the big event between them, and race over a 12-
mile circuit on July 13. The results of the test of
the new contraption will be eagerly awaited all over
the world.
* * * *
So Jake de Rosier’s famous motor-cycle
record for the hoar has been smashed at last, and
on the track whereon it was established, that of
Los Angeles (U.S.A.), where Jake ran 83 miles 135
yards. L. Humiston. the new champion, flew over
88 miles 350 vards in the 60 minutes, covered the
century in 68min. 1 4-ssec., and established a new
set of world’s records from 13 miles up. This
multiplication of records becomes wearisome.

SOME HOLIDAY VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Holiday Longings.
Mi for the sea; I lore its ailver splashing
O* the long hdt' beaches when the tide
runs home;
The sweet, cool thrill of the water, and
the flashing
Of the sunset tintings on its lace-white
foam.
Me for the bush, and the fern trees in the
gully,
The dong of the bell-bird as the dusk
stoops low,
The far camp-fire in the clearing, glowing
dully,
The scents that sweet the breezes where
the wild musks grow.
Me for the hills, and the sombre peaks at
morning,
When the red sun-flames fire the shroud-
ings of the mist;
The sheer, far steeps with the waterfall’s
adorning;
The white of flung clematis in the valley’s
amethyst.
Me for the flats that sprawl beside the
river,
The reeds’ quiet whisper on the dark
pool’s breast
When the hot day faints and the night
winds shiver
The haze above the grasses—ah, I love
the river best.
N.S.W.
THE SNAKE.
Barby.
The creek, beneath the rowan tree,
Is not as brown as Barby’s eyes,
For, though it ripples limpidly,
The creek, beneath the rowan tree,
Is shallow, as the world may see,
And steals its beauty from the skies.
The creek beneath the rowan tree
Is not as brown as Barby’s eyes.
The berries from the rowan tree
Are not as red as Barby’s mouth.
Her lips more crimson are to see
Than berries from the rowan tree.
Although they glow alluringly
In the soft breezes from the south
The berries from the rowan tree
Are not as red as Barby’s mouth.
The starlings in the rowan tree
Are not so black as Barby’s hair,
And when its braids are shaken free
The starlings in the rowan tree
May shake their plumage anxiously
And droop their feathers in despair.
The starlings in the rowan tree
Are not as black as Barby’s hair.
The berries in the rowan tree
Look passing sweet in Barby’s hair.
When starlings whistle happily
Her eager eyes are good to see.
The chill creek water, cold and free,
Makes her red lips look very fair.
The berries in the roivan tree
Look passing siceet in Barby’s hair.
VICTORIA.
CHRISTINA M.
Acushla.
I named her twice, I named her thrice,
I named her ten times over;
The wind heard, and the singing bird,
And the bee in the creamy clover.
Acushla! Acushla!
The cushat dove is cooing;
It's little that a man may do
Whose heart’s a-hot with wooing.
I left the field, the harvest yield—
The grain was ripe to falling—
And ran and ran, a crazy man,
And I the whole time calling—
Acushla! Acushla!
The cushat dove is cooing;
When Love is keeping holiday,
What worn is worth the doino?
Her feet were fleet, her pretty feet,
Upon the hill and hollow ;
She bade me stay, she cried me naj
And still her eyes said, “Follow!’
Acushla! Acushla!
The cushat dove is cooing.
To capture her was sweet, indeed,
Yet sweeter the pursuing.
N.s.w.
RODERIC QUINN.
Three Cities.
Hobart.
Scarce more than child, her arms so filled
With apple blossoms that she spilled
Some wayward sprays upon her track,
She tripped from out the mountain mist
With bright eyes full of wonder wist.
Blown, as the wind willed, down her back,
The tumbled beauty of her hair
Lent to the orchard-scented air
A perfume, subtle-sweet and rare;
Her lips red manna, that the cream
Of her smooth skin made redder seem,
Pouted as if displeased a while
Then parted L the sweetest smile,
Showing within the gleaming pearls
Of just the sweetest girl of girls.
I sought to filch, in loving stealth,
One kiss of all those red lips’ wealth,
But vainly! Up the mountain slope
She sped away—and with her. Hope.
But almost out of sight, she stopped
To fling a kiss to me, and dropped
Some apple blossoms from her store
To make me cherish her the more.
Then, eyes still full of wonder wist,
She hid her from me in the mist.
Melbourne
The gown she wears is quite the latest style,
There’s just the right politeness in her
smile;
Beneath her rustling lingerie she shows
The proper height of silken, high-clocked
hose
To win the connoisseur’s unstinted praise.
Her figure is delightful (for her stays
Are built by those well versed in Paris
ways!)
And through her veil a pair of flashing eyes
Throw out a challenge, full of bold emprise
To whom may read them, promising a flow
Of Pomeroy and Mumm and Veuve Cliquot,
A night of song and corybantic dance,
With only one thing wanting—Sweet Ro-
mance.
Perth.
The charm of mystery is in her eyes,
Tinging with wistful sadness all her face :
As one entranced with older memories
She fronts the future, sees its phantoms pace
In lonely inland vigil, huge with dreams
Wherein no hint of wizard menace seems.
She sees them. . .but her thoughts are other-
where,
Flung backward to a youth that ne’er was
young,
A love made lovelier by its own despair, _
Foiled of its purpose and with passion
wrung.
She looks—a smile is on her lips: she peers
With subtle, strange intentness into Time :
Who knows what stellar murmurings she
hears,
Crooned by the lips of Silence into rhyme?
QUEENSLAND.
BERNARD INGLEBY.
On Board a Ship.
How fine, I thought to take a trip
Remote from every worldly care,
And live at peace on board a ship—
All a delusion and a snare.
Romance, I thought, would roam the deck.
And call forth words of love so dear,
But “Oh, that cat!” and “What a neck!”
Are all the words I overhear.
Strange is the product of the lip
On board a ship.
The passengers recount their woe:
“None are so poorly fed as they,
The sluggish ship should faster go,
Their ills will surely turn them grey.”
All the base fabric of a dream, *
Hark to the other side, 1 beg.
For things are not quite what they seem.
The boot is on the other leg.
Bare of its mask the Truth we’ll strip
On board a ship.
No trouble could compare with that
Borne in grim silence by the crew ;
The doctor, barber and ship’s cat
Have really more than is their due.
An officer has bad luck too,
When in some corner of the deck
A damsel fair, with eyes of blue,
Will thrust an arm about his neck.
For hours he must endure this grip
On board a ship.
The captain hardly sleeps a wink.
The trials that he must endure
Would drive a weaker man to drink,
His job is no mere sinecure ;
When some old lady asks him, “Sir,
What would you do if we went down?”
Forgive him if he answers her,
“Just drown, good woman, simply drown.”
I think he often gets the pip
On board his ship.
The quartermaster has his woes,
His is no life of honeyed Miss
As up and down the deck he goes
Pursued by some precocious miss,
Who wants her deck chair turned around,
Or some such other foolish whim,
A hairpin lost that must be found,
And so she scares the life of him.
In wrath he goes and takes a nip
On board a ship.
A dog’s own life the steward lives
For twenty-six long hours a day,
As drop by drop his blood he gives
For merely miscroscopic pay.
To please the crowd he’d surely need
A body like an omnibus,
The legs of some large centipede,
And arms like any octopus.
Oh. far from adequate his tip
On board a ship.
VICTORIA.
WILL W. ROCK.
The Contradictions of Mabel.
The dainty frocks of Mabel,
The patter of her feet,
Have made my ways unstable
• And filled my world with heat:
Yea, Hades’ self, it seems to me.
Dwells in her lace and lingerie.
Her dark eyes kind and smiling.
Her hair as soft as night.
Were made for men’s beguiling.
And fashioned for delight;
And all that walk in trousers feel
Her sweetness draw like bands of steel.
About the painted city
I love to see her roam,
It seems a deuced pity
That ever she comes home ;
For various serpents coil and hiss
Amid our own domestic bliss.
My heart goes out to greet her
When in the morning fair
The happy wind grows sweeter
With whispers of her hair;
I thrill to all my finger tips
To kiss the rosebud of her lips.
If only I could hold her
Remote and far apart.
In dreams alone to fold her
Close to my beating heart,
This common world would straightway 1
Replete with wafts of deity.
But ill it is to measure
The woes that round me curl
Fast rooted in the treasure
Of one resplendent girl.
The saddest theme that I can sing
Is Mabel’s shining wedding ring.
Bound by that golden fetter
To beauty, love and joy.
The fates arrange the better
My gladness to destroy ;
For life that holds us in Us scorn
Constructs all roses with a thorn.
My will is all a Babel
Of loud conflicting noise;
I want to hold my Mabel
The chief of all my joys,
The while I long to burst apart
Her irking meshes round my heart.
This difficult arrangement
Some kind god ought to make,
To cause a warm estrangement
For peace and pity’s sake;
For what I really want to do
Is have my girl and lose her too!
N.S.W.
GEORGE STREET.

SCENES IN THE CITY POLICE COURT.
A crowd assemble in the city police court
To watch culprits tried by the Beak,
Petty thieves Loafers and Drunkards
Are dealt with every Week ;
Your worship exclaims a policeman
I arrested this man last night
For creating a scene in the city
By urging ruffians to fight.
A fine is imposed on the culprit
Which is paid to the clerk of the court,
In the dock appears an ill-famed woman
One of the notorious sort
Charged with disorderly conduct
Endeavors to plead a sad Tale,
Known to the police as a terror
Sentenced to three months in gaol.
Such are the usual charges
Settled by the serious Beak
Before the spectators assembled
In the court house every Week.

From the Old Soldier : —•.
The report that two cases of diphtheria have
occurred at the Light Horse camp at the capital site
will be a weapon ready to the hands of those who
are looking for flaws in the Commonwealth defence
scheme. The men had been sent into camp to give
brilliancy to the ceremony of naming the capital,
and it happened that they had had several days of
cold, wet weather following on a spell of heat. A
good many of the wretched bell tents, which were
their only protection from the weather, had been
blown down by the gale of the previous Saturday
night, and those that remained standing had the
single cloth of their roofs penetrated by the ram,
and everything inside was drenched. Quite likely,
to most of them, going into camp was nothing new.
But the man who goes camping for his own pleasure
has a fly to his tent, and so has two thicknesses of
cloth to keep out the weather. He can pitch his
camp where he likes, and can always get under some
sort of shelter. His tent, which is oblong, even if it
is only a 6ft. x Bft., will let him stand upright
under the ridge-pole. The soldier’s tent, on the
other hand, has to be pitched like part. of. a mathe-
matical puzzle on open ground, where it is exposed
to every wind, and its conical form makes it im-
possible for a man to stand upright in it. Bell tents
cost £5 each at Woolwich Arsenal, are short-lived,
and will onlv hold a dozen men. It would, there-
fore be infinitely cheaper for the Commonwealth to
house its soldiers in barracks, which would be much
healthier to live in, not to speak of the extra com-
fort they would give. Armies never encamp now in
real war. Thev cannot get transport for the hun-
dreds of tons of tents thev would need, so going into
camp is not “a service condition.”
Item from the Empire Trade commission
session at Christchurch (M.L.) :
Chairman (to J. B. Hall, representing the local
Agricultural and Pastoral Ass.) : You all talk about
wanting labor, but you don t do anything to help
yourselves.
Hall: No. , ,
Chairman: You wait for the- Government to do
something for you.
Hall: We always do.
There’s one thing about the great Backbone
—it’s well covered with hide.
It is notorious that Victoria’s Licenses
Reduction Board has improved the “tem-
perance” aspect of social life bv closing up
unnecessary hotels, and those conducted in
an unpleasant, undesirable and sometimes
illegal manner; that it' is, in fact, doing more
satisfactory work in one year than a dozen
cold-tea fanatics could do in a score. Yet,
one J. C. Martin—a parson, of course —now
accuses it of “buttressing the trade by pro-
viding more seductive houses of entertain-
ment.” Apparently Martin wants places like
Scott’s, the Oriental, the White Hart and
Champion’s wiped out, and the frowsy, un-
kempt beershops that used to disgrace the
“Little” city streets and lanes left un-
touched. At the same time, he probably
wonders why fair-minded people have a
whole-hearted contempt for the Wowser, and
refuse to take him and his howlings seri-
ously.

The above are just a few of th‘e many testimonials that we are continually receiving
from users of Beale High-Grade Pianos. . •
Such success has only been obtained through our unwearying efforts to produce an
instrument of the best Quality. That we have succeeded is proved by the opinions ot
those who should know best —the users of our pianos. . , ,
To obtain this quality we have invented patent methods of construction which render
our pianos more durable, while retaining and enhancing all the musical qualities of the
great makes of the Old World. The use of an iron wrest-plank—which is part of our
patent steel tuning-system—is as great an improvement in the art of piano manufacture
as was the superseding of the old wooden-frame by the iron-frame which is used in all
modern pianos. In addition, we have introduced many other improvements such a om
method of building up the casework from cross-plies of wood which is all thoroughly
seasoned here and hence tit to withstand Australian climatic conditions much better than
timber seasoned in the cold, damp climate of Europe. In order to get the best of skilled
artisans we pay higher wages than those awarded by the Wages Boards in the various
trades We Kurally get the best artisans, and thus the best of skill. We use only the
finest of materials.
We realise that an Australian-made piano is subjected to much closer scrutiny in
Australia than any imported piano, and therefore we make—we are compelled to make —
our pianos much better than those of our competitors. But then, by making pianos in
Australia we are in a position to make them much better suited to Australian conditions.
Any competent cabinet-maker will tell you that timber seasoned in the cold, damp climate
of Europe cannot be expected to withstand Australian conditions of dry heat as well as
timber which is seasoned in this climate. This is a most important point in piano-
making, and is one good reason why so many foreign pianos develop cracked sounding-
boards, split tuning-systems and faulty casework soon after arrival in this country. But
no man can say that he ever saw a Beale piano with a cracked sounding-board. We
absolutely guarantee that the sounding-boards of the Beale Pianos will never crack in any
climate, because our special system of construction renders cracking an impossibility, at
the same time that it greatly enhances the tonal qualities of the instrument. Beale
pianos contain the only practical steel tuning-system in the world —so there can be no
question of splitting of the wrest-plank. And then our patented system of cross-plied
woodwork ensures permanence of the outside case of the pianos.
Be sure of this: the better musician you are, the more you will delight in a Beale High-grade Piano.
We have always on hand a number of good second-hand pianos, taken in part payment of
Beale Pianos. •
Call and inspect the Beale Pianos now. Inspection does not put you under any obligation
whatever. You suit yourself entirely as to whether you purchase.

cash or on easiest terms. -

22 Automatic Rifle
The novelty of its operation is one of
the fascinating features of the Win-
chester .22 Automatic Rifle. Instead of
the downward and upward motion of a
finger lever, or the backward and for-
ward thrust of a slide handle, the pull
on the trigger is the only effort required
to shoot the rifle ten times in success-
ion. The only limit to speed in firing
is the rapidity with which the trigger
can be pulled before each shot. With
this speed is coupled fine accuracy,
light weight, simple and strong con-
struction, and ease and quickness of
handling. Note the beautiful appear-
ance of the rifle. It makes an ideal
rifle for holidays, and is suitable either

“&c.”: At last we have the explanation why the
Australian drama isn’t. In The Theatre Maga-
zine, “C.M.,” stated by that magazine to be the play-
reader to J.C.W., Ltd., remarks: “If anyone should
be in a position to elucidate it, surely I am he.”
And he modestly proceeds to elucidate. For five
years he has been the official play-reader for the
Firm, and “during that time I have been astonished
at the smallness of the output of local dramatists.
There haven’t been six Australian plays submitted.”
He further elucidates that by Australian plays he
means plays typical of this country, and that they are
“about as rare as snakes in Ireland.” Then, with
massive calm, or ponderous hide, he admits that he,
the play-reader who is It for the Firm, did not recog-
nise the Australian drama when he bumped into it.
It is admitted by J.C.W. that “On Our Selection”
was submitted to his play-reader, yet the excellent
opportunity of securing it was lost. “C.M.” elucidates
that he actually got into communication with Steele
Rudd and suggested collaboration. The author replied
“You shape the play from MS. in your hands, and I
will write as you require.” Here was the ideal col-
laboration —the author of the most popular book in
Australia, and an experienced play-reader who
knows exactly what the Australian drama is and
what the Australian producer wants. What hap-
pened? “Fate and pressure of other work were
against the fulfilment of this agreement, and in the
meantime Messrs. Bailey and Duggan had completed
their clever stage adaptation, and nobody is more
delighted than I to see them succeeding as they are.”
No action for damages can lie against Fate, but what
pressure of other work could have been sufficient to
prevent “C.M.” adapting this play for the stage?
He admits that he is doubtful whether he could have
done it as well, even if he could have done it at
all. He not only let the opportunity slip for Steele
Rudd, he let it slip for his employers, and “nobody
is more delighted than he.” This naive confession
explains why so few Australian plays are submitted
to the Firm. The Australian would-be playwright
has found “C.M.” out.
“Chasuble”: I cannot find a record of Allegri’s
“Miserere” being given in Australia until it was
programmed at St. Paul’s Anglican Cathedral for
every Friday in the present Lent. It is gi ven
the Sistine Chapel in Rome every Good Friday nivtit
and those readers who have heard it there and then
will testify to its overpowering effect. Behind the
altar is Michel Angelo’s “Last Judgment,” and the
ceiling is covered with his marvellous frescoes a
peculiarity of this “Miserere” is that if you go" over
it on the piano, or hear it given straight on the
organ, it sounds feeble and insipid; its own kind of
matchless greatness only comes out when it is ren-
dered by the voices in nine parts. There is some-
thing of Allegri in Chopin’s “Marche Funebre ”
heard at a number of Australian temples in connec-
tion with Scott and the Pole, and more poignant 1
think, than Handel in “Saul.”
“Theorbo”: At a Melbourne Old Colonist shivoo last
week, an identity declared, with pardonable exaggera-
tion, that Farquharson was the greatest basso ever
known. Vistors like him and Brooke certainly did
incalculable service in Australia. Farquharson came
in 1856, with Julia Harland, Hoskins, Sherwin and
Linley Norman (conductor). Operas were given,
with Hoskins in farces. This company was alleged to
have introduced “Maritana,” though I’ve an idea
that Nellie Stewart’s mamma was previously heard
in it. Anyhow, Farquharson became of supreme value
to the Melbourne Philharmonic, shining especially in
“Elijah.” Lyster came from America in 1860, with
Lucy Escott, Rosalie Durand, Georgia Hodson,
Squires, and F. Lyster. They opened in Melbourne
with “Lucia di Lammermoor,” Farquharson having
been joyfully taken into the troupe. His great suc-
cess was as Marcel in “The Huguenots,” which ran
a month, with the same performers every night.
Beaumont joined the company later. He first
appeared in Sydney in “The Bohemian Girl.”
When a playwright strikes success in England he
stands a fair chance of being worked to death.
Edward Knoblauch, having scored a double with
“Kismet” and “Milestones,” is naturally in great
request. His latest contribution is a four : act
comedy, “The Headmaster,” written in collaboration
with a rising young author named Coleby, and pro-
duced by Cyril Maude at the Playhouse. The play
is not epoch-making. The humor is of the simple
order. For example, in one scene Maude, as the
“head,” prepares elaborately to cane two of the
school-boys who have been brought up for punish-
ment. Says the first boy to the second, “If I’d
known it was to start like this I wouldn’t have
come.” He turns out to be the wrong boy. He had
come to be “confirmed”!
“Private Boxer”: I am inclined to agree with
“Tony” (B. 20/2/’l3) that pictures are on the wane.
If the demeanor of the audiences is anything to go
by, they are, in fact, dead as mutton already. I
was much struck during recent visits to various six-
penny “palaces,” by the tomb-like silence which
prevails at the finish of any film, serious or comic.
The screen becomes black, the band breaks off with
a jerk, and then —death-like stillness. No sign of
applause; nothing but stillness. A cinematograph
claque, or sometlr'ng, is badly needed to infuse a
little ginger into the proceedings. What about
taking the raucous-voiced gentleman in off “the door”
and making him principal hand-clapper? In the
dark he would have a fair chance of being mistakoi
for a genuine patron. His presence would certainly
make the auditorium less like a morgue.
“Rawlet”: We move! The other night I saw
the Northern Territory moving-picture film (taken
specially for the Federal Government) at a show at
North Carlton. As a prelude the map of Aus-
tralia was thrown on the screen, and it was re-
ceived w 1 th enthusiastic applause. The vigor of
that spontaneous outburst made the blood race
through my veins.
The Rickards vaudeville programme has presum-
ably ceased to announce little Alberto Marini as “late
principal tenor of the Quinlan Opera Co.” Quinlan,
in a friendly way, threatened an injunction to prevent
any further such glorification of Alberto.
Quinlan has engaged Felice Lyne, the American
prima donna, for the Australian grand opera tour
which begins in Melbourne in August. Felice is the
lady who made her London dFbut at Hammerstein's
Grand Opera House, and so annoyed the boss by
accusing him of striking her with a score of “Faust
that he fired in a writ claiming £5OOO damages.
THE MANAGER EXPLAINS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Chestnuts —“East Lynne,” “Camille” and “Uncle
Tom.”— “Sundry Shows” (B. 20/2/’l3).
The chestnuts do not spurn;
They do the boss a
Plays spanking new that draw are few;
I’m not on earth for Art —are you?
The old ’uns (here’s the proof
They’re good) still make much oof —-
You cannot keep the public from
“East Lynne,” “Camille” or Uncle Tom”!
To drop the furtive tear
Gives pleasure more than Beer!
Chestnutty plays that wring the heart,
And make the briny tear-drops start,
They are the sort that pay ;
The trio, old and grey,
“Go off” as well as any bomb —
“East Lynne,” “Camille” and “Uncle Tom.”
So managers, in doubt,
Put “East Lynne” posters out,
And see a crowd that does them proud
Roll up! Here I may be allowed
To whisper unto thee:
These dear old plays are free
Of authors’ fees and agents’ com. —
“East Lynne,” “Camille” and “Uncle Tom.”

IN BANANA LAND
Maoriland is being plunged by the
Wowser crowd into the throes of a fresn
Bible-in-State schools agitation. Mean-
time in Queensland schools the thing in the
vast majority of cases is simply a dead letter,
for neither teachers nor parents have any
sympathy with it. Bananaland pedagogues
smile when the thing is mentioned —and
there the matter ceases, as far as they are
concerned. And the public, wise in this
matter, is quite content to let it cease.
“Dano”: Another instance of the debili-
tating climate of North Queensland. Arrived
in Herberton the other day, for repairs, one
Sliadforth, aged 82. He has been in the
North only a matter of 40 years, most of it
spent in the Gulf country, and already he
finds it difficult to ride more than 80 miles a
day without feeling tired. He says his
friends want him to settle down, but he is
never so happy as when he is on the track,
and calculates to put in a few more years in
the saddle before enjoying a quiet old age.
Government Botanist Bailey is just issu-
ing a fresh catalogue of Australian flora, by
way of demonstrating what a Queenslander
can do when bordering on his 90th year. Six-
and-eighty years does the old man nurnber.
and the cursed northern climate will probably
land him in the next decade, if he doesn't
mind.
Lord Chelmsford, in a recent good-bye
speech, remarked that his seven and a half
years of residence in Australia “had been
years of pure, undiluted joy.” Judging by
his late Ex.’s demeanor in Queensland during
the constitutional crisis of 1907-1908, his
“undiluted joy” at that period was a very
mixed emotion.
“T.D.Iv.”: Just met another proof that
the white man can’t live long in the North.
It was at Calliope, in a bar. Three others
were there, and their, ages totted up 248
years. One was 84, another 83, the third
merely 81. And they had a very good thirst,
too, for men who must, I suppose, have been
dead a long while.
The. ticket-scalper has spoiled his own
game in Queensland ; the Commissioner pro-
poses to follow N. S. Wales and presently
abolish return tickets except on suburban
lines. But Queensland ought to do some-
thing which N. S. Wales hasn’t done, and
make it widely known that the passenger
may not break his journey. Every day,
almost, outback N. S. Wales passengers
who drop off and spend a night on
the way—often at a “change trains
here” station —are hauled up and told
that. .their ticket ran out when they
didn’t make the run straight through.
If Evans wants to minimise the cursing
over the abolition of return tickets, he will
see to it that a big notice, “No break of
journey allowed,” hits the eye of every back-
blocker when he takes his ticket.
A couple of quaint and interesting extracts
from a Courier just 50 years old :—•
There is a weed growing wild about the more un-
frequented streets of Brisbane which has often been
voted a perfect pest. It grows about 4ft. or oft.
high, and springs up with marvellous celerity....
Mr. Hill, the director of the Botanical Gardens,
finds that this plant—the Sida retusa —yields a
beautiful fine fibre of silky texture and great
strength.... An advertisement aopears in our
columns offering £3O a ton for 3000 tons of this
fibre.
Excerpt No. 2 is, a letter from a correspond-
ent describing the three principal kinds pf
rabbits that ought to be introduced. The
writer goes on to say that he has reason to
believe that bunny is very prolific, “and that
the grasses of Queensland would be quite
sufficient for the rabbit.” That writer was
quite correct —the grasses of Bananaland
suit bunny admirably, and the birth-rate in
his family is eminently satisfactory.
Brisbane Daily Mail, forecasting a deficit
of £70,000 for the current financial year, ven-
tures on this kindly hunk of criticism : ‘‘On
the other hand, the Treasurer has a card up
m his sleeve in certain amounts which, to
reduce last year’s surplus, he transferred
from loan to revenue and charged before due
date to revenue account of 1911-12.” In
plain language, the Tory journal accuses the
Tory Treasurer of faking. And this faking
has gone on continually since Wullie Kid'ston
initiated the practice in 1909, the object
being to defeat the excellent Queensland law
which says that surpluses shall be applied to
the reduction of the State debt. Apparently
the average Treasurer will do anything rather
than get even one stitch of the country’s
clothes out of pawn.

From the Far North
The par. (B. 6/3/’l3) anent Barambah aboriginal
settlement is a chunk of truth. Should any inquiry
be held, the conditions Binghi works under when
hired out to local cockies should be investigated.
This is a sample. One of the backbone outside
Murgon has had five or six blacks scrub-falling on his
selection. When pay-day comes round he procures
a couple of bottles of rum, to which he treats the
abos., and when they are well saturated he intro-
duces a little game of two-up, and of course succeeds
in winning back all the wages paid to the original
inhabitants. The poor food supply accounts for the
groups of half-starved cadging gins and piccaninnies
one sees about Wondai and Murgon. On a recent
Sunday the programme at the settlement included
a cricket match and five funerals. It seems as if
the old method of flour and strychnine would be, if
not more effective, at least quicker.
The ways of the land agent are various. The way
of Isles, Love and Co., of Brisbane, who have the
Boonara estate, on the Kingaroy line, in hand, was
to offer £5O in prizes for the best lucerne patches
produced on the first subdivisions. The prizes have
now been awarded by an expert, who, of course, adds
that the flats “will compare favorably with the best
lucerne lands in Queensland,” and that the people
who come along and buy the remaining 11,000 or so
acres ought to be soon riding in motor-cars.
“Kai” about a sad business :
The magisterial inquiry into the Murphy’s Creek
railway accident has been a wearying and unsatis-
factory job. Not one witness seemed to have any
idea of who was responsible. It certainly appeared
that no protection was afforded the breakdown gang
working on the debris of the first wreck. Charles
Redjnond Parkinson, one of the assistant engineers,
told the magistrate that none of the men on the
scen4 of the derailment knew of any protection. This
is hardly surprising, in view of the proven fact that
it was everybody’s job to provide protection, and
everybody’s job is nobody’s job. Probably the De-
partment, in order to furnish a scapegoat, will pick
out some poor devil of an enginedriver or pilot. It’s
an unfortunate fact that, during Charles Evans’s
reign as Commissioner, the red roll of the Queensland
railways has risen with a jump, in spite of the excel-
lent reputation he had built up for himself in the
State’s service.
Her Pearls.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
During the year 1911, 15 Javanese divers died
from diver’s paralysis and three from suffocation.
The deaths were about 11 per 100 employed.
They haul him dying from the depths that
hide
The frozen teardrops of the jealous sea;
Limply he lies his monstrous gear beside ;
The tropic sun glares down relentlessly.
That broken thing upon the lugger’s deck
Has paid for pearls that cling about her
neck!
When, Wearied quite, she cannot bear the
touch
Of precious rubbish on her slender throat,
Oh, can it be that cold ghost-fingers clutch
The gems so dearly won in seas remote?
By ev’ry risk that brings men’s schemes to
wreck,
My lady wears men’s lives about her neck !
BANANALAND.
DIVER.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, March 15, 1913.
My Dear Thabgomindah,—
Their Exes.’ daughter, who dropped in
from England the other day with a color in
her cheeks that has caused Envy to vibrate
upon the surrounding atmosphere, brought
with her a bale of new hats and London
gowns. On Tuesday she joined heartily in
the preparations which are being made by
an enthusiastic committee for the Oriental
ball, and has promised to sell tickets, give
advice, and do any other odd job calculated
to make the said ball roll with extra-special
energy.
Just to keep her hand in and her talents
from rusting, the Vision also offered to odd-
job for the agitated people who are struggling
with the Ilenley-on-Brisbane scheme. The
offer was gushingly accepted.
To-day, in the company of his Ex. and
Lady Macgregor, she patronised the races.
Lady Mac. wore another amethyst frock, and
the Vision was appropriately draped in a
white ninon affair, and a shady white hat
massed with stock. Whittingham, M.L.C.,
the two pretty Taylor girls, and Theo Met-
calfe and his father were included in the
party. To-night his Excellency will-eat with
the Hibernian Association, in honor of St.
Patrick, who knew more about snakes than
Australia s Morrissey. Anyhow, that’s what
a man who has had plenty of snakes told me.
Agricultural Minister White, Under-Secre-
tary Scriven, and Chief Justice Cooper on
f I'iday helped Sir William and Lady Mac. to
look over Gatton College. I haven’t heard
ot anybody being hanged or reduced in salary,
so I suppose the party was satisfied with what
it saw.
Handsome Miss Marion Darvall will change
her name to Wilkinson on the 27th of this
month. Ihree teas in her honor are threat-
ened for next week, with Mrs. David Hamil-
ton, Mrs. Cameron and Miss McSwaine as
hostesses. Another wedding is that of Miss
lemple Curtis, whose Irish eyes and brogue
captured the heart of Mr. Ernest Trafford. a
master at tne local Boys’ Grammar School.
Miss Lrost is hostessing a kitchen tea for
the colleen on Tuesday next.

Lillies in Sydney about Easter time. The
1 ercy Rylances and their seven little Aus-
tralians have gone to England.

Traffic-Manager Brown, of the Northern
section, was in Brisbane this week, to meet
his doctor son. Brown, junior, came over
surgeon on one of the big Maoriland shin
ping co.’s boats, and will move for London
per the Roscommon later on.
Fanny Durack put up another world’s
record here on Wednesday night, this one for
the 220-yards splash through fresh water
(It would have been fresher if a hogshead or
two of eau de lavande had been swilled into
it.) In addition to Fanny Durack, Mina
Wylie and little Isma Amor showed them-
selves to be wonderful swimmers. The former
beat Fanny’s record for the 100 yards breast
stroke swim by the fifth of a second ; and the
latter’s rush over the course in the 30-yards
junior championship will call for a lot of
trying before it is beaten.
The N. S. Wales swimmers seemed to enjoy
their week here. On Monday the Manly
Club gave them a day on the bay. On Tues-
day they were driven round the city in drags
and on Thursday the Q.L.A.S.A. took them for
a trip on the Lucinda, and handed over the
prizes, most of which were afterwards packed
in the departing visitors’ baggage.
Lots of Brisbane girls spent last week in
Warwick for the picnic races. Mollie Ferrier,
of Coorparoo, made her debut, and other
pretty Brisbane girls included Alice Morgan,
Gladys Pollard, Alice Flower and Freda
Pagan.
Miss Josephine Lewis, the talented daugh-
ter of Telegraph Lewis, is giving a concert
in the Exhibition Hall some time next month.
Later in the year, with her mother, she will
flit to England and the Continent, and add a
further polish to her already delightful piano
playing.
Mrs. Gilbert Wilson, our own particular
song-bird, is at present in Melbourne.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JAME.

Last week the Jap steamer Shinsei Maru |
loaded a fall cargo of wheat for Tokio, this
being the first time since the Japanese were
ordered to eat wheaten bread instead of
rice, that a whole wheat cargo has been
sent from Australia to Japan. The officially-
advised change from rice flour to wheaten
flour is designed to improve the stature and
stamina of Nippon. However, the wheat
may not be gone for good: Australia will
probably get it back again—some day.

Important Cattle Sale
ON THURSDAY. AND FRIDAY,
24th and 25th APRIL, 1913. 3
NOON FIRST DAY; 10 a.m. SECOND DAY.
At Freeman R. Jackson and Co.’s Yards,
Wanganui.
BUSHY PARK STUD HEREFORDS,
Kai Iwi, Wanganui,
New Zealand.
rrUIIS FINE HERD was founded upon
1 Mr. James Stuckey’s renowned TE
lIANGITUMAU HERD. It has even been
still further improved from time to time by
importations from the best strains of Great
Britain. Its present owner, MR. FRANK
MOORE, can confidently claim that it is un-
rivalled in the Australasian Colonies for
EVENNESS OF TYPE, CONSTITUTION,
EARLY MATURITY and GRAND, RICH
DARK COLOR.
Owing to the expiry of certain Leases, the
whole of these FAMOUS CATTLE are to be
sold bv PUBLIC AUCTION on APRIL 24
and 25, 1913,
ABSOLUTELY WITHOUT RESERVE.
The Sale is a Genuine Clearing One.
The BUSHY PARK HERD includes,
approximately:—
101 COWS now in calf, and which will be
again in calf. From these are expected
about 45 Heifer Calves this season.
29 HEIFERS, two-years-old. They will be
in calf at time of sale.
23 HEIFERS, yearlings.
5 COWS, old, in calf.
These latter are the only OLD cows in the
lot. The foregoing constitute an aggregate
of
203 FEMALE STUD HEREFORD
CATTLE.
To this total must be added a
LARGE NUMBER OF STUD BULLS
of different ages. The majority of these will
be rising two years at time of sale.
Then there will also be sold 45 Bull Calves,
These MAGNIFICENT CATTLE are
practically all eligible for entry in the ENG-
LISH HERD BOOK, and they will be in
snlendid condition when sold.
The Sale of the BUSHY PARK STUD
IIE REFORDS is a unique opportunity for
New Zealand breeders to improve their
Herds, or for Australian and South Ameri-
can buyers to introduce a new strain of
blood.
In the conduct of the Sale there will act
in conjunction:—
MURRAY. ROBERTS AND CO., LTD.,
FREEMAN R. JACKSON AND CO..
WILLIAMS AND KETTLE, LTD.,
Auctioneers.

ARE YOU
DEAF?
If you are deaf, or going deaf, you will be glad to
hear of a simple and scientific device which restores
hearing, no matter how old you are. No mystery or
secrecy about it; we describe it fully, and explain
just how it acts. You use spectacles as sight magni-
fiers, and in the same way you use this sound magni-
fier to hear better. Unlike spectacles, the Wilson
Common-sense Ear-Drums are invisible and comfort-
able. No one can tell you are wearing them. They
rest the ear nerves by taking the strain off them—the
strain of trying to hear indistinct sounds. What a
relief to have done with that tiring strain. Being
made of the softest Para rubber they cannot be felt,
even when the head rests on a pillow. For the same
reason, a blow on the side of the head will not hurt
the ear. They protect the tender inner parts of the
ear, that are so sensitive, from cold winds, dust, sud-
den and piercing sounds.
So that you may clearly understand how the Sound
Magnifiers restore hearing, we give an illustration of
one as it lies in the ear. You will notice that the
Sound Magnifier is shaped like a trumpet, and so con-
veys even the faintest sounds right to the natural drum.
Being of the softest rubber, it cannot hurt the ear.
It fits the orifice of the ear
with precision and comfort.
The division that runs
across the mouth is to al-
low the removal and inser-
tion of the Ear-Drum, and
to brace it.
The shape of the Wilson
Ear-Drum has been chosen
after years of expert««
the best adapt-
ed to carry the
faintest sound.
Of course, if
you have lost
the h e a r i ng-
nerve com-
pletely, or were
born deaf, we
cannot pretend
to cure you. Only a miracle could
do that, in spite of some of the
absurd claims made to-day. But
of you can hear the faintest sound,
do not despair. You will never
regret trying the Wilson Ear-
Drums. We have letters to show
you from deaf people in Australia
and New Zealand, who have been
cured, and you can be cured, too. You mav wear
the Ear-Drums day and night, and take them out
whenever you wish. Children can wear them (we
have special sizes for children from six to twelve).
Even a child can insert and remove them without the
least danger.
The price is £1 (the fee for a doctor’s single
visit). This includes a pair of drums (they are only
sold in pairs), a fine nickel remover, and also an
inserter. After the first pair is bought you may pur-
chase a single drum at any time for Bs., but the
pair you get at first will last quite two years. Do
not ask for a trial pair. We do not send them out
on trial, and so they are never secondhand. Use the
Order form below, and the Outfit will be sent you
at once, with the fullest instructions. Don’t delay,
but have the comfort of good hearing as soon as you
can.

baldness, itching scalp, etc., know full well the

1 am not an advertising quack, but am a business
man. My hair began to grow thin about the time
1 reached the age of thirty. When I had reached the
age of thirty-five the top of my head was quite bald.
I had considerable pride, and sought for something
to cause renewed growth. I invested in every pre-
paration that I could see or hear of, and here l et rne
say that not only did I try all the English so-called
restorers, but those of Continental Europe. I also
visited specialists. My experimenting cost me many
sovereigns and considerable time —yet the hairs came
not. I had given up the idea of ever growing hair
again. One afternoon, while enjoying a brief busi-

collegiate chemist, whose hair was particularly plen-
tiful. I jokingly remarked that I wished he could
spare some of his hair for my benefit. This lead him
to tell me that he understood the human hair as well
as a good dentist understands teeth. He wrote out a
formula, and suggested that I have a quantity of the
compound prepared. I did this a lew days later. 1
was astonished to see the hair coming forth after
several applications, and within six weeks my head
was covered by a firm, yet soft, black growth of hair.

as when in my school days. Having arranged for
the legal right to do so, I have for some time been
privately selling this marvellous hair-growing com-
pound. I hold over one thousand letters from people
who have actually grown new hair through the
medium of my true hair grower. I do not pretad
to say it is the only hair grower in the world—l am
not interested in decrying others —but I do assert
that it is the most reliable, and in the end by far the
cheapest.
