Business, Bobbery, etc.
The record order for Australian-made goods was re-
ceived lately by H. V-. McKay, of the Sunshine Har-
vester. The order was for 800 Bft. harvesters, 700
7ft., and a lot of 6ft. machines; delivery at the rate
of 50 a week. A bank draft for £230,000 accom-
panied the order, which came from the United States.
This is a triumph for the Australian manufacturer,
for Australian wood, and for Australian workmen con-
jointly. Money talks, and on this occasion it spoke
up like a megaphone; in fact, it fairly bellowed.
“Whaffor”: The display of the resources of the
Northern Territory at the A.N.A. Exhibition in
Melbourne is absolutely the poorest on record. All
that the department can produce is shown on a shelf
3ft. wide and 30ft. long. These products of a great
land consist of a few bundles of sisal hemp, a few
saucers filled with maize, millet and Kaffir corn, a
handful of cotton, a plate of upland rice, another of
swamp rice, some osprey feathers and a few cocoa-
nuts. There is no display of buffalo horns (speci-
mens 12ft. from tip to tip are often secured) or
hides. Buffalo-raising should be a thriving industry.
Wool from the Barkly tableland, where some of the
finest in Australia has been grown, is not amongst the
exhibits. Nor is a single specimen of minerals to be
seen on the board. The Territory will be first devel-
oped through its mineral wealth, yet there is nothing
in the “Court” to expound that minerals exist
there. Here we have an immense region of country
carrying gold, copper, tin, lead, wolfram, iron and
the rarer minerals, and there is not a hint of that
wealth anywhere. The Government’s ideas are so
paltry that it displays a bagful of tropical products
grown in a garden, and some of the poorest looking
wool this scribe has ever seen. Truly, Gilruth, Thomas
and co. are making a deplorable hash of their great
trust. #
“I. R. Wynne”: The ways of the confidence man
are various. At a recent Melbourne, suburban fire
many houses were threatened, and their owners were
making frantic efforts to remove their furniture to
safer places. One of the (alleged) owners, who was
more frantic than most, offered to sell his whole lot,
as it stood, for £4 down. His offer was snapped
by a dealer; but when the gentleman started to re-
move the goods, he met with unexpected trouble —and
the real owner 1 The ability to thus collar opportun-
ity by the forelock commands my admiration ; but a
very mad dealer, who was £4 out of pocket, refused
to see the fine human points in the transaction.
* * * *
“Trotter” : I object to tips of any kind in a demo-
cratic community. It is demoralising for any citizen
to accept a tip, with its concomitant hat-touching
and fawning. At the Melbourne railway station to-
day I had to tip a red-capped luggage porter 6d. to
carry a little luggage a little way. Who could offer
a man 3d.? Yet the work was not a tenth part of
that involved in a threepenny shave. But that is not
the worst feature. Look at the women who travel in
Victoria. Porter with the red cap comes and takes
their luggage. “Thanks, oh thanks.” “Excuse me,
mum, but I don’t git paid. All I have is what pas-
sengers give me.” This may not be un-English, but
it is confoundedly non-Australian —a degrading bit
of departmental “economy.” And grossly unfair,
because the rich man gives more, and, of course, gets
better served. The porter is a public utility, and
like every other should have his fixed wage.
•+**'*
The backbone of the country, who crowds into the
shire councils and sees that his own bit of road gets
attention, has been striking quite a lot of trouble in
the N.S.W. courts lately. One of him, at Stroud,
signed a contract with the council in his brother-in-
law’s name (“apparently without that brother-in-
law’s authority,” Justice Pring added), and of
course demanded payment for the job. Nothing but
unpleasant remarks have dropped on him so far. But
up in the Blue Mountains one of the same kidney
struck worse luck—or a more persistent sort of oppo-
sition councillor. When he was found guilty of
being interested in a timber contract, the P.M.
said coldly that it would cost him £5O.
“W.”; Allow me, of your charity, a couple of
strenuous moments with the quack rupture specialist.
He swarms both in Australia and England, and he
is a heartless swindler and a danger to the public
health to boot. Wherefore I claim that the Law
should get to him without delay. There is a certain
sameness about his tactics. His device is always
claimed to do away with the necessity for a truss.
However, it is invariably a truss itself—usually a
bit of elastic webbing like a cricket belt with a lump
of wood or similar substance to fit over the rupture.
The cost, wholesale, would be under 55., but the
specialist (who always cured himself with the appli-
ance originally) charitably claims something well
up in the pounds for it. Then, as a guarantee of
good faith and an exposition of pure benevolence, he
agrees to take a sum down, which only allows him
about 300 or 400 p.c. on the cost of the contrivance,
the balance to be paid when the patient is cured.
This is called a guarantee, and referred to in glow-
ing terms by the specialist as a no-eure-no-pay trans-
action. If you write to one of the tribe, he first
sends you much literature about his particular brand
of atrocity. If you don’t reply, he intermittently
sends more, each instalment becoming more affection-
ate and impassioned. At last he begins to men-
tion that he is lying awake at night thinking of the
risks you are running. Then he offers out of sheer
human kindness to drop the price about 30 p.c.
Later, he bursts into tears, and offers further reduc-
tions. If you continue adamant, he points sadly to
all the stamps and literature he has wasted, makes
one more wild, haunting appeal, and dies away into
silence, diversified by curses. If you buy his futile
contraption, you are (1) robbed, (2) imperilled in
the important matter of your health. Why should
this noxious trade be suffered to live any longer?
“Vulcan”: The present boom in the burglary line
is responsible for some weird precautions
Sikes. I know of one coih-collector who, after plac-
ing the cash-bag beneath his bed before retiring,
arranges on an adjacent table, for all the world like
the innocent paraphernalia of a final “nip,” a tum-
bler, water and whisky. The originator’s theory is
that the teetotal Sikes is rare, and that a “doc-
tored” whisky in Sikes’s own hand is likely to be far
more lethal than a gun in the quaking hand of his
adversary.
COAST LIGHTS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In a report to the Minister for Customs, Com-
mander Brewis says that various new coast lights
have already been ordered, and construction has been
commenced. The work of replacing the light at
Wilson’s Promontory by a powerful modern light
is in hand. The apparatus has arrived from, Eng-
land.—Press report.
The Text.
A glimpse of tops’ls showing—
A far, faint smudge of black
Over the world’s edge blowing—
The lone ships keep the track!
Outwards with tweeds and leather,
Homeward with hides and wool,
Through fair and ugly weather
They plug for their Father Bull.
The Raw Australian hails them
With things from field and mine;
Sometimes he humbly sails them
Over the heaving brine;
Spinning the wheel, or hauling
Wet ropes, throughout the night
He hears Australia calling
And looks for an English light!
And the Usual Sermon.
Too long have we been steering
By England’s lights afar ;
The Commonwealth is veering
Towards Cohen’s sanded bar!
But when our own lights flashing
Shall guide our people home,
Less imports will come dashing
O’er seas of Freetrade foam!

A SOUND FORMULA.
It is the fashion among certain sections of
the community to sneer at Proprietary Medi-
cines, but in many cases there is consider-
able self-interest behind the sneer. In spite
of this attempt to discredit all Proprietary
Medicines, thousands of people are using, for
instance, Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills, and
get just the results they are looking for, and
no amount of sneering and attempts to dis-
credit them can alter the opinion of people
who know by experience that they are using
a useful and effective medicine and at a frac-
tion of what it would cost to be running to
a doctor or chemist. These pills are based
on a sound formula, and are produced by
men of experience. There is nothing known
about pills that they do not know, there is
no ingredient that is known to be effective for
the liver, for aiding digestion and purifying
the blood, that they do not know. It is
their business to know. They not only
know these ingredients, but they know how
to compound them to secure the highest effi-
cacy. Just as any cake, omelet, or any article
of food can be hopelessly spoiled because the
cook is incompetent, and, even when given
the best material, absolutely fails to make an
eatable article; when another cook, using the
/same materials, accomplishes a result that Is
a delight. So it is in compounding drugs,
only even to a greater degree. The ingredi-
ents used in producing Dr. Morse’s Indian
Pills are of the highest standard, and they
are selected to secure the best results, and
in proportions to doubly secure ibis. Some
people are always sceptical about a proprie-
tary medicine, but does it not stand to reason
that it costs as much for machinery and
labor to produce a poor pill as a good one?
In one case satisfaction is given because the
article is good, and in the other dissatisfac-
tion because it is valueless. Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root Pills are a good pill in every
sense of the word, a fact that Is abundantly
proved by the thousands who continue to
use them year after year as their sole remedy
for all the little ills of life. They are pre-
pared under the best possible conditions, and
there is no better pill made in this or any
other country, for biliousness, constipation,
liver and kidney troubles and female ailments.
The care taken in the production of these
pills is illustrated by the manner in which
they are packed. Instead of being put up in
cheap wooden boxes —that absorb poisonous
and other substances—they are packed in
glass bottles, at a cost of five or six times
that of wooden boxes. The extra cost is
more than made up in the satisfaction of
knowing that the pills reach the public just
as they left the laboratory, unaffected by
moisture or climatic changes, and free from
contamination by contact with any foreign
matter. Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills are a
purely vegetable sugar-coated medicinal pre-
paration carefully compounded by experts, and
are manufactured on the premises under the
direct supervision of the proprietors.
COMSTOCK’S “DEAD SHOT” WORM
PELLETS.
A SAFE, SURE AND RELIABLE REMEDY
I OR THE ERADICATION OF WORMS IN
CHILDREN AND ADULTS.
Worms, by their movements, and the in-
terruption they offer to the contents of the
bowels, generally occasion pain or uneasiness
m the abdomen, sometimes spasmodic, some-
times gnawing or biting, and not infre-
quently of a vague, indescribable character,
and yet very distressing. Sympathetic with
this is a sensation of itching at the anus and
at the nostrils, producing a disposition to
scratch the fundament and pick the nose,
which is characteristic of this affection. The
bowels are often disordered, sometimes con-
stipated or stopped, and sometimes relaxed
with occasional bloody discharges, as in
dysentery. The discharge is sometimes in
shreds or flakes, which are not infrequently
mistaken for fragments of partially-digested
food. It is not uncommon for portions of
undigested food to pass with the discharges.
The appetite is exceedingly variable; in some
cases natural, in others deficient, craving or
depraved; one condition not infrequently al-
ternating with another. The abdomen is
often hard, swollen and tympanetic, the breath
heavy or foetid, the tongue furred, with dis-
agreeable taste, and a copious flow of saliva;
swelling of the upper lip, bleeding of the
nostrils, and disposition to grind the teeth
during sleep are other characteristic symp-
toms. The effects, however, often extend
beyond the alimentary canal, and various
derangements of health are experienced in
consequence either of the direct irritation of
the worms or of the disordered digestion which
they occasion. Among the most common of
these are nervous affections, such as fretful-
ness, irritation of temper, wakefulness, or dis-
position to excessive sleep, disturbed sleep,
sudden starting out of sleep, as if from
fright, dizziness, spasmodic movements of
the eyelids, dilated pupils, perverted vision,
temporary blindness and deafness. General
convulsions are not infrequent in children,
and symptoms strongly resembling “Water
on the Brain” have been ascribed to worms
and have ceased on thei expulsion.
Cases of St. Vitus’ dance, melancholia and
nervous diseases, and even insanity, have been
attributed, with the most apparent reason,
to the same cause. In addition to these affec-
tions may be mentioned obstinate cough,
spasm of the glottis, palpitation, languid cir-
culation, pale and yellowish skin, sunken
eyes, a livid circle about the eyelids, and a
general emaciation. This condition is often
accompanied by fever, and is then called
worm fever. Persons in apparently perfect
health may be afflicted with worms, and the
only symptoms may be periods of irritable-
ness or a constant desire for something to
eat, which may last for months until the
system breaks down, and the worms mani-
fest their presence in some other manner.
Worms have frequently been described by
medical men as “The Children’s Enemy.” Un-
doubtedly there is no greater enemy to
health, happiness and comfort of children
than the presence of worms in the stomach
and intestines; causing an indescribable irri-
tation and gnawing in the bowels, which up-
sets its victim, impairs the health, spoils the
temper, and drives the mother to distraction,
and earns for the little innocent the harsh
and undeserved reputation of being “a cross
child.”
Unfortunately many mothers fail to recog-
nise the fact that their little ones are suffer-
ing intense pain and distress, and are practi-
callv irresponsible for the outbursts of tern-
per, restlessness and peevishness, and until
some more serious symptoms appear nothing
is done to alleviate the distress. The symp-
toms of this disease are somewhat variable,
but generally they are so characteristic that
no mother need have any doubts. The best
proof of worms is worms, and if the general
conduct of the child is such as to give the
slightest suspicion, an examination of the
stools (evacuation from the bowels) will
afford ample proof.
A child who is affected with Tnread Worms
will often appear to the eye to be in an in-
different state of health. It becomes peevish
and irritable in its temper, and complains of
itching of the nose, whilst the same symp-
tom may be very prominent indeed i>
lower part of the bowel. Another point to
which the attention of mothers should be
specially directed is that the child is apt to
start in its sleep, its repose being in this
way of a disturbed nature. Note also that
many children who are in the habit of wetting
their beds do so by reason of the irritation
which these worms produce in the body, the
irritation extending to the bladder, and caus-
ing it involuntarily to part w r ith its contents.
In every heap of decaying matter there is
always to be found a nest of worms. They
breed and multiply in impure matter. And
so it is in the human system. The accumu-
lation of partially digested food in the intes-
tines, which becomes putrid when the bowels
are constipated, induces the presence of
worms. A few doses of Comstock s Dead
Shot” Worm Pellets, followed by a dose or
two of Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills, will
disturb them in their nest and drive them
out of the system.
INCORRECT DIAGNOSIS.
TONIC PRESCRIBED FOR WELL-KNOWN
SYDNEY MAN WHO HAD LOST THIRTY
POUNDS IN WEIGHT.—WORMS THE
CAUSE.
“Some time ago I began losing weight in an
alarming manner,” writes Mr. G. B. Renton,
of 14 Sir John Young’s Crescent, Sydney, “so
much so that I went to see a doctor, who ex-
amined me and came to the conclusion it was
due to overwork and worry. I continued to
lose weight, and also suffer from peculiar in-
ternal pains. I became very anxious at the
continued loss of weight, and thinking I
might be going into a decline went to a
second doctor, who examined me thoroughly
and gave about the same opinion as the for-
mer one had done, and prescribed a tonic.
One night going home in the train I read
one of your advertisements giving symptoms
of people who have worms. The symptoms
given strongly coincided with my own, and I
determined to test the question. I accord-
ingly procured a packet of your Comstock s
‘Dead Shot’ Worm Pellets, and took eight
tablets, and in the morning four Dr. Morse's
Indian Root Pills, and to my intense satisfac-
tion and relief passed two very large stom-
ach worms. I took them in a glass and
showed them to the doctor who had last ex-
amined me, and w r ho had prescribed the
tonic, and it is needless to say he was sur-
prised at the result. Altogether I lost thirty
pounds in two months, due to these worms,
and you may believe me I was thoroughly
alarmed, thinking I might have consumption.
I regained all this lost weight immediately I
rid my system of the worms, and am in splen-
did health at present. These are the simple
facts, which speak for themselves, and I shall
be only too pleased to confirm them to any
person who doubts their truth. You have
my permission to use this for publication.”
AFTER ONE DOSE.
WORMS EXPELLED FROM THREE
CHILDREN.
Mrs. WiUmott, .of Cooma, N.S.W.. writes: —
“Hearing about the wonderful success of your
Worm Pellets, I decided to give them a trial.
I gave my three children, Roy, Phyllis and
Horace, a dose each, as they had all the symp-
toms of worms. The pellets were taken at 6
o’clock in the morning, and at 10 o’clock
Roy had passed a stomach worm about 12
inches in length, and the other children passed
quite a number of thread worms. Since tak-
ing the Pellets the children seem greatly re-
lieved, and appear to be better in health. I
shall always recommend them to my friends,
and you may use this testimony for publica-
tion in any way you please.’’

DUE TO WORMS. MARVELLOUS RESULTS
OBTAINED FROM COMSTOCKS “DEAD
SHOT” WORM PELLETS.
“My little girl, now four and a half years
of age, was suffering for over four years,”
writes Mrs. F. Weiss, of 545 Crown Street.
Surry Hills, Sydney. “She appeared to have
consumption of the bowels, and I took her to
the hospital, where they prescribed powders,
which I gave her, but did not seem to do any
good. I then discovered she had Worm
Fever, and tried various medicines, which,
although they caused her to pass some worms,
only afforded temporary relief, One day I
received one of your books describing what
Comstock’s ‘Dead Shot’ Worm Pellets had
done for other children, and I straightway
procured a packet. After the first dose, the
result was simply marvellous, the child pass-
ing hundreds of worms. This was four
months ago. I have since continued the
treatment, and feel sure she is now practi-
cally rid of them. Whereas previously my
daughter was very thin and that weak she
could not walk, she is now full of life and
energy, and for this I have to thank Com-
stock's ‘Dead Shot’ Worm Pellets. You have
my permission to use this for advertising
purposes, and I shall be only too pleased to
recommend them at every opportunity.”

GREATLY DISTRESSED BY THREAD
WORMS.

thread worms; in fact, one of the children.
Laura, was literally alive with them. I had
recourse to many remedies without success,
The distressing symptoms of restlessness at
night and peevishness for a long period,
worried me considerably, until I came to try
Comstock’s ‘Dead Shot’ Worm Pellets, and
I found them most effective. My children
have now recovered and enjoy their sleep and
make flesh, whereas they both previously
grew thin. I now recommend your medicine
as a sure remedy, having derived immense
benefit from its use.”

Do You Want to Increase Your Business?
IVtS:
Do you realise how easy it would be to make
“personal” calls on thousands of people who would
probably like to do business with you if they only knew
you and the good goods you could furnish them? You
can do this by sending to each one a personal type-
written letter—Oh, no, you won’t have to write them
on your typewriter, that would take too long—but you
can typewrite them on the Multigraph.
WHAT THE MULTIGRAPH IS.
It is a multiple typewriter, used to produce sales let-
ters in quantities The typewritten effect is secured by.
the use of typewriter type, a typewriter ribbon, and a
rubber platen, which produces a letter actually type-
written, not an imitation, mind you. They carry that
“personal” element, which invites a careful reading.,
and which is totally lacking in the ordinary “circular”
letter. Does this not suggest to you how you could
place a knowledge of your goods right into the hands
and minds of thousands of customers at little more than
the cost of the postage?
OTHER USES OF THE. MULTIGRAPH.
In addition to increasing your business by producing personal sales letters,
you can use it for producing reports, bulletins, notices, price lists, and other
office duplicating of highest quality, as clear as the original.
AN OFFICE PRINTING MACHINE.
It will also print your office forms. Real printing, using electrotypes and
printers’ ink. You can print your own stationery, office or factory forms, adver-
tising matter, etc., right in your own office at your own convenience, with your own
help, and save money on it, too. We shall be glad to tell you more about the
Multigraph in relation to your office and business, with no obligation on your own
part, and you cannot buy a Multigraph unless you need it.

ANOTHER splendid cure by Bile
Beans is that of Miss R. Baker, of
Wilton Street, off Ipswich Road, Woolloon-
gabba, Brisbane, who says: “From childhood I was
subject to biliousness. Dizziness caused my head to feel as if it were
turning round, and it would ache something fearful. Terrific pains
across my temples nearly drove me distracted. These attacks would
continue for a while, and I would become fearfully sick and retch most
violently, the sensations of which were horrible. Utter prostration
followed, after which I would sleep for hours. My skin turned sallow
and the whites of my eyes yellow. During the many years I suffered I

La Belle Dame sans Whisky.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
f | HE reddest crayfish ever caught
.1 When seethed in boiling brine.
The brightest lustre Bacchus taught
To flame in oldest wine
Was never redder than the lips
She pressed so close to mine.
Let me explain. The moon was young.
The night was crisp and clear,
Warm wisps of wind were lightly flung
Across the atmosphere ;
I wore beside my beating heart
Some quantity of beer.
I saw her walking on the sea
As Peter tried to do.
Her hair, that floated wild and free,
Was either green or blue,
But exquisitely beautiful
With moonlight streaming through.
Her sandals shone like burnished gold,
But all her toes were pink
Her robe was like some dream an old
White god had dared to think,
As tender, clear and nebulous
As draughts the moonbeams drink.
She wore no wings but her white wrist
Was soft, and warm, and round.
Her circling armlets ringing kist
And made a pleasant sound,
And one grey string of flashing pearls
About her neck was bound.
With some full note of siren song
She drew me to the shore,
The beer-pulse at my heart was strong,
As I have said before,
And all the surf its whiteness spread
Along the sanded floor.
I said : “O Princess of the Moon,
Or Goddess of the Night,
The wide, free world is ours to spoon.
And I should find delight
Freely as young Endymion,
Though some might call me tight.”
We sat upon my overcoat
There on the sandv beach,
With only sea and sky to note
The thing the classics teach,
That if you catch a goddess young
She really is a peach.
The tide came crawling up the sand.
I know not how it came,
For soft as velvet was her hand.
Her lips a wild, sweet flame —
I really do not think the girl
Was specially to blame.
The large, green wave above me curled.
The white foam round me flew,
A ton of sea about me hurled
Somewhat obscured my view.
And I was wetter than a bed
Of tulips in the dew.
The maid was gone, the ocean wide
Was everywhere about.
With all the breath I had inside
I raised a fearful shout,
And some large person waded in
And kindly pulled me out.
The reddest crawfish ever caught,
As seen through ruby wine
When Bacchus at the Dago’s sought
To make one mad hour shine.
Was never redder than the lips
She pressed so close to mine.
Let me explain. The moon was brave,
The night was full of cheer.
The girl wflio came across the wave
Was all sorts of a dear.
But I object when people call
My whole adventure beer.

CUKSE O' MOSES.
By Rail and Semaphore.
HOW MR. JONES WENT NORTH
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
fMHE North Island of Maoriland was
| booming, and the settlement of the
country and the opening up of fresh
lines brought about an exodus of young rail-
way officers from the southern sections.
Jones was one of the first to receive notice
of transfer, and he was booked for the
Rutaniwha section. Nobody knew much
about the Rutaniwha district from a rail-
way point of view, but it was an historic
region—it had been attacked by Maoris in
the long ago, and real battles had been
fought there, and dotted over it were graves
of men who had been killed in the conquest.
Moreover, it was the North, and on the bor-
ders of the Enchanted Land where hot
springs spouted, and mud geysers bubbled,
and it was renowned for the frequency ot its
earthquakes. ~
“Anyhow r , there ought to be some excite-
ment there occasionally—this hole only
livens up once in every three, years at elec-
tion time. An earthquake is better than
stagnation,” said Jones when he read the
notice. ,
It w T as not till the time, came to depart
that Jones got even the slightest suspicion
that he was not the most popular man on
the Section. The discovery pained him
deeply. He was astounded at the ingrati-
tude of the staff generally. From the moment
he had received the notice of transfer Jones
had pictured himself departing in a blaze ot
glory, carrying a nice gold-mounted walking
cane, or some other souvenir,, suitably in-
scribed. to show' the new r friends on the
Rutaniw’lia how he had been a father to
them all down at Romahapa.
But the last days ebbed away, and there
was no hint of even a farewell dinner, trie
fellows who owed him money avoided him,
and the fellow's who had paid up all they had
borrowed were beastly distant, tor the lact
that the Labor Saver w'as a man of . afflu-
ence outside his salary made, every junior
about the place appeal to him the third
week of the month. There were two or
three fellows in the service w’ho made a
good thing by loaning money for the last
week of the pay period at 10 P er cel jb
Jones only charged five, and donated the
profits, less bad debts, to the departmental
fund for the relief of the widows and
orphans of the service. '
reason for the cold shade of in-
difference. in which he found himself was
his crankiness. He was of a combative dis-
position, and went out of his way to get
into rows, and the number he found was
astonishing. The Most Perfect Gentleman
on . Maoriland Railways said good-bye
officially the last day Jones was on duty,
which was three days before the morning of
his departure, and summed up the general
estimate of the Labor Saver pretty accu-
rately when he said :
“Well, good-bye, Mr. Jones. I hope you
will get on all right. I’m sure you will
admit that I am paying you the highest
compliment possible when I say that you
are as clever as a railway officer as you are
cranky as a man.”
When the last day dawned, and there was
still no word of any banquet or presenta-
tion, Jones determined that if they wouldn’t
give him a farewell he would present them
with one, and in the morning everyone in
the Passengers, Goods and Traffic Offices
of Romahapa received an invitation to a
farewell “blow-out” at the Imperial Hotel.
He issued these invitations in bitterness of
heart, and his intention was to give them a
first-class evening, and then fill their mouths
with ashes by means of a farewell speech
that would make their whiskers curl with
rage and shame, after which he would
ostentatiously hand each man who owed
him money a clear discharge of the lia-
bility, and cast him off as if he were a
viper.
When the staff arrived they found that
Jones, as usual, had done the thing pro-
perly. The room was draped with streamers
of red, white and green, the colors of the
railway signal flags; and over the door of
the banquet room a semaphore of white
daisies and red poppies showed “clear line”
to the tables.
When the staff saw the splendid room and
still more splendid tables with a forest of
gold tops gleaming amid flowers and silver,
its collective conscience began to smite it
hard. It felt that things were the wrong
way up, and the deeper it got into the
champagne, and other things, the meaner
it felt.
And all the while Jones smiled grimly as
he felt the little bundle of receipts pressing
into his chest.
They are a strenuous race, the Maori-
landers, and when they lay themselves out
to honor a man by eating and drinking at
his expense—well, they do honor him. It
was a great “blow out.” At 2 a.m. the
Inspector of Police, who had called in to
see what the row was about, was under the
table asleep, and Jones persuaded the Juno-
esque barmaid to sing a song that even
Wagpole, of the Goods, declared he would
never forget. She sang it in a wild boom-
ing contralto that went echoing down the
moonlit street, and amid the thunder of ap-
plause Jones seized her by the waist and
swung her up on to the table, and won her
heart right there by bis ability to do it. He
was the first man who had made her feel
she was made to be caressed. Then he
danced a Highland schottische with her
over the ruins of the oyster patties. Driver
MacGregor tootled “the pipes,” Beauty Bir-
chell clanged the barbaric piano, and the
Inspector of Police awoke and yelled “Arrah
na pogue,” whatever that might mean.
Jones balanced himself on the neck of the
heaving barmaid, and commenced his fare-
well speech, and the noise died down and
the staff smoked and sipped in silence.
“God bless you, chappies; every one of
you. I’m too drunk—not with liquor,
gentlemen, but grief at parting from such
rattling good fellows —to make a proper
speech.” (Here he felt the receipts against
his chest, and wondered vaguely what they
were. Remarks and murmurs from the
staff of “Good old Jones!” and “Bully for
you, Labor Saver!”) “I feel, chappies,
that I cannot rise to the occasion, but my
last words to you are, stick together; buck
one another up through thick and thin, be
loyal, and you’ve got the beastly public by
the neck.” (Here he kissed the barmaid,
who responded.) “I’ve been a father to
you all, chappies; and I hope you won’t
forget all I have taught you.” (Derisive
laughter from the S.M. and others.) “Ash-
bourne told me yesterday, or the day before,
or some other time, that I was the crankiest
man he’d ever known; but if he’d only
known it, it was for his own good, just to
keep him in the way he should go.” (Here
he put the other arm round the barmaid’s
neck and pulled her head on to his shoul-
der.) “There was something that I in-
tended to say to all you fellows to-night,
but it has slipped my memory, and I’m sure
I won’t remember it till I’m just going to
sleep—so you’ll have to do without it.. I
suppose there are a lot of you here to-night
I shall not see again. Some of us are sure
to be killed off before I come back to Roma-
hapa. and some of us will be in gaol, but
not many. I hope. Keep your fingers out
of the till; leave the women and horses
alone, and vou won’t be tempted to dip into
the till.” (Just here he kissed the barmaid
again, and she clung to him.) “Money is
a curse, chappies, and wine’s, a mocker, but
work well done and a girl thoroughly
hugged endureth for ever. I remember the
night I slugged Bryden on the breakaway—
I was terribly worried that we’d be in
Heaven before I could get his other eye
blackened. I don’t know what the devil I m
talking about, but good luck to you all.
Pass up the whisky.”
It took the crowd about another ten
minutes to make sure that all the bottles
were empty and no cigars had been left in
the boxes, and then they filed off hilariously.
j jater — a while later —Jones and the bar-
maid took a tearful farewell of each other,
and promised to store each other s images
in their hearts for ever.
Next morning Jones woke with a thirst,
and remembered that he had got side-tracked
overnight. After carefully destroying the re-
ceipts, he had breakfast, went to the bank,
had a few farewell drinks with various
friends, and then realised that he had only
17 minutes left to catch the express. He
flung his coat over his arm (Ins luggage had
gone down long before) and wondered if he
had time to take a final farewell of Juno at
the Imperial—somehow he felt attracted to
that girl. Then he decided that he hadn’t
time, "and he started for the station, calling

KEEP YOUR KIDNEYS WELL, AND
THEY WILL KEEP YOU WELL”
There is not a drop of pure blood in the body until
it has passed through the kidneys.
For the kidneys are the blood-filters. From birth
to death the blood is constantly flowing through them
to be filtered. The heart pumps the blood, but the
KIDNEYS purify it. They strain out the uric acid
poison and waste water, and dispose of it through the
urinary system.
Every twenty-four hours the kidneys remove
about 500 grains of urinous poison and three pints of
water from the blood. But when they are weakened
by a cold, overwork, or excess of any kind, they begin
to lose their power of separating the urine from the
blood.
Then the poisonous waste they leave in the blood
begins to make you feel heavy, dull and drowsy; you
suffer from headache, sick headache, dizziness, or
rheumatic twinges. The water left in the system
collects under the skin and causes the swellings of
dropsy. There are urinary disorders and gravel.
Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills have a quick, sooth-
ing, tonic, and strengthening effect upon weak
kidneys, gently leading them back to health and
activity.

ip at the traffic office to take his official fare-
well of the District Traffic Manager, Then
he walked across, and climbed on board the
express as she was moving off.
Once inside, he wrapped up three half-
fcrowns to drop out at the Bay of Dogs for
young Hervey, who had been unintentionally
Omitted from the invited, so that he might
have a private blow-out on his own. At the
Bay, Plug held the white flag with the dig-
nity of Kaiser Bill, and young Hervey was
sitting on the railing trying to dislodge, with
a long pole, something which seemed to be
caught in the piles on which the station
stood. Jones flung the cash at Hervey with
a view to making him look round to wave a
farewell to. But it flew high, and it caught
Hervey under the ear, and, glancing off, fell
into the bay. Moreover, it gave Hervey such
a fright that he fell off the railing into the
putrid waters beneath, and the last Jones
saw of the bay was the figure of Plug hurry-
ing around with a rope. He learned later
by wire that Hervey was rescued before the
water poisoned him.
Thenceforward nothing exciting happened.
He had to amuse himself with books and
papers after another couple of hours, for
they were passing over sections that knew
not the Labor Saver, and who would have
regarded him as only slightly better than the
beastly public—the odious proletariat which
was always trying to reduce the salaries.
At 9.45 that night the Labor Saver found
himself walking across the rails at Port
Lynette, to where the s.s. Stormbird lay.
The flaring arc lamps shed a heliotrope-tinted
light, and once in the crowd he caught a
glimpse of a figure that reminded him of
Juno at the Imperial, and he fell to thinking
it was perhaps fortunate that he was on
his way to Rutaniwha; there were possibili-
ties of trouble about Juno. His blood went
faster as he thought of the Highland schot-
tischg of the night before.
On board the Stormbird he found that all
the cabins were occupied, but the steward
made a shake-down for him at the end of
the cabin, just over the kick of the screw.
As soon as they were clear of the Heads he
turned in, and being still tired from the
effects of the night before and the long jour-
ney, went to sleep in spite of the kick of the
screw.
He was aroused by a crash, a succession
of bumps and a shock which hurled him
nnt, of his berth on to the cabin floor.
. “What the ’s wrong, old man?” he
inquired from a reeling steward, who started
to haul lifebelts down from a rack.
“We’re on a flaming reef, and by the list
she’s got I reckon we’ll be under the water
in a quarter of an hour. Get into your
clothes and put this on before the electric
light goes out. Hurry up!”
Then he gathered up the lifebelts and ran
swiftly down the line of sleeping cabins,
from which the occupants were beginning to
peer forth and shout “waiter” and “steward”
in anguished tones.
On the deck was the trampling of many
feet. _ The foghorn burst out harsh and ear-
splitting, and the steamer gave a terrific
lurch and remained in a canting position.
I here was a roar of escaping steam, a wave
of fear seemed to sweep through the passen-
gers as the stewards came trooping along
with lanterns.
“To the deck, everybody. Take your life-
belts, Captain orders everybody to go on
deck at once. Put on your lifebelts, gentle-
men.”
The electric light trembled down, glim-
mered, and went out. The passengers went
swarming up the companion-way, and as he
passed the door of the ladies’ cabin a tall
figure in a macintosh stepped out and
grabbed Jones’s arm.
“Good Lord, what are you doing here?”
gasped the Labor Saver, as he recognised the
face and form of Juno of the Imperial, with
whom he had danced the schottische 24 hours
before.
Then he dragged her up the stairs.
“By Jove, I told those chaps last night
some of us ’d be dead before long. Strikes
me I’ve as good a chance of being the first
as anyone. What are you doing here?”
They reached the deck. Bengal lights
flared up and showed a raging ocean and
wild white horses leaping over the front part
of the steamer. The wind howled through
the rigging, and the hoi'ror of the position
froze the barmaid into silence, and she clung
to the Labor Saver’s arm in genuine womanly
terror. All the forward boats had been
smashed by the hundreds of tons of water
that had been poured over them, and they
were hoisting out the rest. Stewardesses
were fastening lifebelts on to women and
children, and telling them with trembling
lips not to be frightened. The sailors cursed
and swore as they ran the boats clear and
placed water and food on board. And ever
the ship listed over, the wind howled through
the rigging and the screech of the foghorn
drowned the voices. Staring through the
gloom and sea spume Jones could make out
dimly the ragged outline of the horrible Kai-
kouras.
The screech of the foghorn died down and
ceased, the last steam was exhausted. The
boats had been filled with women and chil-
dren, and had disappeared in the night.
“If they come back in time we may have
a chance yet, old girl. There are not more
than half a dozen women left, so you must
be included.”
“I won’t go without you. I loved you the
moment you swung me up on to that table
as though I was a little girl, and now I have
you I won’t part with you, no matter what
comes.”
“Well, I’m darned !” gasped the astounded
Jones.
“Yes, when you went away last night I
knew you liked me, and I lay and tossed and
wondered if ever I would meet another man
like you. It wasn’t worth while losing you
for the sake of a little conventionality. So
I just packed up and caught the express—
I was afraid to discover myself in case you’d
send me back. You know, dear, I’ve always
hated myself for being so big and heavy. I
would love to be like other women, not too
big and not too little; just tall enough to
be kissed comfortably and light enough to
be lifted around. Heavens! how easily you
swung me on to that table last night; it was
lovely.” And she clung to his arm in the
ecstasy of the recollection.
Away to the south, through the mist and
darkness, a light flared up. and an answer-
ing foghorn sounded faintly through the.,
gale. The sailors toiled at the building of a"
raft large enough to accommodate all who
were left, and lashed the women and children
to it, in case the ship fell from beneath them
suddenly. An hour went by and there was
no sign of the boats, and then out of the
darkness, on the top of a mountainous wave,
loomed the red funnel of a 3000-ton steamer,
which signalled that it would stand by till
morning. It dared not approach the reef any
closer in such a sea. Somehow, the presence
of that other steamer in the distance com-
forted them somewhat. Jones found a shel-
tered nook under the funnel, and having cut
Juno loose from the raft, carried her in his
arms to the sheltered spot, and sat there
with her head against his shoulder, swearing
lie would never send her away.
The wind howled through the rigging, tons
of water came pouring over the decks and
ran round them, the shattered steamer gave
a groan and a stagger occasionally; but
Juno took no heed, and half drowsed with
her arm around the neck of the only man
who had ever tossed and carried her about
as though she were small and light, as she
would have loved to be.
Then the dawn struggled through a
ragged sky; the maniacal sea went down,
and the distant steamer felt her way cauti-
ously nearer and took them all off in her
boats, and filled them with beef tea and
brandy, and landed them at Wellington in
the afternoon. The boats had reached the
shore and found their doom there, being
pounded to. fragments between the breakers
and the shingle beach.
As soon as lie got into a berth. Jones
started to think hard. It was alb very well
to promise to keep Juno with him on the
deck of a sinking steamer; it was a differ-
ent matter now they had both got another
indefinite lease of life. Yet he wanted to
keep his promise. She might be a barmaid
and she might not be a classical scholar, but
she was a handsome girl, could sing like an
organ, and she was very much in love with
him; and Jones liked being the adored party.
He was perplexed. There’d be a fearful
row, and divorce proceedings for a certainty.
He was half sorry they hadn’t got away in
the boats; the problem would have been
solved for him then.
He flung himself out of the berth, and got
into his dry clothes. Then he went down to
the bar and had a peg. As he stepped back
into the saloon, wiping his mouth, she came
towards him timidly, and put her hands on
his arm.
He looked at her critically. A frightened
look came into her face. The topaz tinted
eyes grew absolutely scared. He noticed
that her red-brown hair was half-wavy, and
the way it clustered down over her ears
pleased him. After all, her face was refined
enough. The long straight nose terminated
with very delicately curved nostrils; and
what the devil more did any man want.
“I believe you’ve been thinking bad
thoughts—you’ve been contemplating send-
ing me away. But you won’t, will you, dear ?”
“I ought to. I think I told you I am a
married man?”
“Yes, worse luck; but you also told me
you don’t live together; so that doesn't
matter. I’ve thought it all out.”
“Now, look here, Juno, just you consider
where this sort of thing will land you. I’m
an awfully bad lot; I am reallv
and all that sort of thing, vm
drink like a fish. Why, if I w as a

no good to curse
When things have kind o’ jumped the
rails. They might have, been
much worse.
There's silver tailings in the clouds—or su
I’ve heard it said ;
And. it there’s not, you're still alive when
other chaps are dead.
When you’re defunct, my gloomy friend
they’ll dump you in a hole;
And never more you’ll kiss Jeanette, or pass
around the bowl,.
They’ll plunk the sods across your chest'
six feet they’ll pack them high;
And you’ll lie frozen in the dark while wi>
go marching by—
Beneath the sun-warmed sky by day, the
star-splashed sky by night,
To greet To-morrow on its way, the seasons
in (heir flight;
The buds of Spring, the thrill of girls, the
joys that never pall—
Thank God it isn't me that’s with the Dead
who miss them all.
The rain sweeps up the lamp-lit street; the
homing < ars go through—
With ankles flashing ’neath their skirts there
pass the girls who’re true
Or false. It matters not at all as long as r
they are girls—■
The falsest of them still have lips, the cold-
est trembling curls.
The night lights fade before the dawn, and
one more toast to go ;
(Oh, soft blue of tol lacco smoke above the
old pipe’s glow) :
“The men we know, the girls we love,
Life’s honey in Time's hive,
A brain to plan, an arm to work, and
The fact that we're alive.
N.S.W.

THE DORR THICKENER
The only approved mechanical device which will continuously decant
a liquid in a clear state from finely divided material, such as slime or
fine sand, held by the liquid in suspension, and will deliver at the
' same time the thickened material as fast as it is formed, either
continuously or intermittently as desired.
ADVANTAGES OF DORR THICKENER.
Small cost of operation and installation—larger capacity and higher
extraction—reduced head room and economy of water.
